Jerusalem Talmud Sukkah
תלמוד ירושלמי סוכה
The Jerusalem Talmud, translation and commentary by Heinrich W. Guggenheimer. Berlin, De Gruyter, 1999-2015
https://www.nli.org.il/he/books/NNL_ALEPH001901012/NLI

Jerusalem Talmud Sukkah



Chapter 1



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A <i>sukkah</i> which is higher than twenty cubits is disqualified; Rebbi Jehudah qualifies it<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Since the essence of a <i>sukkah</i> is its roofing, סְכָךְ, which has to be temporary and made from vegetable material, giving little protection from the rain, the roofing always should be visible to any person inside the <i>sukkah</i>.</i>. It is disqualified if it is not ten hand-breadths high<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Since nobody could dwell inside.</i>, or does not have three walls<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Even though Isaias (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Isaiah 1:8" href="/Isaiah.1.8"><i>Is.</i> 1:8</a>) gives the name of <i>sukkah</i> to a watchman’s shelter from the sun, a roof over 4 poles without any walls, this does not apply to the holiday hut which must be a dwelling place.</i>, or where the sunshine is larger than the shade.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “An alley which is higher than twenty,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">This text was copied at the beginning of <i>Eruvin</i> Chapter 1, explained there in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sukkah 1:1:4-7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sukkah.1.1.4-7">Notes 31–57</a>. A dead-end alley can be turned into the private domain of its dwellers for the rules of the Sabbath by turning its entrance into a symbolic gate. For the cross-beam which designates the gate the same measurements as for a <i>sukkah</i> are required for the rabbis and R. Jehudah.</i> Rebbi Yose said it without attribution, Rebbi Aḥa in the name of Rav: The rabbis inferred it from the door of the Sanctuary, but Rebbi Jehudah inferred it from the door of the Hall. An argument from the door of the Hall is sufficient only up to forty cubits, as we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Middot 3:4" href="/Mishnah_Middot.3.4">Mishnah <i>Middot</i>3:4</a>.</i>: “The door of the Hall was forty cubits high and twenty cubits wide.” Rebbi Ḥiyya stated, even forty or fifty cubits; Bar Qappara stated, even a hundred cubits. Rebbi Abin said, Rebbi Jehudah follows his opinion and the rabbis follow their opinion, as we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 9:1:2-5:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.9.1.2-5.3">Mishnah <i>Eruvin</i>9:5</a>.</i>, “and similarly one carries under panelled bridges on the Sabbath, the words of Rebbi Jehudah, but the Sages forbid it.” As you are saying there, one considers the roof as if it came down and closed, here also one considers the cross-beam<sup class="footnote-marker">167</sup><i class="footnote">Greek μέλαθρον, τό, a substantial load-bearing beam, not simply a thin plank. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:1:26" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.1.26"><i>Eruvin</i> Chapter 1, Note 41</a>.</i> as if it came down and closed. This is the opinion of Rebbi Jehudah in the case of a <i>sukkah</i>, this is his opinion in the case of an alley. Also the opinion of the rabbis in the case of a <i>sukkah</i> is their opinion in the case of an alley. But they are not comparable. There are items qualified for a <i>sukkah</i> which are disqualified for an alley and those qualified for an alley but disqualified for a <i>sukkah</i>. Double pointed poles<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Greek δίκρανον, τό.</i> are qualified for a <i>sukkah</i> but disqualified for an alley, and it was stated thus: If he brought four double pointed stakes and thatched over them it is qualified as a <i>sukkah</i> but disqualified for an alley. That is, if they are higher by more than three {hand-breadths} than the walls of the alley. But if they are not higher by more than three {hand-breadths} than the walls of the alley it is qualified. If they are not, but if they are four [hand-breadths] wide they are qualified even if arbitrarily high. Walls may be qualified for a <i>sukkah</i> but disqualified for an alley, and it was stated thus<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sukkah (Lieberman) 1:13" href="/Tosefta_Sukkah_(Lieberman).1.13">Tosephta <i>Sukkah</i>1:13</a>.</i>: “Two regular and the third even one hand-breadth is qualified.” Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Two of four hand-breadths each and the third even one hand-breadth is qualified. But for an alley only if it is closed in its four directions. Wider than ten cubits is qualified for a <i>sukkah</i> but disqualified for an alley. “If he drew a vine, or squash, or ivy, and used it as roofing it is disqualified.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sukkah 1:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sukkah.1.4.1">Mishnah <i>Sukkah</i>1:4</a>.</i>” That means, up to an area of two <i>bet se’ah</i>. But more than an area of two <i>bet se’ah</i> it is a partition made for agriculture where one may transport only four cubits. If its sunshine is more than its shade it is disqualified as <i>sukkah</i> but qualified for an alley. A roofed <i>sukkah</i> is disqualified, a roofed alley is qualified. Rebbi Immi in the name of Rebbi Hoshaia: Not only roofed, but if he put there a cross beam four hand-breadths wide, it makes the alley permitted.
Now is a house which is very high not obligated to have a parapet, or to have a <i>mezuzah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Since a <i>sukkah</i> is a temporary house, one has to understand why the rules for a <i>sukkah</i> should be different from those for a house; in particular why a <i>sukkah</i> can be called by this name only up to a height of 20 cubits (11m). Naturally, a <i>sukkah</i> needs no parapet (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 22:8" href="/Deuteronomy.22.8"><i>Deut</i>. 22:8</a>) since nobody can walk on its roof, and it needs no <i>mezuzah</i>(<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 6:9" href="/Deuteronomy.6.9"><i>Deut</i>. 6:9</a>) since it is used only for 7 days and the obligation of <i>mezuzah</i> starts only with 30 days’ use.</i>? But a house is roofed, a <i>sukkah</i> is not roofed<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">A house must be a permanent shelter which also protects against winter rains; this does not apply to the thin thatching covering a <i>sukkah</i>.</i>. We find that thatching is like roofing, as we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sukkah 1:10:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sukkah.1.10.1">Mishnah 1:10</a>. Everywhere an empty space up to 3 hand-breadths width is disregarded (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:8:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.8.3"><i>Eruvin</i> Chapter 1, Note 248</a>). This also applies to houses.</i>, “if the thatching was three hand-breadths distant from the walls it is disqualified.” But a house is completely enclosed, a <i>sukkah</i> is not completely enclosed. If a porch<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ’εξέδρα ‘η, a covered walkway. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:1:8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.1.8"><i>Eruvin</i> Chapter 1, Note 88</a>.</i> was totally open to the public domain, do not Rebbi Ila [in the name of]<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Corrector’s addition. A comparison with the text in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:1:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.1.9"><i>Eruvin</i>(Chapter 1, Note 92</a>) shows that it is unnecessary, and could have been replaced by a comma.</i> Rav and Rebbi Joḥanan both say, one may carry in its entirety<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">While this is a well-defined space, it has no walls. In order to treat it by the rules of an alley one has to hold that the beams which form the frame for the roofing (which may be either a complete roof or simply lattice work) define virtual walls around the walkway. For the rules of the Sabbath, one should compare the porch to an enclosed alley, whose entry beam also cannot be higher than 20 cubits. The rule is waved for the porch.</i>? What about it? Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: The Torah said, <i>in sukkot you shall dwell</i><sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:42" href="/Leviticus.23.42"><i>Lev.</i> 23:42</a>.</i>. Up to twenty cubits a person sits in the shade of the thatching; from twenty cubits and higher he sits in the shade of the walls. Rebbi Jonah said, that is, if you are saying, if it<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">The thatching. For him a low thatching in very high walls creates a qualified <i>sukkah</i>.</i> is put higher than twenty cubits on the walls. But if it was put lower than twenty cubits inside the walls it is qualified. Rebbi Yose said to him, in your opinion, since you are saying that it is depending on the walls, it should state that “a <i>sukkah</i> which is higher than twenty cubits on the walls is disqualified.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">R. Jonah’s explanation is rejected; the thatching must be on top of the walls.</i>” Rebbi Abba in the name of Rav, if it only holds a person’s head, most of his body, and his table<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">A minimal <i>sukkah</i> has space for one person. Since they were taking dinner lying on couches, there must be room inside for that part ot the couch on which his head and body rest; the legs may be outside. In addition there must be a small table for the food. For any <i>sukkah</i> larger than this, the restriction in height is not valid. Accepted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 2b" href="/Sukkah.2b">Babli, 2b</a>.</i>. But if it holds more it is qualified. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Joshia, only if its walls do not reach up all the way, but if its walls reach up all the way it is qualified. But does not a <i>baraita</i> disagree<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sukkah (Lieberman) 1:1" href="/Tosefta_Sukkah_(Lieberman).1.1">Tosephta 1:1</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 2b" href="/Sukkah.2b">Babli 2b</a>.</i>? “Rebbi Jehudah said, it happened that the <i>sukkah</i> of Queen Helena<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Of Adiabene, who moved to Jerusalem as a widow.</i> in Lydda was higher than twenty cubits and the Sages were coming and going there and nobody was saying a word. They said to him, because she was a woman, and a woman is not obligated<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">As a positive commandment tied to a specified time it does not apply to women, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.7.1">Mishnah <i>Qiddušin</i>1:7</a>.</i>. He said to them, is that a proof? Did she not have seven learned sons<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">It is spelled out in the Tosephta that they were sitting with their mother in her <i>sukkah</i>.</i>?” Could you say that the <i>sukkah</i> of Queen Helena only held a person’s head, most of his body, and his table? But it must be that the walls do not reach up all the way. What Rebbi Josia said is reasonable and the <i>baraita</i> does not disagree, since it is the way of rich people to make their walls airy so that cool air may enter<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">Therefore it is accepted practice that the height restriction does not apply to a <i>sukkah</i> which is not minimal and whose thatching rests on the walls.</i>.
Rav Hoshaia asked: If he brought a plank<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">A solid wooden plank. While it is of vegetable origin, it cannot be used for thatching if it is too wide since it is impermeable by both light and rain. The pillar is standing in the middle of the <i>sukkah</i>. One may either sit under the protruding plank or on top of it.</i> and put it protruding on top of a pillar. It is obvious that if he measures from the plank there are twenty cubits, if he measures from the ground it is not twenty cubits<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">But more than that.</i>. How do you treat this? As disqualified space, as disqualified thatching? If you are treating it as disqualified space, it disqualifies by three hand-breadths<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">If it cannot be used for thatching, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sukkah 1:1:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sukkah.1.1.3">Note 13</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sukkah 1:11:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sukkah.1.11.2">Halakhah 10</a>.</i>. If you are treating it as disqualified thatching, it only disqualifies by four cubits<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">If the roof is made of solid material, only in the center there is a skylight which can be covered by thatching; this may be a qualified <i>sukkah</i> if the solid material does not extend 4 cubits from the walls. The solid roof then simply is considered a horizontal part of the vertical walls.</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun in the name of Ḥizqiah: Disqualified thatching only disqualifies by four cubits, since it serves only to permit the <i>sukkah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 4a" href="/Sukkah.4a">Babli 4a</a>.</i>. Rebbi Miasha said, I am wondering what was Rav Hoshaia’s problem? Why does he not infer from the statement of Rebbi Abba bar Mamal, since we stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sukkah 1:10:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sukkah.1.10.1">Mishnah 1:10</a>.</i>: “If one makes the wall hanging from top to bottom, if it is more than three hand-breadths higher than the ground it is disqualified<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">Up to three hand-breadths of empty space are disregarded both on top (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sukkah 1:1:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sukkah.1.1.3">Note 13</a>) or on the bottom of a wall. The wall is considered as if standing on the ground.</i>;” and Rebbi Abba bar Mamal said, if he does not sit and eats in the shade of the walls, but if he was sitting and eating in the shade of the walls it is qualified<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">If he sits on the ground in such a <i>sukkah</i>, it is as if he sits in the open. But if he lies on a couch or sits on a chair higher that 3 hand-breadths, he is surrounded by walls and is under a thatched roof, fulfilling the commandment to sit in the <i>sukkah</i>. Similarly, in the case of Rav Hoshaia, one should say that if he sits on the ground, the <i>sukkah</i> is disqualified, but not if he sits on the plank.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, that of Rebbi Abba bar Mamal is not an inference since Rebbi Abba bar Mamal learned it from another Mishnah, as we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 8:8:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.8.8.1"><i>Eruvin</i> Mishnah 8:9</a>.</i>: “From a balcony above the sea one may not draw water on the Sabbath unless one made a partition ten hand-breadths high either above or below<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">The house is on a lakeshore, the balcony is built over the lake, and there is a hole in the balcony through which a pail may be lowered to draw water. While obviously water is always moving and it cannot be asserted that water drops found under the balcony on the Sabbath were there at sundown, and the water could have come from outside the house’s Sabbath domain, since the restriction is purely rabbinic it is enough that under the balcony one make a symbolic wall whose extension would enclose the water.</i>.” And Rebbi Ze`ira said, Rav Jehudah in the name of Rav: Only if the partition is lowered into the water the full length of a pail<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">A quote from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 8:6:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.8.6.2-4"><i>Eruvin</i> Chapter 8 (Notes 73–80</a>). Since water is always moving, it is impossible to know which molecules will be where at a given time. The answer is that this is irrelevant; at the time the pail is lowered it will draw water only from water on the house’s side of the partition lowered into the lake.</i>. But one cannot compare it. The sea is <i>karmelit</i>, neither private nor public domain<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">A technical term defined in this sentence. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shabbat 1:1:24" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shabbat.1.1.24"><i>Šabbat</i>1, Note 73</a>.</i>. But here the Torah said, <i>in sukkot you shall dwell</i><sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:42" href="/Leviticus.23.42"><i>Lev.</i> 23:42</a>.</i>. From the floor of the <i>sukkah</i> you measure twenty cubits<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">In all cases. R. Abba bar Mamal’s statement is disproved.</i>.
If it was (higher) [lower]<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">The text in parentheses is the scribe’s, the one in brackets the corrector’s. The numeral in the masculine means that the reference is to a <i>sukkah</i> 10 hand-breadths high, and therefore one has to accept the scribe’s text and reject the corrector’s.</i> than ten, and he hung there decorations which could be used for thatching; these reduce it to disqualify. Otherwise they reduce it but qualify<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">If the decorations could serve as thatching, the <i>sukkah</i> does not have the minimum height and is disqualified. Otherwise they are disregarded and the <i>sukkah</i> is qualified.</i>. If it was higher than twenty cubits and he hung there decorations which could be used for thatching they reduce it to (disqualify) [qualify]. Otherwise they reduce it to qualify [disqualify]<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">By the same argument as in the previous Note, here one has to accept the corrector’s text, provided that decorations which can count as thatching are hanging down below 20 cubits. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 4a" href="/Sukkah.4a">Babli 4a</a>.</i>. Rebbi Shammai said, what they said about above they said about below. <sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">This text is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 7:1:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.7.1.2"><i>Eruvin</i> Chapter 7 (Note 24</a>). There it is noted that a ditch 10 hand-breadths deep or a wall 10 hand-breadths high separate domains for the rules of the Sabbath. If the ditch is filled with material that is easily blown away, it remains a separator; if it is filled with pebbles it disappears as Sabbath boundary. Similarly, if walls of a <i>sukkah</i> end higher than 3 hand-breadths from the ground, the <i>sukkah</i> cannot be qualified by filling the distance with straw, provender, or grasses; it is qualified if the hole is filled with dust and pebbles. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 4a" href="/Sukkah.4a">Babli 4a</a>.</i> Straw and provender do not diminish in it; dust and pebbles do diminish in it. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, if grasses grew in it, they would not diminish it.
<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shabbat 1:1:19" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shabbat.1.1.19"><i>Šabbat</i> Chapter 1, Notes 118</a> ff. While here the problem is that a roof less than 10 hand-breadths from the ground does not have the status of a cover, for the rules of the Sabbath public domain reaches only from the ground to a height of 10 hand-breadths, while private domain extends to an indefinite height. The parallel discussion in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 5a" href="/Sukkah.5a">Babli is <i>Sukkah</i>5a</a>. Since the entire discussion is Amoraic, one must conclude that for earlier times the limitation of the public domain to 10 hand-breadths from the ground and the minimal height required for a <i>sukkah</i> was tradition not subject to verification from biblical sources.</i> From where that higher than ten [hand-breadths] is a different domain? Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: <i>There I shall make My Appearance to you, and I shall speak to you from above the cover</i><sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 25:6" href="/Exodus.25.6"><i>Ex.</i> 25:6</a>.</i>. And it is written, <i>you have seen that from Heaven I spoke to you</i><sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 20:22" href="/Exodus.20.22"><i>Ex.</i> 20:22</a>.</i>. Since speech mentioned there is from another domain, also speech mentioned here is from another domain. But is the Ark not nine hand-breadths<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">1.5 cubits (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 25:10" href="/Exodus.25.10"><i>Ex.</i> 25:10</a>).</i>? In the House of Rebbi Yannai they said, the cover was one hand-breadth<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 5b" href="/Sukkah.5b">Babli 5b</a>, this is questioned but in the end accepted. The biblical data do not fit the assertion. The cover was square of surface area 3.75(cubit)<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup> (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 25:17" href="/Exodus.25.17"><i>Ex.</i> 25:17</a>). The hand-breadth and the cubit are not well defined.. If we take a small hand-breadth of 8.5 cm, cubit 51 cm, then a cover 2 cubits long, 1.5 cubits wide, and one hand-breadth height would have a volume of 108(hand-breadths)<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup> = 66 (dm)<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup>. Since the cover was of pure gold, of specific weight about 19, the total weight would have been 1260 kg. But the total weight of gold contributed for the Tabernacle (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 38:24" href="/Exodus.38.24"><i>Ex.</i> 38:24</a>) was 29.25 talents or, on a basis of 12.5 g for the sheqel, about 1097 kg, not to speak of the fact that a weight of 1.26 tons would have crushed the wooden ark. All these data were well within the knowledge of antiquity. One has to conclude that they imagined the cover to be hollow.</i>. Rebbi Ze`ira asked, from where that the cover was one hand-breadth? Rebbi Ḥananiah bar Samuel stated, for all the vessels in the Sanctuary the Torah gave the measure of length and width, and gave the measure of its height. Except for the cover where the Torah gave the measure of length and width, but did not give the measure of its height. Therefore we shall learn it from the smallest vessel in the Sanctuary, <i>you shall make for it a frame of one hand-breadth all around</i><sup>49</sup><i class="footnote">This cryptic statement is explained in the Babli (in the name of Rav Aḥa bar Jacob’s teacher Rav Huna) as referring to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 16:2" href="/Leviticus.16.2"><i>Lev.</i> 16:2</a> where Aaron is warned not to appear <i>before the face of the cover</i> except on the day of Atonement. This implies that the cover was not simply a sheet lying over the Ark but had a face, a vertical dimension which is not negligible.</i>. Since there it was one hand-breadth, so also here one hand-breadth. But maybe it refers only to: <i>make a golden wreath as its frame all around</i><sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">It is impossible to say that in general a new domain starts at 10 hand-breadths since it is commonly accepted that a private domain remains private to an indeterminate height. The Ark was standing in the private domain of the Sanctuary; a reference to it seems to be irrelevant.</i>? Since there it is a minimal size, so here also a minimal size. What about it? Rav Aḥa bar Jacob said, <i>face</i>. There is no <i>face</i> less than a hand-breadth<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">In the desert, the Ark was travelling in front of the people (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 10:33" href="/Numbers.10.33"><i>Num</i>. 10:33</a>) while the Sanctuary was carried in the middle (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 10:17" href="/Numbers.10.17">v. 17</a>). At the resting place, the Ark automatically assumed a West-East direction so that the Sanctuary could be placed around it and the tribes camped correctly in the four directions of the compass (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 2" href="/Numbers.2"><i>Num.</i>2</a>). The Ark had functions outside the Sanctuary; the reference is legitimate.</i>.
Rebbi Yose asked, if there were a very high chest standing in a house, would it not be permitted to move anything from in it into the house or from the house into it<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">In Mishnaic times, the building cubit was a standard 6, the vessel cubit 5 hand-breadths. If the Ark was built to vessel standards, it cannot be used to define domains. Whether the vessel standard was used in the Temple is a matter of controversy in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 98a" href="/Menachot.98a">Babli, <i>Menaḥot</i>98a</a>.</i>? But we are dealing with the case when it was indicating to them the geographic directions<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 31:12-18" href="/Exodus.31.12-18"><i>Ex.</i> 31:12–18</a> and 35:1–3 the observation of the Sabbath is emphasized in the instructions for the building of the Tabernacle. One concludes that the work forbidden on the Sabbath is exactly work needed in building or transporting the Tabernacle. The wagons mentioned here are the 6 wagons donated by the tribal chiefs for the transport of the Sanctuary (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 7:3" href="/Numbers.7.3"><i>Num</i>. 7:3</a>).</i>. This is understandable for him who said, a cubit of six {hand-breadths}<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">Nine hand-breadths of the Ark and one of the cover.</i>. But for him who said, a cubit of five hand-breadths? Would the Ark not be seven and one half hand-breadths? Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa said, the House of Rebbi Yannai and Rebbi Simeon ben Yoṣadaq. One learns it from the Ark, but the other learns it from the wagons. [We do not know who learned it from the Ark and who learned in from the wagons.]<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">Corrector’s addition from <i>Šabbat</i>.</i> It is reasonable that the House of Rebbi Yannai learned it from the Ark since the House of Rebbi Yannai said, [the ark was nine and]<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">Corrector’s addition.</i> the cover was one hand-breadth. Rebbi Simeon ben Yoṣadaq learns if from the Wagons. Rebbi Ze`ira asked, from where that the Wagons were ten {hand-breadths} high<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">There are no biblical indications about the height of the wagons’ platforms above ground.</i>? Rebbi Yose said, and even if you said that they were ten high, did not Rebbi Neḥemiah state that the Freight Wagons were like a cupola<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">Greek καμάρα, Latin <i>camara, -ae, f</i>. (also <i>camera</i>) “arched or vaulted roof or ceiling, distinct from an outer roof”.<br>The wagons are called עֶגְלֹ֥ת צָב֙. Now צָב means “turtle”; from this comes the usual translation “covered wagon”, using the image of the domed back of the turtle. The expression really is a technical term “freight-wagon”, Accadic <i>ṣubbum, ṣūbum</i>.</i>? If there was a hole in the public domain ten deep and four wide, would it no be forbidden to move anything from in it into the public domain or from the public domain into it<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">This is a side remark. A hole in the public domain 10 hand-breadths deep and 4 by 4 wide creates a new domain for the rules of the Sabbath and is considered a private domain from which nothing may be transported to the public domain. This cannot be derived by comparison either to the Ark or to the wagons.</i>? But when they were transferring the planks from one to the other they were straight<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">Whether the wagons were covered with a convex covering or not is irrelevant since at the moment of loading the planks and the gobelins of the Sanctuary they were uncovered and certainly had flat loading areas. For the meaning of “straight” for תְּרוּטוֹת cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Middot 2:5" href="/Mishnah_Middot.2.5">Mishnah <i>Middot</i>2:5</a>.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">The paragraph is copied from <i>Ma`serot</i>3:8 (Notes 126–129, <b>ע</b>). It is a general rule that agricultural produce is subject to tithe at the latest when it is brought into a house for use or storage. R. Jehudah stated that a <i>sukkah</i> qualifies as a house for the rules of tithes while the other authorities disagree.</i> It was stated: Rebbi says, four cubits even though there are not four walls<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">For him a minimal <i>sukkah</i> must have a thatched roof of at least 4-by-4 cubits surface area but may have less than 4 walls.</i>. Rebbi Simeon says, four walls even though there are not four cubits. Rebbi Jehudah says, four walls and four cubits<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph explains why R. Jehudah holds that a <i>sukkah</i> counts as a house for the rules of tithes; for him a <i>sukkah</i> must be a complete house since one is required to dwell in it. Rebbi’s standpoint is quoted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 3b" href="/Sukkah.3b">Babli 3b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 10a" href="/Yoma.10a"><i>Yoma</i>10a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sukkah (Lieberman) 2:2" href="/Tosefta_Sukkah_(Lieberman).2.2">Tosephta 2:2</a>.</i>. Similarly, Rebbi Jehudah requires it to have a <i>mezuzah</i>[even though it has neither four cubits nor four walls.]<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">Corrector’s addtion, to be deleted.</i> It is reasonable that Rebbi Jehudah agrees with these rabbis<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">He does not agree but he requires everything any other authority requires.</i>, but these rabbis will not agree with Rebbi Jehudah, for even if it has four cubits and four walls it is not required to have a <i>mezuzah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">Since it must be temporary.</i> and it does not create <i>ṭevel</i><sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ṭevel</i> is the state of agricultural produce from which heave and/or tithes are required but which were not yet taken.</i> for tithes.
Rebbi Simeon says four, but the rabbis say three<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">Since it was established that according to the majority opinion a <i>sukkah</i> does not have to be a complete dwelling, the problem remains to find biblical support for a decision about the number of walls the <i>sukkah</i> must have.</i>. What is the rabbis’ reason? סֻכּוֹת סֻכּוֹת סֻכּוֹת, these are three<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:42" href="/Leviticus.23.42"><i>Lev.</i> 23:42</a>,43 the word is used three times.</i>. One for the top, and two for the bottom, and the third from their words<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">Since the verses define the obligation to live in huts, the first mention is needed to establish the fact that the essence of a <i>sukkah</i> is its roofing, סְכָךְ, which has to be temporary and thatched from vegetable material. Since then only two occurrences of the word are remaining, in this opinion only two complete walls are required by biblical standards; the third wall is rabbinic or conventional usage.</i>. What is Rebbi Simeon’s reason? סוּכּוֹת סֻכּוֹת סֻכּוֹת <i>plene</i><sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">In fact, in v. 42 one reads twice סֻכֹּת, in v. 43 once סֻכּוֹת. The first two follow the spelling standards of Moses’s time, the second one that of Ezra who copied the Torah from Hebrew into Aramaic letters (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 21b" href="/Sanhedrin.21b">Babli <i>Sanhedrin</i>21b</a>, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 7:2:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.7.2.3"><i>Sotah</i>7:3 Note 57</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Megillah 1:9:2-20" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Megillah.1.9.2-20"><i>Megillah</i>1:11</a>). It is presumed that Ezra incorporated here an oral tradition from earlier times. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 6b" href="/Sukkah.6b">Babli 6b</a>.</i>, these are four. One for the top, and three for the bottom, and the fourth from their words. Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Ada said, two Amoraim. One explained the rabbis’ reason, the other explained Rebbi Simeon’s reason. What is the rabbis’ reason? <i>A sukkah shall be for shade during daytime against dryness</i><sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Isaiah 4:6" href="/Isaiah.4.6"><i>Is.</i> 4:6</a>. A verse from prophets or hagiographs never can prove a commandment but is valid to verify common usage. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 6b" href="/Sukkah.6b">Babli 6b</a></i>, that is one for the top. [סוּכּוֹת סוּכּוֹת [סוּכּוֹת, these are three. What is Rebbi Simeon’s reason? <i>A sukkah shall be for shade during daytime against dryness</i>, that is one for the top. סוּכּוֹת סֻכּוֹת סֻכּוֹת <i>plene</i>, these are four. The rabbis of Caesarea understand the reason of Rebbi Simeon and the rabbis’ reason from this verse: <i>a sukkah shall be for shade during daytime against dryness</i>, that is one. <i>For shelter and hiding</i>, that is two. <i>From stream and rain</i>. The rabbis consider <i>from stream and rain</i> as one, Rebbi Simeon considers <i>from stream and rain</i> as two<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">In all three derivations, the requirement of 3 or 4 walls is “rabbinic”, i. e., an old common usage.</i>.
And it was stated thus<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sukkah (Lieberman) 1:13" href="/Tosefta_Sukkah_(Lieberman).1.13">Tosephta <i>Sukkah</i>1:13</a>.</i>: “Two regular and the third even one hand-breadth is qualified.” Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Two of four hand-breadths each and the third even one hand-breadth is qualified. Rav Hoshaia asked, what if the one hand-breadth one is put in the middle<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">Normally one would expect that the two large walls form a right angle Γ with the third (rabbinic) rudimentary wall parallel to one of the larger ones. He asks whether the two large ones may be parallel and the third at right angles to them: ׀ _ ׀. Since up to three hand-breadths open space are disregarded, the distance between the two parallel walls can be up to 7 hand-breadths, qualified according to everybody. Therefore the answer has to be positive.</i>? He came back and asked, if one of the four hand-breadths ones was put in the middle<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">That the shape is ׀ _׀. In this case there are three walls of 4 cubits each but the missing part is larger than 3 hand-breadths.</i>, how is it? Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa: Such a case came before Rebbi Yasa and he qualified it. And it was stated thus<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sukkah (Lieberman) 1:8" href="/Tosefta_Sukkah_(Lieberman).1.8">Tosephta 1:8</a>. Since later the rule is compared to those for <i>eruvin</i> and <i>kilaim</i>, one has to assume that on each side the total length of the open space is less than the sum of the diameters of the columns (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:8:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.8.3"><i>Eruvin</i> Chapter 1, Note 246</a>.)</i>: “If a courtyard is enclosed by pillars, the pillars are like walls.” Does not a <i>baraita</i> disagree? If they are made according to the rules they are qualified, not according to the rules disqualified. They wanted to say, in the middle<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">That “not according to the rules” means the small (rabbinic) wall in the middle.</i>. Rebbi Samuel bar Rav Isaac said, if these are not opposite those<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">A configuration like<sup class="footnote-marker">׀</sup> _ <sub>׀</sub> is disqualified.</i>. Rav said, that one of one hand-breadth must be removed from the wall by one hand-breadth<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sukkah 1:1:10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sukkah.1.1.10">Note 74</a>, with the permitted empty spaces the theoretical space covered by the one hand-breadth wall must be 7 hand-breadths.</i>. Samuel said, even if it is arbitrarily close, one considers it as if distant<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">It is enough if one has one hand-breadth and adjacent three empty ones.</i>. Rebbi Abba, Ḥinena bar Shelemiah, Rav Jeremiah in the name of Rav: even if it is arbitrarily close, one considers it as if distant. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa said, Rebbi Joḥanan and Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish, one follows this one, the other one the other. Cahana and Assi came and asked before Rav following that of Samuel<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">Since the answer is not given, we do not know Rav’s real position.</i>. Rebbi Jehudah bar Pazi in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: Only if it is within three hand-breadths like a lathe<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">In order to be able to carry in a dead-end street (bordered by the walls of houses) with an <i>eruv</i>, the dead-end street must be separated from the public domain by a visible entrance. This can be effected either by making a symbolic door by putting a horizontal beam over the entrance (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sukkah 1:1:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sukkah.1.1.2">Note 6</a>) or creating a symbolic entrance post by putting up a vertical lathe. As door-post it has to be on the wall bordering the street. According to our rules, “on” always means “within three hand-breadths.”</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">The following text is both in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 4:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.4.4"><i>Kilaim</i>4:4 (Notes 51–81</a>) and in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1"><i>Eruvin</i> Chapter 1 (Notes 256–283</a>). Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 11a" href="/Eruvin.11a">Babli <i>Eruvin</i>11a</a>.</i> Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish in the name of Rebbi Yose ben Ḥaninah: If one put in four poles at the four corners of a vineyard and connected them with a bast string on top, this saves as a symbolic fence<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">In Tractates <i>Kilaim</i> and <i>Eruvin</i> the word פֵּיאָה does not mean “corner” as in biblical Hebrew and in Tractate <i>Peah</i> but a wire or string strung as a symbolic fence around a field or a vineyard to separate it from the neighboring one, or around a town to make it a “walled” town for the rules of the Sabbath. The imaginary fence represented by the string “saves” from the prohibition of <i>kilaim</i>.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, the rules of separation walls for the Sabbath are the rules for <i>kilaim</i><sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 11" href="/Eruvin.11">Babli <i>Eruvin</i>11a/b</a>, the statement of R. Simeon ben Laqish in the name of R. Jehudah (not Yose) ben Ḥaninah explicitly excludes the expedient of the bast rope as <i>peah</i> for the Sabbath, but R. Joḥanan excludes it also for <i>kilaim</i>. R. Simeon ben Laqish is reported there to accept for himself the statement attributed here to R Joḥanan, that the rules for Sabbath and <i>kilaim</i> are identical, whereas R. Joḥanan rejects this in general. The position of the Babli is that R. Simeon ben Laqish only reports the opinion of R. Judah ben Ḥaninah without accepting it himself, and that R. Joḥanan prohibits the use of a rope for openings wider than 10 cubits. The Yerushalmi accepts R. Simeon ben Laqish’s statement as describing his own position.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, it happened that Rebbi Joshua ben Qorḥah went to Rebbi Joḥanan ben Nuri at Nagnigar<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">A place in lower Galilee of unknown location.</i>; he showed him a field called <i>Bet Rata</i> whose wall had parts torn down more than ten [cubits] wide. He took wood to insert, forked poles<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Greek δίκρανον, τό.</i> to insert, until he reduced to less than ten. He said, just like that is a dividing wall for the Sabbath. Rebbi Ze`ira said, Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish agrees that on the Sabbath, a symbolic fence does not save more than ten<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">The string represents a gate, not a wall. The maximum size of a gate accepted for the rules of the Sabbath is 10 cubits wide.</i>. Rebbi Ḥaggai said, the Mishnah says so: “One may surround by three ropes one higher than the other.” If you say that the string saves, one rope would be sufficient<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:9:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.9.1">Mishnah <i>Eruvin</i>1:9</a>. A caravan which is preparing to rest for the Sabbath marks a rectangular domain by putting camel loads at four corners and drawing ropes around it. There must be three ropes, distant not more than 3 hand-breadths one from the next, which together constitute a wall of 10 hand-breadths hight. The caravan clearly wants more space for animals and people than 10 by 10 cubits; they need a surrounding wall, not a surrounding gate. If it is possible to symbolize a wall with one rope, why does one need three?</i>.
Rebbi Jonah said: Rebbi Hoshaia asked, what do you say about that symbolic fence, on top or at the sides<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">If four poles are put up at the four sides of the vineyard, does the string have to go from top to top, to create the impression of a gate, or is it strung at half height between the poles, to give the impression of a wall?</i>? If you say on top, so much more on the sides. If you say on the sides, then not on the top. If you say on the top, Rebbi Ḥaggai said it correctly<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">Since <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:9:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.9.1">Mishnah <i>Eruvin</i>1:9</a> does not require the lowest rope to be within three hand-breadths of the ground, it is clear that even if the rows are strung high one needs three ropes rather than just one.</i>. If you say from the sides, Rebbi Ḥaggai did not say anything<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">If the rope must be low, the case of פֵּיאָה is different from that of the Mishnah.</i>. The rabbis of Caesarea in the name of Rebbi Jeremiah: Explain it if they were strung on forked poles<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">If the poles have separate hooks so that each side has a complete arrangement of poles and rope, then even R. Ze`ira will admit that the rope forms a valid enclosure for the Sabbath. In this case, by necessity the rope is only on top. In this, the Yerushalmi parallels the final explanation of the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 11b" href="/Sukkah.11b">Babli 11b</a>, rejecting symbolic walls but accepting symbolic gates.</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Greek δίκρανον, τό.</i>.
Rebbi Ze`ira, Rebbi Eudaimon of Haifa in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: In height even one hundred cubits<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">If a rope is drawn from the top of one pole to the next, there are no height limitations (except, naturally, that it cannot be lower than 10 hand-breadths.)</i>. Rebbi Judan said, that is, for <i>kilaim</i>, but as regards (Peah)<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">For “<i>peah”</i> read with the parallels: “the Sabbath”.</i>, a symbolic fence should not be better than a beam. Rebbi Yose said, it is the same for <i>kilaim</i> and Sabbath. According to Rebbi Yose, what is the difference between a beam and a symbolic fence? A beam saves in one direction<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">Beam or lath are needed only at the entrance to the alley.</i>; a symbolic fence does not save from one side unless it is used to close all four sides;. This follows what Rebbi Ze`ira said in the name of Rav Hamnuna: A symbolic fence does not save unless it seals all four sides<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">The problem is not discussed in the Babli.</i>.
It is forbidden to carry under the four-gated bowers<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">Greek τετράπυλον, τό, “archway entered from four sides.” Bowers erected at the crossing of two paths, with only the posts and the connecting beams built, and the roof formed by plants climbing on the poles. Rebbi Abba bar Mamal considers these beams as cross beams under which one may not carry in a dead-end street on the Sabbath.</i> in vineyards<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">The vineyard in question is not enclosed by a wall, or it is enclosed but too large (larger than 5000 square cubits) to be considered a courtyard for the rules of Sabbath; hence, one may carry under the roof of the bower but not outside.</i> since they form the end of the roof and the end of a roof cannot save as a symbolic fence. Rebbi Phineas said, a case came before Rebbi Jeremiah of four pillars and on them four beams<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ’επιστύλιον, τό, “architrave.”</i>; he permitted to carry under them because of a symbolic fence. Rebbi Abun and Rebbi Abun<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Kilaim</i>“R. Bun and the rabbis.” S. Liebermann points out that in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 11:4:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.11.4.5"><i>Terumot</i>11:4 (Note 88</a>) for the Leiden text “R. Abun asked (verb in plural)” the Rome ms. reads: “R. Abun and R. Abbin asked”. This probably should be read here also, as it is later in this Chapter.</i> asked before Rebbi Ze`ira, does a symbolic fence save in a <i>sukkah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>sukkah</i> must have roofing of plant material. The walls may be made of any material. The question is whether a virtual wall is permitted.</i>? He said to them, a symbolic fence saves in a <i>sukkah</i>. Does the end of the roofing save in a <i>sukkah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">Since the roofing must have some support, the four corner posts of the <i>sukkah</i> must have some connection on which the roofing may rest. Can these connections be considered as <i>peah</i>, following R. Jeremiah in the preceding case?</i>? He said to them, the end of the roofing does not save in a <i>sukkah</i>. What is the difference between them? One is made for the purpose, the other one is not made for the purpose. Rebbi Abbahu said, all these things<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">The rulings of R. Ze`ira. Building a <i>sukkah</i> is a positive commandment but neglecting it is not punishable in court. But desecrating the Sabbath is a capital crime.</i> are for discussion’s sake, but as far as teaching, it is forbidden to teach it. If this is forbidden for the minor commandment of <i>sukkah</i>, so much more for the strict commandment of Sabbath! Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya asked before Rebbi Ze`ira: Is not the Tanna, according to whom a symbolic fence saves, Rebbi Joḥanan ben Nuri? He said to him, that is the Tanna<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">Hence, in regard to the Sabbath this is the opinion of a minority of one, which cannot be operational practice.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 4:2:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.4.2.9"><i>Kilaim</i>4:4 (Notes 82–87</a>) also is found in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:8:3-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.8.3-4"><i>Eruvin</i> Chapter 1 Notes 246–251</a> The <i>baraita</i> is similar to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Kilayim (Lieberman) 4:6" href="/Tosefta_Kilayim_(Lieberman).4.6">Tosephta <i>Kilaim</i>4:6</a> quoted also in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 16a" href="/Eruvin.16a">Babli <i>Eruvin</i>16a</a>. The main difference between Yerushalmi and Babli-Tosephta is that in the latter source, each breach has to be smaller than the surrounding wall whereas in the Yerushalmi only the total length of standing wall has to be larger than the total length of the missing parts.<br>The problem of <i>kilaim</i> addressed here is that grain must be separated from vines by at least 4 cubits. If the farmer wants to sow grain close to his vineyard, he has to fence off the vineyard by a wall. The question is, what is called a wall?</i> It turns out that concerning <i>kilaim</i> everything less than three {hand-breadths} is considered closed<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">As noted before, an opening of less than three hand-breadths in a wall is disregarded; in computing the total length of the torn-down parts, breaches smaller than three hand-breadths in width are disregarded.</i>. From three to four, if what is standing is more than what is torn down it is permitted, but if what is torn down is more than what is standing, it is forbidden. From four {hand-breadths} to ten {cubits}, if what is standing is more than what is torn down, it is permitted; but if what is torn down is more than what is standing it is permitted where it is standing<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">The Babli notes that not only are breaches up to three hand-breadths long disregarded as holes, but also standing pieces of wall up to three hand-breadths wide are disregarded as walls. This is the background of the discussion later in this paragraph.</i>, forbidden where it is torn down.
Concerning the Sabbath, everything less than three {hand-breadths} is considered closed. From three to four, and from four {hand-breadths} to ten {cubits}, it is permitted if what is standing is more than what is torn down, but forbidden if what is torn down is more than what is standing. More than ten, it is forbidden even though what is standing is more than what is torn down. Rebbi Ḥananiah, Rebbi Judah bar Pazi, in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: There is no “from three to four.<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">R. Joḥanan wants to eliminate the middle part of the <i>baraita</i>, the one dealing with breaches of between three and four hand-breadths, in case that the standing walls also are in pieces less than four hand-breadths wide. While a hole wider than three hand-breadths is considered a breach, a wall narrower than four hand-breadths is not a wall. In that case, there are only holes and no wall at all; the entire wall should be considered as non-existent! He requires in this case that any sown field should be at least four cubits distant from the vineyard.</i>” There is a hole of three and no place of four! Rebbi Mana objected: Did we not state<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:1:3-10:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.1.3-10.6">Mishnah <i>Eruvin</i>1:10</a>. The caravan instead of using horizontal ropes may use vertical sticks on condition that the distance between two sticks not be larger than 3 hand-breadths.</i>: “One surrounds with sticks,” does a stick form a place<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">Since the sticks are counted and no stick is even one hand-breadth wide, any piece of wall should be considered a wall. The answer is that as long as no two sticks are three hand-breadths distant from each other, the entire dividing fence is considered one solid wall.</i>? He said to him, do not object to me from a breach less than three hand-breadths, since anything less than three is considered closed! Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun in the name of Rav: In any case, since what is standing is more than what is torn down, it is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">This explicitly rejects the text of Babli/Tosephta, that no single hole may be wider than both adjacent pieces of standing wall; the wall is valid as long as the combined length of standing wall (of at least 4 hand-breadths length) is larger than the combined length of the breaches.</i>.
Excess sticks that exceed the <i>sukkah</i> are considered part of the <i>sukkah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 19a" href="/Sukkah.19a">Babli 19a</a>.</i>. Rebbi Ḥuna in the name of the rabbis there: Only if it is on the entire front<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>sukkah</i> has three regular walls, II; the fourth side forms the entrance door with a rod on top supporting the thatching. Sticks forming the roof stick out over this rod in a uniform way. Sitting under the rods outside the rod is counted as sitting in the <i>sukkah</i>.</i>. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa, Rebbi Yasa, Rebbi Joḥanan in the name of Rebbi Hoshaia: Even at a wall. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa, Rebbi Ḥiyya, Rebbi Joḥanan in the name of Rebbi Hoshaia: Even not at a wall<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">If the <i>sukkah</i> does not have parallel walls of equal length, ׀_<sub>׀</sub> and the sticks complete the rectangle.</i>. In the opinion of Rebbi Yasa it is understandable<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">This is the case discussed earlier, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sukkah 1:1:10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sukkah.1.1.10">Note 74</a>.</i>. In the opinion of Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba, how could you be lenient in this if its sunshine is more than its shade<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">The people sitting outside are not sheltered by any walls and may be exposed to the sun.</i>?
“Or where the sunshine is larger than the shade is disqualified;” therefore half and half is qualified. “A wilted <i>sukkah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">Arabic ד̇בל “wilting”. Probably it does not mean actually wilting but apt to wilt.</i> whose shade is more than its sunshine is qualified.<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sukkah 2:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sukkah.2.3.1">Mishnah 2:3</a>. If it only is qualified if the shade is more than the sunshine, half and half is disqualified.</i>” Here you are saying it is qualified, there you are saying it is disqualified. The rabbis of Caesarea, Rebbi Isaac bar Naḥman in the name of Rebbi Hoshaia: It is different for a tree which usually is spreading<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">If the material used for the thatching is apt to wilt, one has to be restrictive from the start.</i>.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The House of Shammai disqualify an old <i>sukkah</i>, but the House of Hillel qualify it. What is an old <i>sukkah?</i> Any which he made<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">I. e., putting up the roofing.</i> thirty days before the holiday. But if he made it for the holiday it is qualified even from the beginning of the year<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Since the festival of Tabernacles starts on Tishre 15, two weeks after New Year’s day, this “beginning of the year” refers to the preceding year.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “The House of Shammai disqualify an old <i>sukkah</i>, but the House of Hillel qualify it.” It was stated: One has to renew something in it. The colleagues say, one hand-breadth; Rebbi Yose says, anything. He who says anything, but along the entire length. Is it the same with an old <i>mazzah</i>? Is an old <i>mazzah</i> a disagreement between the House of Shammai and the House of Hillel? Rebbi Yose said, it is everybody’s opinion; since he did not make it for Passover it is certain that he was not careful about it<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">An old <i>mazzah</i> is one baked before Passover. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Pesachim (Lieberman) 2:21" href="/Tosefta_Pesachim_(Lieberman).2.21">Tosephta <i>Pesaḥim</i>2:21</a> permits the use of old <i>mazzah</i> if it was baked for Passover use. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 2:4:11" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.2.4.11"><i>Pesaḥim</i>2:4, Note 240</a>.</i>. A <i>sukkah</i> of shepherds<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">Is disqualified in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sukkah (Lieberman) 1:4" href="/Tosefta_Sukkah_(Lieberman).1.4">Tosephta 1:4</a>, but as the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 8b" href="/Sukkah.8b">Babli 8b</a> explains for potter’s <i>sukkah</i>, if the thatching of the roof was made expressly for the holiday, every kind of <i>sukkah</i> is qualified.</i>, a <i>sukkah</i> of potters is qualified. A <i>sukkah</i> of Samaritans, if it is made according to the rules<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">For the thatching of the roof. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 8b" href="/Sukkah.8b">Babli 8b</a>.</i> is qualified, not according to the rules is disqualified.
One<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">The first sentences are also in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 9:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.9.3"><i>Berakhot</i>9:3 (Notes 182–184</a>). There is a similar statement in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Berakhot (Lieberman) 6:9" href="/Tosefta_Berakhot_(Lieberman).6.9">Tosephta <i>Berakhot</i>6:9</a> which is quoted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 46a" href="/Sukkah.46a">Babli <i>Sukkah</i>46a</a>; the note about the benediction when making a <i>sukkah</i> for somebody else is found only here. Hence, the source here is not the Tosephta which also starts with a general declaration that: "A person performing any commandment recites a benediction over it." The practice of the Babli does not follow the discussion in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 46a" href="/Sukkah.46a"><i>Sukkah</i>46a</a> but a statement in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 42b" href="/Menachot.42b"><i>Menaḥot</i>42b</a> that no benediction is due for a preparatory action, such as making the <i>sukkah</i>, but only for the required act itself such as living in the <i>sukkah</i>.</i> who makes a <i>sukkah</i> for himself, says: “He Who sanctified us by His commandments and commanded us to make a <i>sukkah</i>.” For others: “to make a <i>sukkah</i> in that one’s name<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">One has to pronounce the recipient’s name at the place reserved for “that one’s name.”</i>.” If he enters it, he says: “Praise to Him Who sanctified us by His commandments and commanded us to dwell in a <i>sukkah</i>.” After he recited the benediction in the first night of the holiday he does not have to recite it again<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli (<i>loc. cit</i>.), this is the opinion of the Babylonian Samuel, supported by the Tosephta. In the name of the Galilean R. Joḥanan, it is reported that one has to recite the benediction every day. Since R. Johanan is a higher authority than Samuel, Babylonian practice followed what there is declared to be Galilean.</i>. Ḥanin bar Abba in the name of Rav: Does one have to mention the time after nightfall<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">The benediction required on all holidays, “Who has kept us alive, maintained us, and enabled us to reach this festive time.”</i>? Rav Hoshaia said, one has to mention the time. Rebbi Ila said, one has to mention the time. Rebbi Zevida said, one has to mention the time. Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Ada formulated it as a tradition: Rebbi Ze`ira, Rebbi Isaac bar Naḥman, Rebbi Hoshaia, Rebbi Jehudah bar Pazi in the name of Rebbi Ḥama the father of Rebbi Oshaia: After nightfall one has to mention the time.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If one makes his <i>sukkah</i> under a tree it is as if he made it in the house<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">It is disqualified since part of the shade comes from the leaves of the tree which still are connected to the ground and vegetal matter connected to the ground is disqualified (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sukkah 1:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sukkah.1.5.1">Mishnah 5</a>.)</i>. One <i>sukkah</i> on top of the other, the upper one is qualified and the lower one disqualified. Rebbi Jehudah says, if there are no dwellers in the upper one the lower is qualified.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Two thatched roofs one on top of the other, the upper one has more sunshine than shade, the lower one has not more sunshine than shade<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">This is not the case of the Mishnah, since there one must presume that both roofs are qualified with more shade than sun. Here the question is when thatching in layers is considered to constitute one roof.</i>. Together, their shade is more than their sunshine. How much may be between them so that they combine? Two Amoraim, one said, ten, the other one said, four<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">Since the numerals are in the masculine, all measures are hand-breadths.</i>. The one who said ten objected to the one who said four: because of a tent? We find a tent which is one hand-breadth<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Oholot 3:7" href="/Mishnah_Oholot.3.7">Mishnah <i>Ahilut</i>3:7</a>. In matters of impurity of the dead, one cubic hand-breadth is considered a tent. The minimum height of a <i>sukkah</i> is ten hand-breadths. Therefore we find 1 or 10 units as legitimate requirements, but not 4. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 10a" href="/Sukkah.10a">Babli 10a</a>.</i>.
“Rebbi Jehudah says, if there are no dwellers in the upper one the lower is qualified.” What? Really dwellers or even appropriate for dwellers<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">Does R. Jehudah disqualify the lower <i>sukkah</i> only if there are people in the upper one or even if there are none but they could be (i. e., if somehow the upper one has a floor)?</i>? Since an old man stated before Rebbi Ze`ira, “even the lower one is qualified,” there is nobody who says “even” if he does not agree with the preceding. This implies really dwellers<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">Rejected by the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 10a" href="/Sukkah.10a">Babli 10a</a>.</i>.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If he spread a sheet over it because of the sun, or under it because of fallen leaves<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">Nothing which may become impure can be used as material for thatching the roof, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sukkah 1:12:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sukkah.1.12.1">Mishnah 1:12</a>. Therefore a textile sheet spread either on top or below the thatched roof disqualifies.</i>, or if he spread it over the posts of a four-post bed<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">Greek κωνωπεών, τό, Latin <i>conopium</i>, a bed with mosquito curtains. The ceiling of a four-post bed is horizontal; it creates a ceiling below the roof and disqualifies all space below it.</i>, it is disqualified. But one may spread it over single posts<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ’ανάκλιτα “things to lean on”. Poles in the middle of headboard and foot-end of the bed with a rod connecting them. The mosquito net spread over them forms on both sides a slanting roof which is disregarded since for the majority a slanting roof is not qualified for the <i>sukkah</i>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sukkah 1:12:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sukkah.1.12.1">Mishnah 1:12</a>.</i> of the bed.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> It was stated: if he hung there quilts<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">Sheets sewn together from rectangular pieces of cloth.</i> it is qualified. Rebbi Ḥanina said, that you are saying on the sides<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">Decorating the walls, not under the roofing. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 10b" href="/Sukkah.10b">Babli 10b</a>.</i>, but on top it is disqualified.
“Or under it because of fallen leaves.” Rebbi Yose said, they only said, “because of fallen leaves.” Therefore if not because of fallen leaves it is qualified<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">For decoration, not utility. He disagrees with R. Ḥanina. Explained otherwise <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 10a" href="/Sukkah.10a">Babli 10a</a>.</i>.
“But one may spread it over single posts of the bed.” Rebbi Bevai in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Since so he makes space with the extremities of his hands<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">Since when he sleeps in the <i>sukkah</i> he may lift the sheet, or the mosquito net, with his fingers, it is not different from the net hanging down in an incline.</i>.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If he drew over it a vine, or squash, or ivy<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">Greek κισσός, ‘ο.</i>, and used it as roofing, it is disqualified. But if the roofing was more than these<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">If cut vegetal material was more than 50% of the thatching and the remainder still connected to the ground.</i> or he cut them off from their roots<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">Even after the roof is finished.</i> it is qualified. This is the principle: One does not thatch with anything which may become impure or is not grown from the earth. But with anything which may not become impure and is grown from the earth one does thatch.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Abba in the name of Rav: Only if he drew them (on the spot) [for this purpose]. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Ze`ira: He needs to move it. Rebbi Yose said, both of them are for leniency. If he drew them (on the spot) [for this purpose] even if he did not move it; if he moved it even if he did not draw them (on the spot) [for this purpose]<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote">Creepers used for roofing become qualified after being cut from their roots only if either they were put there for this purpose or were moved after cutting.</i>.
Rebbi Abba, Ḥinena bar Shelemia, Rav Jeremiah in the name of Rav: If he used steps of ladders for roofing it is (qualified) [disqualified]<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote">Wooden ladders are implements and as such subject to impurity. Steps are shaped at the ends; while they have no concavity which would make them susceptible to impurity, they are implements not to be used.</i>. Male parts, it is qualified<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote">Wooden parts which are straight rods are impervious to impurity.</i>. Female parts, it is disqualified<sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote">Wooden parts with cavities are subject to impurity.</i>. If he roofed with bundles of flax<sup class="footnote-marker">147</sup><i class="footnote">Bleached flax is subject to impurity as textile.</i>, it is disqualified, with raw flax it is qualified. If he roofed it with ropes, there are Tannaim who state, qualified, and there are Tannaim who state, disqualified. He who said qualified, with bast ropes<sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote">Made from palm bast which is not considered textile.</i>; but he who said disqualified, with linen ropes.
Rebbi Joḥanan said, <i>when you collect from your threshing floor and your wine press</i><sup class="footnote-marker">149</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 16:13" href="/Deuteronomy.16.13"><i>Deut</i>. 16:13</a>.</i>. From the waste of the threshing floor and the wine press you make your thatching<sup class="footnote-marker">150</sup><i class="footnote">The verse in <i>Deut</i>. describes the festival of Tabernacles which, as the name indicates, is characterized by the obligation to dwell in a <i>sukkah</i>. The verse hints at the rule of the Mishnah that the roof of the <i>sukkah</i> has to be made from agricultural waste (which is grown from the earth but as waste is not subject to impurity even if cut and wetted. It is possible that R. Joḥanan reads אספ like Arabic “to have regret” to have waste rather than usable produce.) <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 12a" href="/Sukkah.12a">Babli 12a</a>.</i>. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, <i>and mist rose from the earth</i><sup class="footnote-marker">151</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 2:6" href="/Genesis.2.6"><i>Gen</i>. 2:6</a>. This refers to the standard interpretation of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:43" href="/Leviticus.23.43"><i>Lev.</i> 23:43</a> that the Children of Israel dwelt in huts in the desert, when in reality they dwelt in tents. Therefore the ‘huts” are taken as the Divine cloud cover which shielded the encampment (as spelled out in the Targumim to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:43" href="/Leviticus.23.43"><i>Lev.</i> 23:43</a>), which R. Simeon ben Laqish takes as formed by mist from the earth; thatching has to imitate the cloud cover arising from the earth. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 11b" href="/Sukkah.11b">Babli 11b</a>.</i>. Rebbi Tanḥuma said, each of them follows his own opinion. Rebbi Joḥanan who said that the clouds were above infers it from <i>when you collect</i>. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish who said that the clouds were below infers it from the clouds. Rebbi Abbin said, each of them follows his own opinion. Rebbi Joḥanan compares it to one who sends to another an amphora and its pitcher. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish compares it to one who says to another, bring your box and take wheat for yourself<sup class="footnote-marker">152</sup><i class="footnote">R. Joḥanan also agrees that the roof of the <i>sukkah</i> has to symbolize clouds. He does not agree that clouds are generated by mist from the earth but are sent by God to give rain to the farmers. R. Simeon ben Laqish is presumed to think that clouds are generated by the earth and then filled with rain by God. Both agree that the <i>sukkah</i> is a charm to assure abundant rain in the coming wet season. Detailed argument <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bereishit Rabbah 13:10" href="/Bereishit_Rabbah.13.10"><i>Gen. rabba</i>13(10</a>).</i>.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One does not thatch with bundles of straw, and bundles of wood, and bundles of shoots<sup class="footnote-marker">153</sup><i class="footnote">The soft shoots of vines which are edible, in contrast to wood which is hard and inedible.</i>; but all of them are qualified if untied. All of them are qualified as walls.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Because it looks like storage<sup class="footnote-marker">154</sup><i class="footnote">Tied straw and wood is as impervious to impurity as is untied. The reason one may not use bundles is that this is the way the materials are stored; the prohibition is rabbinic because it makes the roof look like a depository and not like a roof or, as the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 12a" href="/Sukkah.12a">Babli 12a</a> explains, one might use the space as depository and later change one’s mind and make it the roof of a <i>sukkah</i>; then the roof was not made for the holiday and as such is disqualified.</i>.
Rebbi Jacob bar Abbaya, Rav Sheshet in the name of the Great Rebbi Ḥiyya: There is no bundle with less than twenty-five<sup class="footnote-marker">155</sup><i class="footnote">Therefore small bundles may be used as roofing when still tied.</i>. Ḥinena bar Shelemia in the name of Rav: If he cut for roofing it has no handles<sup class="footnote-marker">156</sup><i class="footnote">In general, only edible produce can become impure. But inedible stems to which produce is attached transmit impurity to the produce; the detailed rules are given in Tractate <i>Uqeṣin</i>. It is stated here that if grain is cut as roofing, the straw only has to be more than the grain kernels. No inedible part of the stalk has to be compensated for. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 13b" href="/Sukkah.13b">Babli 13b</a>.</i>. If he cut as roofing and then intended it as food it follows his thought<sup class="footnote-marker">157</sup><i class="footnote">And become disqualified as roofing by his thought.</i>. If he cut for food and then intended it as roofing? Others say, only if the straw is more than the food and the handles<sup class="footnote-marker">158</sup><i class="footnote">The shells and the ends of the stalk also have to be compensated for by straw. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 13b" href="/Sukkah.13b">Babli 13b</a>.</i>. Rav follows the others. Where do we hold? If he cut for roofing but it became prepared and he said, after they will have fulfilled the commandment I shall bring them back to the threshing floor, do they need preparation a second time or are they already prepared<sup class="footnote-marker">159</sup><i class="footnote">In general, produce becomes susceptible to impurity only by contact with fluid desired by or desirable for the owner. This is called “preparation for impurity”, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 2:3:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.2.3.2"><i>Demay</i>2:3, Note 141</a>.<br>The question is not answered. Therefore it is possible that the material is susceptible for impurity, disqualified as roofing, and prepared for impurity as food. No answer is support for the second alternative. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 13b" href="/Sukkah.13b">Babli 13b</a>.</i>?
Rebbi Yasa in the name of Rebbi Ḥama bar Ḥanina: <i>You shall cover the Ark with the cover</i><sup class="footnote-marker">160</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 40:3" href="/Exodus.40.3">Misquote of <i>Ex.</i> 40:3</a>; scribal error. Instead of “cover” it must be “gobelin”. Since the gobelin was the dividing wall between the Ark and the interior of the Tent, the intended meaning is: <i>You shall hide the Ark behind the gobelin</i>.</i>. From here that the wall is called cover. From here that one makes walls with matter susceptible to impurity<sup class="footnote-marker">161</sup><i class="footnote">Since the root סכך is used, it follows that all that is acceptable as roofing a <i>sukkah</i> is acceptable as wall. Since the gobelin is mentioned which is susceptible to impurity, walls may be made from matter susceptible to impurity. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 7b" href="/Sukkah.7b">Babli 7b</a>.</i>.

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One may use planks as roofing, the words of Rebbi Jehudah, but Rebbi Meïr forbids. If he put there a plank four hand-breadths wide it is qualified on condition that he not sleep under it<sup class="footnote-marker">162</sup><i class="footnote">Everybody agrees that a plank 4 hand-breadths wide, while impervious to impurity, is regularly used in roofing permanent housing, should not be used for the <i>sukkah</i>. But if it is put on the sides, along a wall, it is considered extension of the wall and not part of the roof and therefore disqualifies the roof only if 4 cubits wide (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sukkah 1:1:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sukkah.1.1.4">Note 30</a>). The <i>sukkah</i> is qualified but sitting under the plank is not counted as sitting in it.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi<sup class="footnote-marker">163</sup><i class="footnote">As the quote later in the paragraph (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sukkah 1:7:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sukkah.1.7.2">Note 144</a>) and chronology imply, everywhere one has to read Rav Jeremiah (the first generation Babylonian colleague of Rav) instead of Rebbi Jeremiah (the fourth generation head of the Academy of Tiberias.)</i> Jeremiah in the name of Rav: They disagree if it is [four]<sup class="footnote-marker">164</sup><i class="footnote">Corrector’s addition, rectifying a scribal omission. In the argument later the numeral is in the masculine (hand-breadths); it seems necessary to consider the feminine form here as sloppy spelling, not referring to cubits.</i> wide. Rebbi Yasa in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: They disagree if they have been polished for implements<sup class="footnote-marker">165</sup><i class="footnote">Even if they qualify as פְּשׁוּטֵי כְלֵי עֵץ “wooden implements without concavities” which are impervious to impurity. These are excluded by tradition, as they could be seen as finished implements, not by any biblical standard. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 15a" href="/Sukkah.15a">Babli 15a</a>.</i>. But was not Rebbi Jeremiah {saying} in the name of Rav: They disagree if it is four wide? Therefore if they have been polished for implements everybody agrees that it is permitted. Since Rebbi Jeremiah said in the name of Rav, if he used steps of ladders for roofing it is disqualified<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote">Wooden ladders are implements and as such subject to impurity. Steps are shaped at the ends; while they have no concavity which would make them susceptible to impurity, they are implements not to be used.</i>, and they are saying that he ruled according to Rebbi Meïr, this implies that both cases are the same<sup class="footnote-marker">166</sup><i class="footnote">Since Rav stated that planks four {hand-breadths} wide are disqualified but also that non-concave wooden parts turned into parts of implements do disqualify, for him both his and R.
