Joḥanan’s statements are operative.</i>. Since Rebbi Yasa said in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: They disagree if they have been polished for implements, therefore if it is four {hand-breadths} wide everybody agrees that it is forbidden. Now Rebbi Yasa ruled at the big assembly that one may use a cross-beam<sup class="footnote-marker">167</sup><i class="footnote">Greek μέλαθρον, τό, a substantial load-bearing beam, not simply a thin plank. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:1:26" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.1.26"><i>Eruvin</i> Chapter 1, Note 41</a>.</i> for the thatched roof, and they said that he ruled following Rebbi Jehudah, this implies that both cases are the same<sup class="footnote-marker">168</sup><i class="footnote">Since the beam need not be polished, the ruling cannot be based on his statement in the name of R. Joḥanan. He also admits Rav’s description of the description of the disagreement between RR. Jehudah and Meïr.</i>.
Samuel said, this<sup class="footnote-marker">169</sup><i class="footnote">The last statement in the Mishnah, that one may not sleep under a plank four hand-breadths wide. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 15a" href="/Sukkah.15a">Babli 15a</a>.</i> you only are saying lengthwise; but crosswise it is qualified. Rebbi Joḥanan and Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish both are saying, both lengthwise and crosswise are qualified<sup class="footnote-marker">170</sup><i class="footnote">If the plank is on the side along a wall it becomes part of the walls, not of the roofing. But if it lies crosswise it is disqualified roofing. If on both sides there is qualified roofing of more than 4 hand-breadths, the 4 hand-breadths wide plank is disregarded and the entire roofing is qualified. The last sentence of the Mishnah is undisputed.</i>. Rebbi Zeriqan in the name of Rav Hamnuna: Following the words of him who disqualifies here it disqualifies there<sup class="footnote-marker">171</sup><i class="footnote">In his opinion, the last sentence of the Mishnah follows R. Jehudah and is denied by R. Meïr.</i>. Rebbi Abbin and Rebbi Abun asked before Rebbi Ze`ira: Why are Rebbi Joḥanan and Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish both saying? He told them, because disqualified roofing disqualifies only in four cubits<sup class="footnote-marker">172</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 17b" href="/Sukkah.17b">Babli 17b</a> this is Rav’s opinion, disputed by Samuel.</i>. A <i>baraita</i> disagrees with Rav Hamnuna: A <i>sukkah</i> which only contains his head, most of his body, and his table. If he brought a plank and added from it three hand-breadths. If you would say crosswise, this is impossible since Rebbi Zeriqan said in the name of Rav Hamnuna: Following the words of him who disqualifies here it disqualifies there<sup class="footnote-marker">173</sup><i class="footnote">Even if only 3 hand-breadths were used, the plank is four wide and therefore disqualified.</i>. So we have to hold lengthwise, but it is impossible<sup class="footnote-marker">174</sup><i class="footnote">The usual Yerushalmi meaning of איפשר. Since in such a minimal <i>sukkah</i> it is impossible to lie down without part of one’s body being under the plank.</i> not to sleep under it.

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A roof without insulation<sup class="footnote-marker">175</sup><i class="footnote">The roof is formed by wooden logs without mortar or any insulation against the rains from non-vegetal sources.</i>, Rebbi Jehudah says in the name of the House of Hillel either he moves or he lifts one in the middle<sup class="footnote-marker">176</sup><i class="footnote">Either one re-arranges the logs or one lifts the middle one of any three parallel logs and fills the space by qualified thatching.</i>, but the House of Shammai say, he moves and lifts one in the middle. Rebbi Meïr says, he lifts one in the middle and does not need to move.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> But if he moved he does not have to lift one in the middle<sup class="footnote-marker">177</sup><i class="footnote">Following Rebbi Meïr.</i>.

Halakhah 9

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If one is roofing his <i>sukkah</i> with spits<sup class="footnote-marker">178</sup><i class="footnote">Which are metal and therefore disqualified.</i> or with girders from the bed frame<sup class="footnote-marker">179</sup><i class="footnote">Which are susceptible to impurity even if wooden.</i>, if the distance between them is equal to their diameter<sup class="footnote-marker">180</sup><i class="footnote">If the space between the girders is filled with qualified vegetal material, the <i>sukkah</i> is qualified.</i> it is qualified. If somebody hollows out a grain stack for a <i>sukkah</i>, it is no <i>sukkah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">181</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in the Halakhah.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> It was stated, more than their diameter<sup class="footnote-marker">182</sup><i class="footnote">The only way one can be sure that the diameters of the disqualified girders are not larger than the intervals covered by qualified thatching is if the qualified intervals actually are somewhat larger than the measured diameters of the girders. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 15b" href="/Sukkah.15b">Babli 15b</a>.</i>. The colleagues say, because no hand-breadth enters into a hand-breadth<sup class="footnote-marker">183</sup><i class="footnote">Since hand-breadth is not an objectively defined measure, there is no way of making sure that two hand-breadths applied to two different places are absolutely equal.</i>. Rebbi Abba bar Mamal objected, is not for glass vessels one hand-breadth equal to another hand-breadth<sup class="footnote-marker">184</sup><i class="footnote">It is difficult to make sense of this statement. But if it refers to the glass maker, Tosephta <i>Kelim Bava Meṣia`</i> 3:11 states that the glass-maker’s container (καλύβη) is pure if split exactly in the middle. If it were impossible to exactly compare measurements then a statement requiring a 50–50 split never would be applicable.</i>? Rebbi Yose said, there it enters and leaves, here it enters and does not leave<sup class="footnote-marker">185</sup><i class="footnote">It is easy to check equality of length of solid objects that can be superimposed on one another; it is impossible to do so for unrelated distances.</i>.
Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Because it looks like a storage<sup class="footnote-marker">186</sup><i class="footnote">And not like a <i>sukkah</i> made expressly for the holiday. In his opinion the prohibition is purely rabbinic.</i>. Rebbi Ḥiyya stated, <i>you shall make for yourself</i><sup class="footnote-marker">149</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 16:13" href="/Deuteronomy.16.13"><i>Deut</i>. 16:13</a>.</i>, not from what already was made for you<sup class="footnote-marker">187</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>sukkah</i> is invalid by biblical standards.</i>. What is the difference between them? If it is in it<sup class="footnote-marker">188</sup><i class="footnote">There is a hollow in the stack before he starts making a <i>sukkah</i>(<a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 17a" href="/Sukkah.17a">Babli 17a</a>). Carving out the <i>sukkah</i> means making walls. Since the essence of the <i>sukkah</i> is its roofing, being preexisting it is disqualified for R. Ḥiyya. It may be qualified for R. Joḥanan since one does not remove parts in the middle from a stack used as storage.</i>. In Rebbi Ḥiyya’s opinion it is disqualified, in Rebbi Joḥanan’s opinion it is qualified.

Halakhah 10

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If one drops walls down from top to bottom, if they remain higher than three hand-breadths it is disqualified<sup class="footnote-marker">189</sup><i class="footnote">But walls starting less than 3 hand-breadths from the ground are equivalent to walls standing on the ground.</i>. From bottom to top<sup class="footnote-marker">190</sup><i class="footnote">Standing on the ground, even if the roofing is less than 10 hand-breadths from the ground.</i>, if it reaches ten hand-breadths it is qualified. Rebbi Yose said, just as from bottom to top ten hand-breadths so from top to bottom ten hand-breadths<sup class="footnote-marker">191</sup><i class="footnote">For him a wall 10 hand-breadths high always is qualified, irrespective of vertical distances from ground or roof.</i>. If the roofing is three hand-breadths distant from the walls it is disqualified<sup class="footnote-marker">192</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>sukkah</i> is disqualified if along any wall the roofing is not within 3 horizontal hand-breadths.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">193</sup><i class="footnote">A parallel text is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 8:8:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.8.8.5"><i>Eruvin</i>8, Notes 110–117</a>. Since the reference to the rules of <i>Sukkah</i> is formulated there differently from here, the texts in their second parts are different versions, rather than straight copies.</i> Rebbi Joḥanan said, Rebbi Yose said it only for Tabernacles, but in the matter of the Sabbath even Rebbi Yose agrees<sup class="footnote-marker">194</sup><i class="footnote">For the Sabbath and <i>Eruvin</i>, R. Yose will not admit cleavages wider than 3 hand-breadths.</i>. The word of Rebbi Ḥanina implies that it is even for the Sabbath, as Rebbi Ḥanina said, a ruler came to Sepphoris and they suspended quilts<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">Greek κωνωπεών, τό, Latin <i>conopium</i>, a bed with mosquito curtains. The ceiling of a four-post bed is horizontal; it creates a ceiling below the roof and disqualifies all space below it.</i>; Rebbi Ismael ben Rebbi Yose permitted carrying under them following his father’s argument. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun in the name of Rebbi Samuel ben Rav Isaac: Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Ḥanina comes like Rebbi Ḥanania and both disagree with Rebbi Joḥanan’s argument, as Rebbi Joḥanan<sup class="footnote-marker">195</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that one should read: R. Yose ben Ḥanina but both texts have “R. Joḥanan.”</i> said, Rebbi Jehudah, and Rebbi Yose, and Ḥanania bar Aqabia all three said the same. Rebbi Jehudah of the open bridges<sup class="footnote-marker">196</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 9:1:2-5:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.9.1.2-5.3">Mishnah <i>Eruvin</i>9:5</a>, where R. Jehudah defines the space under a bridge as private domain if the underpinning of the bridge structure extends 10 hand-breadths below the roadway.</i>, Rebbi Yose that of tabernacles, Rebbi Ḥanania ben Aqabia as it was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">197</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shabbat 3:3:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shabbat.3.3.7"><i>Šabbat</i>3, Notes 101–103</a>; Babli 87b.</i> Rebbi Ḥanania permitted three things, he permitted a balcony, and seaweed, and bringing bathtowels<sup class="footnote-marker">198</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Shabbat 22:5" href="/Mishnah_Shabbat.22.5">Mishnah <i>Šabbat</i>22:5, Note 21</a>.</i>.

Halakhah 11

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If a house partially collapsed<sup class="footnote-marker">199</sup><i class="footnote">A one story building of which part of the roof collapsed while all of the walls are standing. The only part of a <i>sukkah</i> for which R. Ḥiyya biblically requires new material expressly for the holiday is the roof. Therefore the remaining house may be used as a <i>sukkah</i> if the hole in the middle of the roof is patched over.</i> and he thatched over it, it is disqualified if from the walls to the thatching it is four cubits<sup class="footnote-marker">200</sup><i class="footnote">As was stated earlier, a solid roof adjacent or connected to the walls is considered part of the wall, not of the roof. But this is accepted only up to a distance of 4 cubits from the nearest wall. Empty space is acceptable only up to 3 hand-breadths, disqualified material up to 4 cubits.</i>. The same holds for a courtyard which is surrounded by a walkway<sup class="footnote-marker">201,</sup><i class="footnote">The entire courtyard may be turned into a <i>sukkah</i> using qualified thatching, with the walls of the houses bordering the courtyard forming its walls, on condition that the roof of the walkway be less than 4 cubits wide.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ’εξέδρα ‘η, a covered walkway. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:1:8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.1.8"><i>Eruvin</i> Chapter 1, Note 88</a>.</i>. A large <i>sukkah</i> is disqualified if it<sup class="footnote-marker">202</sup><i class="footnote">The qualified roofing. The makers of the <i>sukkah</i> did not have sufficient material to extend the qualified roof from wall to wall. Then it is much better to fill the remaining space to the walls by disqualified material which disqualifies only by 4 cubits than to leave it empty which disqualifies by 3 hand-breadths.</i> was surrounded by material which cannot be used for thatching covering four cubits<sup class="footnote-marker">203</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>sukkah</i> is qualified only if no place 4 cubits away from the nearest wall is directly below disqualified material.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Ḥiyya stated: Disqualified space disqualifies in three hand-breadths; disqualified roofing disqualifies in four cubits. Rebbi Yose said, we have stated both. “Disqualified space disqualifies in three hand-breadths,” as we have stated: “If the roofing is three hand-breadths distant from the walls it is disqualified<sup class="footnote-marker">192</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>sukkah</i> is disqualified if along any wall the roofing is not within 3 horizontal hand-breadths.</i>.” “Disqualified roofing disqualifies in four cubits,” as we have stated: “If a house partially collapsed and he thatched over it, it is disqualified if from the walls to the thatching it is four cubits<sup class="footnote-marker">200</sup><i class="footnote">As was stated earlier, a solid roof adjacent or connected to the walls is considered part of the wall, not of the roof. But this is accepted only up to a distance of 4 cubits from the nearest wall. Empty space is acceptable only up to 3 hand-breadths, disqualified material up to 4 cubits.</i>.” Therefore less is qualified. May one sleep under that? Rebbi Isaac ben Elyashiv answered: pourable mud completes a <i>miqweh</i> but one may not immerse in it, here also it completes the <i>sukkah</i> but one may not sleep under it<sup class="footnote-marker">204</sup><i class="footnote">The answer is to a question asked not here but in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 7:1:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.7.1.4"><i>Berakhot</i>7:1 (Notes 19–29</a>) about the interconnection between rules referring to distinct domains. A <i>miqweh</i> can be used to remove impurities only if it contains 40 <i>se`ah</i> of water, about 135 gallons. If there is not enough clear water, the required volume can be obtained by adding mud which is so moist that it can be poured from a bucket (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Mikvaot 7:1" href="/Mishnah_Mikvaot.7.1">Mishnah <i>Miqwaot</i>7:1</a>.) R. Eliezer forbids to immerse oneself in mud (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Mikvaot 2:10" href="/Mishnah_Mikvaot.2.10">Mishnah <i>Miqwaot</i>2:10</a>) but R. Joshua permits. The argument has to be rejected here since R. Joshua will agree that disqualified roofing adjacent to a wall is considered wall, not roofing. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 19a" href="/Sukkah.19a">Babli 19a</a>.</i>. Ḥizqiah said, because it looks like a bent wall<sup class="footnote-marker">205</sup><i class="footnote">In his opinion the ruling is a later rabbinic one based on an explicit argument.</i>. Rebbi Hoshaia stated, because it looks like a bent wall. Rebbi Abba, Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, because it looks like a bent wall. Rebbi Ze`ira, Rebbi Yasa in the name of Cahana, because it looks like a bent wall. Rebbi Jonah said to Rebbi Yose, why do we not say, because disqualified roofing only disqualifies in four cubits<sup class="footnote-marker">206</sup><i class="footnote">In his opinion, this belongs to the traditional rules of <i>sukkah</i>, predating any rabbinic establishment.</i>?

Halakhah 12

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If one makes his <i>sukkah</i> as a shed<sup class="footnote-marker">207</sup><i class="footnote">More like a tent in that it has no solid walls but the walls are slanting and serve as a roof.</i> or that he supported it on a wall<sup class="footnote-marker">208</sup><i class="footnote">The roof is rectangular, one end is supported by a wall, the other on the ground. The Sages hold that walls are not required by the biblical text; they accept a <i>sukkah</i> which is all roof.</i>, Rebbi Eliezer disqualifies because it has no roof but the Sages qualify it. A large reed mat made to lie on is susceptible to impurity<sup class="footnote-marker">209</sup><i class="footnote">Any implement of any kind made to sit or lie on is biblically susceptible to severe impurity; this includes reed and bast mats (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Kelim 20:7" href="/Mishnah_Kelim.20.7">Mishnah <i>Kelim</i>20:7</a>). The majority holds that small mats always are made to lie on; these are always disqualified from being used as roofing; only mats too large to be used as mattresses are possible candidates for roofing materials of a <i>sukkah</i> which must be impervious to impurity.</i> and one may not use it as roofing; if made as roofing it is not susceptible to impurity and one may use it as roofing. Rebbi Eliezer says either large or small if made to lie on it is susceptible to impurity and one may not use it as roofing; if made as roofing one may use it as roofing and it is not susceptible to impurity.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Eliezer agrees with the Sages that it is qualified if it is built on four stones or otherwise elevated from the ground at least one hand-breadth<sup class="footnote-marker">210</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 19b" href="/Sukkah.19b">Babli 19b</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sukkah (Lieberman) 1:10" href="/Tosefta_Sukkah_(Lieberman).1.10">Tosephta 1:10</a>.</i>. It was stated: If one builds his <i>sukkah</i> like the house of the Lebanon Forest<sup class="footnote-marker">211</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Kings 7:2-5" href="/I_Kings.7.2-5"><i>1K</i>. 7:2–5</a>, built on cedar pillars with a slanting transparent roof.</i> it is qualified. For whom is this needed? For Rebbi Eliezer.
Abba bar bar Ḥana<sup class="footnote-marker">212</sup><i class="footnote">His father was Abba bar Ḥana. One refrains from calling a posthumous child by his real name, A ben A, not to publicize his unlucky fate.</i> in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: They formulated this<sup class="footnote-marker">213</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah is formulated only for mats which may be presumed to be made for sitting or lying on, which may become impure even before being used. In all statements about implements being pure or impure, “impure” means susceptible to impurity, “pure” means impervious to impurity.</i> for Usha mats. Rebbi Eliezer<sup class="footnote-marker">214</sup><i class="footnote">Probably one should read “R. Eleazar.”</i> said, [normally]<sup class="footnote-marker">215</sup><i class="footnote">Corrector’s addition from the following sentence. The word is missing in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 19" href="/Sukkah.19">Babli, 19a/b</a>.</i> Usha mats are impure unless made for tents. Normally Tiberian mats<sup class="footnote-marker">216</sup><i class="footnote">Usha mats were made to sit on, Tiberias mats were made to be used on walls or as door fillers.</i> are pure unless made for sitting. Rebbi Isaac bar Ḥaqula, Rebbi Simeon ben Rebbi instructed about those on the doors of stores that they are pure<sup class="footnote-marker">217</sup><i class="footnote">Since they are used as part of a building, they must be impervious to impurity irrespective of the way they are made.</i>. Rebbi Immi instructed that woven ones are impure. May one use them for roofing? The disagreement of Rebbi Eliezer and the Sages<sup class="footnote-marker">218</sup><i class="footnote">The disagreement about small mats mentioned in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sukkah 1:12:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sukkah.1.12.1">Note 209</a>.</i>. Rebbi Isaac ben Rebbi Eleazar instructed in distress to untie the knots<sup class="footnote-marker">219</sup><i class="footnote">Woven mats whose loose ends are tied together instead of a seam. If these are untied, the entire mat will slowly disintegrate; therefore it is considered no longer usable for its original purpose, and susceptibility to impurity induced by this intended use is eliminated.</i> and use them as roofing. We have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">220</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Kelim 20:7" href="/Mishnah_Kelim.20.7">Mishnah <i>Kelim</i>20:7</a>.</i>: “And similarly if one unties the knots it is pure,” and you are saying so<sup class="footnote-marker">221</sup><i class="footnote">There is no reason to forbid these mats as roofing even if there is no emergency situation.</i>?

Chapter 2



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One who sleeps under the bed did not fulfill his duty<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The duty to dwell in the <i>sukkah</i> for 7 days, since he does not sleep under the thatched roof.</i>. Rebbi Jehudah said, we used to sleep under the beds in the presence of the Elders and they did not say a word. Rebbi Simeon said, it happened that Tabi<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that in Rabban Gamliel’s house all male slaves were called <i>Tabi</i>“boy”, and all female ones <i>Tabita</i>“girl”.</i>, Rabban Gamliel’s slave, slept under the bed. Rabban Gamliel said to the Elders, did you see my slave Tabi, who is learned and knows that slaves are free from the obligation of <i>sukkah</i>, and is sleeping under the bed<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Since the obligation of <i>sukkah</i> is a positive commandment restricted in time it does not apply to women (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.7.1">Mishnah <i>Qiddušin</i>1:7</a>) and therefore not to slaves since the obligations of male slaves cannot be greater that those of free women.</i>. According to our method we infer that one who sleeps under the bed did not fulfill his duty<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Since R. Simeon and Rabban Gamliel form a majority against R. Jehudah.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One who sleeps under the bed,” etc. There, we have stated:<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sukkah 1:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sukkah.1.4.1">Mishnah 1:4</a>.</i> “But one may spread it over single posts of the bed.” And here he says so<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">What is the difference between sleepimg under a mosquito net and sleeping under a bed?</i>? Rebbi Eliezer said, there he and his toga are under the <i>sukkah</i>, but here he and his (bed) [toga] are under the (<i>sukkah</i>) [bed]<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">For R. Eliezer read R. Eleazar.<br>There are two versions here. The scribe's sentence is: but here he and his bed are under the <i>sukkah</i>, the corrector’s sentence is: but here he and his toga are under the bed. The scribe’s version notes that the mosquito net is no different from a nightgown, but the bed represents a flat roof of the space under it and therefore the person sleeping under the bed is under the bed which is under the <i>sukkah</i>, not under the <i>sukkah</i>. The corrector’s version expresses the same idea in a more complicated way.</i>. The argument of Rebbi Jehudah seems inverted. There<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">We do not know where R. Jehudah said this explicitly but it is generally accepted as doctrine of R. Tarphon (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 3:7:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.3.7.5"><i>Pesaḥim</i>3:7, Note 142</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Chagigah 1:7:2-6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Chagigah.1.7.2-6"><i>Ḥagigah</i>1:7</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 40b" href="/Kiddushin.40b">Babli <i>Qiddušin</i>40b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 17a" href="/Bava_Kamma.17a"><i>Bava Qamma</i>17a</a>; <i>Sifry Deut</i>. 41, <i>Cant. rabba</i>2:14, <i>Mekhilta dR. Simeon ben Ioḥai</i>19:17 p. 100), a teacher of R. Jehudah.</i> he said, action has precedence over study, and here he says so? Rebbi Jehudah thinks that one sleeping under the bed is like the one sleeping under the <i>sukkah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">The question presupposes that R. Jehudah agreed that one may not sleep under a bed, only he was taught that listening to the Elders in order to learn from them was so meritorious that it overrides the biblical obligation. This is wrong; R. Jehudah holds that one satisfies the obligation to live in the <i>sukkah</i> by staying there, even under the bed.</i>. So much more is Rebbi Jehudah’s argument inverted, as we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah 1:3. The people sleeping on the bed are compared to those occupying the upper <i>sukkah</i>.</i>, “[Rebbi Jehudah says,] if there are no dwellers in the upper one the lower is qualified.” Therefore if there are dwellers in the upper one the lower is disqualified. Rebbi Yose said, there is another space, but here there is no other space<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Since there is only one roofing, the argument of the preceding Note is invalid.</i>.
The argument of Rabban Gamliel is inverted. As it was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 10:1:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.10.1.7"><i>Eruvin</i> Chapter 10, Note 59</a>.</i>, Tabi<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that in Rabban Gamliel’s house all male slaves were called <i>Tabi</i>“boy”, and all female ones <i>Tabita</i>“girl”.</i>, Rabban Gamliel’s slave, put on <i>tefillin</i> and the Sages did not object to him. And here they objected? In order not to push aside the Sages<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">The reason that R. Jehudah declares a <i>sukkah</i> disqualified if made from a couch with 4 poles and a thatched roof as canopy is that usually the canopy is not 10 hand-breadths higher than the couch on which one rests. Therefore, if this distance is clearly larger than 10 hand-breadths, also R. Jehudah accepts it as qualified.</i>. If in order not to push aside the Sages, he could sit outside the <i>sukkah</i>. Tabi, Rabban Gamliel’s slave, wanted to hear the words of the Sages.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If one supports his <i>sukkah</i> by the frame of the couch it is qualified. Rebbi Jehudah says, if it cannot stand by itself it is disqualified.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Immi said, because<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">The reason that R. Jehudah declares a <i>sukkah</i> disqualified if made from a couch with 4 poles and a thatched roof as canopy is that usually the canopy is not 10 hand-breadths higher than the couch on which one rests. Therefore, if this distance is clearly larger than 10 hand-breadths, also R. Jehudah accepts it as qualified.</i> from the middle of the couch it is less than ten hand-breadths. Rebbi Abba said, because one does not base it on what is impure<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">“Is impure” as always means “is susceptible to impurity”; in the case of a couch the severe impurity induced by people whose body is the source of their impurity.</i>. But was it not stated, it happened that the people of Jerusalem were lowering their couches through their windows and putting thatching over them<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sukkah (Lieberman) 2:3" href="/Tosefta_Sukkah_(Lieberman).2.3">Tosephta 2:3</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 21b" href="/Sukkah.21b">Babli 21b</a>. The Tosephta states explicitly that the roofing was fastened to the windows which were 10 hand-breadths high.<br>In the last sentence the difference between (scribe) and [corrector] is between Galilean and Babylonian styles.</i>? If you are saying, because one does not base it on what is impure, here it is put on what is impure. Therefore the reason only is because from the middle of the couch it is less than ten hand-breadths.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A wilted <i>sukkah</i> whose shade is more than its sunshine is qualified<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sukkah 1:1:18" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sukkah.1.1.18">Chapter 1, Notes 117–119</a>.</i>. A stiff one like a house<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">The thatched roof is waterproof as one of a house is supposed to be; as long as it is from vegetal material impervious to impurity, it is qualified.</i>, even though the stars are not visible from inside, is qualified.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rav and Samuel, one said “thinned out”, and one said “wilted”. He who said “thinned out”, if i6t had much shade<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">If a thick thatched roof is thinned out, one has to be careful that shade should still be more than sunlight; it is not enough that sunlight should not be more than shade.</i>; but he who said “wilted,” if it does not have much shade<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">If from the start one has to worry that the roof will not stay for 8 days but will wilt before that time, one has to add enough material that in all situations there is more shade than sunlight. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 22a" href="/Sukkah.22a">Babli 22a</a>,b.</i>.
This implies<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">The statement in the Mishnah that a thatched roof is qualified even if the stars are not visible implies that usually they should be visible; a requirement for the House of Shammai, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 22b" href="/Sukkah.22b">Babli 22b</a>.</i>, that the stars should be seen from inside. Rebbi Levi in the name of Rebbi Ḥama bar Ḥanina, they stated this about the sun star<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">But in the night no stars need be seen for the most pious interpretation.</i>.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One who made his <i>sukkah</i> on top of a cart or a ship<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Even while these are movable there is no problem on the holiday.</i> is qualified and one may enter it on the holiday; on top of a tree<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Since biblically one is forbidden to cut or pluck anything from a tree on a holiday, rabbinically one is forbidden to climb on it. The Mishnah permits the use of the trunks as walls on the intermediate days of Tabernacles but not on the holiday.</i> or on a camel<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">To ride on a camel is biblically forbidden on the Sabbath and rabbinically on the holiday.</i> is qualified but one may not enter it on the holiday. Two human-made {walls}and one a tree, or two by trees and one human-made are qualified but one may not enter it on the holiday. Three human-made and one a tree is qualified and one may enter it on the holiday<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">Since a <i>sukkah</i> is qualified by three walls, the man-made walls make it qualified in all circumstances.</i>. This is the principle: In any case where it can stand by itself when the tree is removed, it is qualified and one may enter it on the holiday.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Where do we hold<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">When may a <i>sukkah</i> on a ship be qualified?</i>? If put on the ground, everybody agrees that it is permitted. If it sails, there is the disagreement between Rebbi Eleazar ben Azariah and Rebbi Aqiba. But we must hold when it is in port<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Greek λιμήν.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph also is in <i>Eruvin</i> Chapter 1.</i> Rebbi Simeon bar Carsana in the name of Rebbi Aḥa: Rebbi Meïr, and Rebbi Yose, and Rebbi Eleazar ben Azariah, all three said the same. Rebbi Meïr of the laths, [as we have stated:]<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Corrector’s addition, not in <i>Eruvin</i>.</i> “it makes impure because of a cover stone, but Rebbi Meïr declares it pure.<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.7.1">Mishnah <i>Eruvin</i>1:7</a>. R. Meïr does not accept the use of an animal in place of a stone in any way, including as a gravestone, since an animal may run away and therefore is not possibly under the rules of a stone.</i>” Rebbi Yose of <i>Ahilut</i>, “Rebbi Yose said, the house on a ship does not transmit impurity<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Oholot 8:2" href="/Mishnah_Oholot.8.2">Mishnah <i>Ahilut</i>8:2</a>. Since the house on a ship is moving all the time it does not qualify as “tent” and the rules of “tent impurity” do not apply to it.</i>.” Rebbi Eleazar ben Azariah, as it was stated: It happened that Rebbi Eleazar ben Azariah and Rebbi Aqiba came in a ship, and Rebbi Aqiba made himself a <i>sukkah</i> on the ship’s deck. There came a wind which blew it away. Rebbi Eleazar ben Azariah said to him, Aqiba, where is your <i>sukkah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">R. Eleazar ben Azariah did not make a <i>sukkah</i> on the ship since he holds that since such a <i>sukkah</i> is not guaranteed to stay for the 8 days of Tabernacles it is not qualified even if the wind does not blow it away. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 23a" href="/Sukkah.23a">Babli 23a</a>.</i>?
<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph and the next also are in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 2:5:5-8:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.2.5.5-8.3"><i>Eruvin</i> Chapter 2 (Notes 91–101</a>).</i>“If he deposited it in a tree higher than ten hand-breadths it is not an <i>eruv</i>; below ten hand-breadths it is an <i>eruv</i> but it is forbidden to move it; below three it is permitted.<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">The topic here is <i>eruv teḥumim</i>. Since there is a biblical prohibition, <i>nobody shall leave his place on the Seventh Day</i>(<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 16:29" href="/Exodus.16.29"><i>Ex.</i> 16:29</a>), “place” is rabbinically defined as a rectangle, oriented EW,NS, extending 2’000 cubits from the built-up area one is in on Friday evening. It is possible to move this boundary by depositing food at the limits reachable on the Sabbath as <i>eruv,</i> symbolic dwelling place, before the Sabbath; then one may walk 2’000 cubits from this place but loses the same amount in the opposite direction. The prohibitions to use a tree on the Sabbath here are rabbinic (“forbidden” but not “liable”); similarly those regarding the <i>sukkah</i> in the next paragraph.</i>” This is difficult. If his <i>eruv</i> is a {valid} <i>eruv</i> one should be permitted to move it. If one is forbidden to move it his <i>eruv</i> should not be a {valid}<i>eruv</i>. He might transgress the rabbinic prohibition and eat. Then also higher than ten {hand-breadths}<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">If one entertains the possibility that people would disregard rabbinic prohibitions, why would an <i>eruv</i> placed higher than 10 hand-breadths be invalid since one could climb the tree in violation of the rabbinic prohibition.</i>? Rav Jehudah in the name of Samuel, explain it if its stem was four {hand-breadths}<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Then the top of the tree is a private domain and biblically one may not eat the <i>eruv</i> while standing in the public domain.</i>. Rebbi Mana said, only if the public domain surrounds it on all sides; he says my Sabbath rest shall be below it<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">Not all <i>eruvim</i> placed 10 hand-breadths high are invalid; only if the tree is in the public domain, the place of the <i>eruv</i> forms a separate private domain of 4-by-4 hand-breadths, and the person making the <i>eruv</i> does not intend to climb the tree at sundown (which would be permitted).</i>. It was stated: If he put it in a basket and hung it on a tree, higher than ten hand-breadths high it is not an <i>eruv</i>; below ten hand-breadths it is an <i>eruv</i> but it is forbidden to move it; below three it is permitted. This is difficult. If his <i>eruv</i> is a {valid} <i>eruv</i> one should be permitted to move it. If one is forbidden to move it his <i>eruv</i> should not be a {valid} <i>eruv</i>. Rebbi Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Ḥinena: He might turn it upside down and annul the private domain created by it<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">Since the basket can be turned and emptied while hanging on the tree it is not moved from its place, the food is accessible, and the <i>eruv</i> valid. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 33a" href="/Eruvin.33a">Babli <i>Eruvin</i>33a</a>,b.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, this implies that a bench in the public domain high ten and wide four {hand-breadths}, since one may tilt it and annul the public<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">With the text in <i>Eruvin</i> read: “private”.</i> domain of it, it implies that his <i>eruv</i> is a {valid}<i>eruv</i> and it is permitted to move it.
[There, we have stated]<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Corrector’s addition from <i>Eruvin</i>; senseless here since the quote is from <i>Sukkah</i>.</i>: “Two human-made and one a tree, or two as trees and one human-made is valid but one may not enter it on the holiday.” Here you are saying it is valid but one may not enter it on the holiday but there you are saying his <i>eruv</i> is a {valid} <i>eruv</i> and it is permitted to move it. Rebbi Jeremiah said, one from the top, the other from the bottom<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">With the text of <i>Eruvin</i> read: “from the side” (in the case of the <i>sukkah</i>.)</i>. This learns from that and that learns from this. This learns from that, if he put it on the side of a tree his <i>eruv</i> is a {valid} <i>eruv</i> and it is (prohibited) [permitted]<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">With the scribe and the text of <i>Eruvin</i> read: “prohibited.”</i> to move it. That learns from this, if there were two pegs protruding and he thatched over them it is valid and one uses it on the holiday<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Since the roof must be on top of the walls, in the case of the Mishnah it must rest on the top of the trees (or top of branches) which is rabbinically forbidden. But the basket is on the side of the trunk and the statement follows the opinion that the sides of tree trunks may be used on Sabbaths and holidays.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, in both cases it is at the side. How is that? As Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa said in the name of Rebbi Ze`ira: This is Rebbi Eleazar ben Simon’s, as Rebbi Eleazar ben Simon says, one is permitted to use the sides of an animal on the Sabbath. There is no difference between sides of an animal and sides of a tree<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shabbat 5:2:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shabbat.5.2.2"><i>Šabbat</i> Chapter 5 Notes 39–43</a>. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 154b" href="/Shabbat.154b">Babli <i>Šabbat</i>154b</a>.</i>.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If one makes his <i>sukkah</i> between trees it is qualified<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">One may use the tree trunks as walls of the <i>sukkah</i> but the roofing must lie on a frame supported by separate poles, not on the trees.</i>. Agents for meritorious deeds<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">However, it is forbidden to leave one’s family on a holiday even for religious reasons, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sukkah (Lieberman) 2:1" href="/Tosefta_Sukkah_(Lieberman).2.1">Tosephta 2:1</a>.</i> are not liable in matters of <i>sukkah</i>. Sick persons and their caregivers are not liable in matters of <i>sukkah.</i> One eats and drinks casually outside the <i>sukkah</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rav<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">Read “Rebbi”.</i> Ḥuna went to Eintab for the indication<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">The indication is the proclamation of the New Moon, held at Eintab (Ḥammat Gader) when the Academies were in Galilee.</i>. While coming he became thirsty but did not accept tasting anything before he went to the <i>sukkah</i> of Rebbi Joḥanan the scribe of Gufta<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">While he was going to fulfill a religious obligation, and drinking water falls under the exemption of casual consumption, he did not want to take advantage of either dispensation. Since the text is formulated in Galilean Aramaic, it is a popular tradition about holy men and not practice to be obligatory.</i>.
Rebbi Mana said, not only persons dangerously sick<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">The sick persons referred to in the Mishnah. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 26a" href="/Sukkah.26a">Babli 26a</a>.</i> but even sick persons not dangerously sick. It was stated:<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sukkah (Lieberman) 2:2" href="/Tosefta_Sukkah_(Lieberman).2.2">Tosephta 2:2</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 26a" href="/Sukkah.26a">Babli 26a</a>.</i> “Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel said, it happened that my eye was hurting in Caesarea and the great Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">He seems to by R. Yose ben Ḥalafta.</i> permitted me and all my caregivers to sleep outside the <i>sukkah</i>.” Rebbi Abba bar Zavda said, the best men at a wedding and all participants in the wedding are not liable for <i>sukkah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">Disputed in the Halakhah and the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 26a" href="/Sukkah.26a">Babli, 26a</a>.</i>.
It was stated:<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sukkah (Lieberman) 2:3" href="/Tosefta_Sukkah_(Lieberman).2.3">Tosephta 2:3</a>.</i> “Daytime watchmen of a city are not liable for the <i>sukkah</i> during daytime but are liable during nighttime. Nighttime watchmen of a city are not liable for the <i>sukkah</i> during nighttime but are liable during daytime. Watchmen during day and night are not liable for <i>sukkah</i> day and night.” This you are saying, about watchmen about troops<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">Gangs of robbers. Regular police who watch against nonviolent thieves are not freed.</i>. But watchmen about money they treated like watchmen of gardens and orchards.
Eudaimon the salt-dealer slept in front of his store. Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba passed by and told him, go and sleep in your <i>sukkah</i>. Rebbi Mana was best man for Rebbi Jacob bar Yafliti. He went and asked Rebbi Yose, who told him, go and sleep in your <i>sukkah</i>. Rebbi Isaac bar Marion was best man for somebody. He asked Rebbi Eleazar who told him, go and sleep in your <i>sukkah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">Disputed in the Halakhah and the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 26a" href="/Sukkah.26a">Babli, 26a</a>.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sukkah (Lieberman) 2:1" href="/Tosefta_Sukkah_(Lieberman).2.1">Tosephta 2:1</a>.</i>“It happened that Rebbi Ilai went to Rebbi Eliezer in Lydda. He said to him, are you not one of those who rest on the holiday? Did they not say that it is not praiseworthy for a scholar to abandon his house<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">His wife.</i> on the holiday and go somewhere else.” As the following: Rebbi Ze`ira went to a circumcision of Rebbi Ila<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">The circumcision of a son of R. Ilai.</i> and did not accept any food. If it was because he never ate anything before the end of <i>musaf</i> prayers<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">This remark is the source of the common usage that a circumcision on a holiday is performed after the Torah reading but before the <i>musaf</i> prayers. Then R. Ze`ira went home and ate with his wife. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Berakhot 28b" href="/Berakhot.28b">Babli <i>Berakhot</i>28b</a> rejects the rule not to eat before <i>musaf</i>.</i>, or we do not know whether it was because it is not praiseworthy for a scholar to abandon his house on the holiday [and go somewhere else; this]<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">Corrector’s addition, problematic since R. Ze`ira did not leave town and therefore could not be said to have “gone somewhere else.”</i> we do not know.
Rebbi Eleazar said, eating may be casual, sleep cannot be casual<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">On Tabernacles, even a short nap has to be done in the <i>sukkah</i>.</i>. The colleagues say, because a person is enveloped in his sleep<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">Once a person sleeps he lost control over the length of his sleep.</i>. Rebbi Ila said, because a person may sleep a little and it is enough. What is the difference between them? If he let another person control his sleep. In the opinion of the (rabbis) [colleagues]<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">The texts of the (scribe) and the [corrector] mean the same.</i> it is permitted, in Rebbi Ila’s opinion forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">Even if another person is charged with waking the sleeper after a short time, it may have been enough for the whole day.</i>.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> It happened that one brought Rabban Joḥanan ben Zakkai a dish to taste, and Rabban Gamliel two dates and a pail of water and they said, bring them to the <i>sukkah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">They did not want to avail themselves of the leniency of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sukkah 2:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sukkah.2.5.1">Mishnah 5</a>.</i>. But when one gave Rebbi Ṣadoq food<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">Including bread.</i> in the volume of less than an egg he took it with a napkin<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">He covered his hands with the towel so he did not have to wash them before touching the bread.</i>, ate it outside of the <i>sukkah</i>, and did not recite a benediction<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">He did not say Grace for volumes less than a chicken egg.</i> afterwards.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> We were thinking to say that he did not say three benedictions but one benediction he did say<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">Three benedictions are the full text of Grace, used after the consumption of bread. A single benediction is the short form of Grace, after consumption of cereal in forms other than bread.</i>. It was found stated, neither one benediction nor three benedictions<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">Since he holds that these benedictions are appropriate only after a satisfying meal, of a volume greater than a chicken egg.</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">This text is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 6:1:11" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.6.1.11"><i>Berakhot</i>6:1, Notes 61–62</a>.</i> It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">The first half of this statement is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Berakhot (Lieberman) 4:7" href="/Tosefta_Berakhot_(Lieberman).4.7">Tosephta <i>Berakhot</i>4:7</a>.</i>: “On everything that needs three benedictions at the end one says ‘Who produces bread from the earth’ at the start, but on everything that does not need three benedictions at the end one does not say ‘Who produces bread from the earth’ at the start.” They objected: But if it is less than the size of an olive, one does not say three benedictions at the end. Does this mean that one should not say “Who produces bread from the earth” at the start? Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa said, it is needed for other kinds<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">The statement does not refer to volume, but to kinds of food; if it is not bread it does not receive the benediction for bread. Hence, no conclusion as to the correct practice can be drawn.</i>.

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Rebbi Eliezer said, a person is obligated to eat fourteen meals in the <i>sukkah</i>, one during daytime and one in the night. But the Sages are saying, there is no fixed amount except only for the night of the first day of the holiday<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">The start of the holiday of Tabernacles, when the benedictions for the <i>sukkah</i> have to be recited. At all other times one may not eat a meal outside the <i>sukkah</i>, but no meals are prescribed.</i>. In addition, Rebbi Eliezer said, one who did not eat on the first day of the holiday makes it up in the night of the last day of the holiday<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">The holiday is seven days, with a full holiday at the beginning; also the obligation of <i>sukkah</i> is seven days (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:34" href="/Leviticus.23.34"><i>Lev.</i> 23:34</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:42" href="/Leviticus.23.42">42</a>). The eighth day is a separate holiday (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:36" href="/Leviticus.23.36"><i>Lev.</i> 23:36</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 29:35" href="/Numbers.29.35"><i>Num</i>. 29:35</a>). R. Eliezer counts the meal in the evening of the Eighth Day as making up for the missed one on the First day even though it cannot be valid in the <i>sukkah</i>.</i>. But the Sages are saying, this cannot be made up, and on this it was said<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Eccl</i>1:15.</i>, <i>what is distorted cannot be fixed, and a deficiency cannot be filled</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> What is Rebbi Eliezer’s reason? It says here<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:42" href="/Leviticus.23.42"><i>Lev.</i> 23:42</a>.</i>, <i>you shall sit</i>, and it says there<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 8:35" href="/Leviticus.8.35"><i>Lev.</i> 8:35</a>, misquoted.</i>, <i>at the door of the Tent of Meeting they shall sit day and night</i>. Since for the sitting mentioned there He made nights like days<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">“Day and night” is interpreted as “some time during the day and some time during the night” (Yoma 1:1, Note 81); the same applies here.</i>, so also for the sitting mentioned here we shall make nights like days. Rebbi Joḥanan in the name of Rebbi Ismael<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 27a" href="/Sukkah.27a">Babli 27a</a>: in the name of R. Simeon ben Yoṣadaq (R. Joḥanan’s teacher); in the Munich ms. of the Babli R. Ṣadoq.</i>: It is said <i>fifteenth</i> for Passover and it is said <i>fifteenth</i> for Tabernacles. Since for <i>fifteenth</i> said for Passover the first night is obligatory and the remaining days are optional<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">This is an application of one of R. Ismael’s hermeneutical rules. It is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 13:6" href="/Exodus.13.6"><i>Ex.</i> 13:6</a>) <i>seven days you shall eat mazzot</i>, and (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 16:8" href="/Deuteronomy.16.8"><i>Deut</i>. 16:8</a>) <i>six days you shall eat mazzot</i>. R. Ismael’s rule reads: Anything that was in a set and was taken out from the set implies the same for the whole set.” Since the last mentioned verse implies that on the seventh day one need not eat unleavened bread (one may not eat leavened bread, however), the same is true for all seven days of Passover with the only exception of the first night of which it is said explicitly (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:18" href="/Exodus.12.18"><i>Ex.</i> 12:18</a>): <i>In the evening you shall eat mazzot</i>.</i>, so also for <i>fifteenth</i> said for Tabernacles the first night is obligatory and the remaining days are optional. The colleagues asked, since there only that one comes to eat <i>mazzah</i> with an appetite<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 10:1:1-14" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.10.1.1-14">Mishnah <i>Pesaḥim</i>10:1</a>: “On Passover eve, starting close to afternoon prayers, a person may not eat until nightfall” (so he shall eat the first <i>mazzah</i> with a good appetite.) There is no similar rule spelled out for Tabernacles; R. Joḥanan’s argument is homiletic but cannot be legal.</i>, so also here only they come to eat in the <i>sukkah</i> with appetite? Rebbi Ze`ira asked: Since there only one has to eat <i>mazzah</i> in the volume of an olive<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">The biblical obligation contained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:18" href="/Exodus.12.18"><i>Ex.</i> 12:18</a> can only be fulfilled by eating a <i>mazzah</i> in an amount that triggers a biblical obligation to say Grace. But for the <i>sukkah</i>, sitting is prescribed but not eating; the same standards cannot apply.</i>, so also here only one has to eat grain [in the <i>sukkah</i>] in the volume of an olive? Rebbi Hoshaia said, the <i>sukkah</i> is obligatory all seven days. Rebbi Berekhiah said, they<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">R. Joḥanan, who requires a meal only the first night, and R. Hoshaia, who requires it for 7 days.</i> disagree. Rebbi Abuna said, they do not disagree. What Rebbi Joḥanan said, if he put his mind to it; what Rebbi Hoshaia said, if he did not put his mind to it<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">R. Hoshaia really does not require to eat in the <i>sukkah</i> for 7 days; he is more lenient than R. Joḥanan and requires that once one must come to the <i>sukkah</i> to recite the appropriate benedictions (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sukkah 2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sukkah.2.1">Halakhah 1:1, Notes 123–126</a>) but this does not necessarily be the first night.</i>.
It was stated in Rebbi Eliezer’s name: He who did not make his <i>sukkah</i> before the holiday may not make it on the holiday<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 27" href="/Sukkah.27">Babli 27a/b</a>.</i>. But did not Bar Qappara state, if his <i>sukkah</i> collapsed on the holiday, he shall rebuild it on the holiday. Rebbi Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Ḥinena: Rebbi Eliezer punished him because he did not make his <i>sukkah</i> before the holiday<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">But he agrees with Bar Qappara to rebuild a <i>sukkah</i> on the intermediary days of the holiday week.</i>. Rebbi Eliezer’s opinion seems inverted. There, he says, a person is obligated to eat fourteen meals in the <i>sukkah</i>, and here he says so<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">He seems to contradict himself in the Mishnah since he requires a make-up only for the meal in the first night but not for the remaining 13 meals.</i>? Rebbi Aḥa said, they were compared as commandments<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">In the <i>sukkah</i> and at the consecration of Tent of Meeting the commandment is to sit; while in both cases eating there is implied, it is not spelled out as biblical commandment.</i>.

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If a person’s head and most of his body was in the <i>sukkah</i> but his table inside the house, the House of Shammai declare it disqualified but the House of Hillel qualified. The House of Hillel said to the House of Shammai, it happened that the elders of the House of Shammai and the House of Hillel went to visit Joḥanan from Hauran and found him with his head and most of his body in the <i>sukkah</i> but his table inside the house. The House of Shammai answered them, is this a proof? They said to him, if you are used to do this, you never in your life did fulfill the obligation of <i>sukkah</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Not only his table, but even a place for his table<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">It is implied by the Mishnah that the House of Shammai require a <i>sukkah</i> which accommodates most of a person’s body and a table. This does not imply that a table must actually be put into the <i>sukkah</i>; all that is needed is space for a table.</i>. How much is a place for his table? A hand-breadth<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">A hand-breadth square. It is one of the peculiarities of the idioms of both Talmudim that linear and volume measures are clearly named, but area measures (other than agricultural ones determined by the amount of seeds used for a unit) are subsumed under the names of the corresponding linear measures.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph, introductory to the discussion why practice in this case follows the Hose of Shammai, essentially is from <i>Terumot</i>5:4 (Notes 65–71). Mishnah <i>Terumot</i>5:1 states that impure heave which was mixed with other produce must be destroyed (burned) as long as the impure heave constitutes at least 1% of the total. If the amount of impure heave is &lt; 1%, an amount equal to that of the impure heave can be removed from the total, “lifted”, with the impurity transferred to the lifted amount, and the remainder used for its original purpose. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sukkah 2:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sukkah.2.4.1">Mishnah 4</a> reports a dispute between the Houses of Hillel and Shammai about impure heave which fell into 100 times its volume of pure heave, where the House of Shammai forbid everything but the House of Hillel permit. At the conclusion of the argument, R. Eliezer holds that 1 part of 101 has to be lifted and burned, but the Sages say that the impure was lost in its minority, i. e., since everything is heave all 101 parts may be eaten by a Cohen as pure heave.</i> Rebbi Yudan bar Pazi and Rebbi Ayvo bar Naggari were sitting and saying: We did state “after they conceded“; who conceded to whom? The House of Shammai to the House of Hillel or the House of Hillel to the House of Shammai? They said, let us go out and study. The went out and heard Rebbi Ḥizqiah, Rebbi Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Jehudah ben Levi. We never find the House of (Hillel) [Shammai] conceding to the House of (Shammai) [Hillel] except in this matter. Rebbi Ḥunah in the name of Rav Abbaia: The Mishnah<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Ṭevul Yom</i>2:7. A <i>Ṭevul Yom</i> is a person who had been impure, is no longer impure after immersion in a <i>miqweh</i>, but is prevented from touching or consuming <i>sancta</i> until be becomes pure at the following nightfall (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 22:7" href="/Leviticus.22.7"><i>Lev.</i>22:7</a>). If in the period between his immersion and sundown he touches heave, it becomes unusable; this is classified as “impure in the third degree”. If the <i>ṭevul yom</i> touches a stream of heave fluid (wine or oil) poured from one vessel to the other, in the opinion of the Sadducees the entire fluid in both vessels becomes unusable (MMT lines 55–58). In rabbinic tradition, only the part touched by the <i>ṭevul yom</i> becomes unusable; the fluid cannot be used because unusable heave is contained in it, but it can be repaired by the usual lifting of one out of 101.</i> says so. “If someone pours from vessel to vessel and a <i>ṭevul yom</i> touches it, it should be lifted by 101.” If you say that the House of Hillel conceded to the House of Shammai that it cannot be lifted, who is the Tanna here, neither the House of Shammai nor the House of Hillel! Rebbi Idi said, we might say that the House of Hillel stated this before they conceded. Rebbi Yose said, the Mishnah said so<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Terumot</i>5:4.</i>: “After they conceded, Rebbi Eliezer said it should be lifted and burned,” and is Rebbi Eliezer not a follower of Shammai? Rebbi Ḥinena said, the Mishnah said so, after they conceded one to another that it should be lifted, the House of Shammai recused themselves and the others agree with us. Rebbi Abun says, there is another answer<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">Which the House of Hillel could have given to the House of Shammai, in addition to the argument presented in the Mishnah.</i>, following what Rebbi Hoshaia stated: Since pure {heave}, which for outsiders is forbidden as deadly sin, can be lifted, certainly also impure {heave} which is {prohibited} as positive commandment<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">Which is classified as a misdemeanor, rather than a felony.</i> for Cohanim.
From where did the House of Hillel merit that practice should be fixed following their words? Rebbi Jehudah bar Pazi said, because they quoted the words of the House of Shammai before their own words<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 13b" href="/Eruvin.13b">Babli <i>Eruvin</i>13b</a>. Historically one has to note that no renowned scholar of the House of Shammai is known born after the destruction of the Temple.</i>. Not only that, but if they were convinced by the words of the House of Shammai they changed their opinions. Rebbi Simon bar Zeved objected before Rebbi Ila: Or could we say that the Tanna saw that they were older and quoted them first? But did we not state, “it happened that the elders of the House of Shammai and the House of Hillel went to visit Joḥanan from Hauran”? We could have said, our elders and your elders<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">The formulation “elders of the House of Shammai and elders of the House of Hillel” is original, not a formulation of the editor of the Mishnah.</i>. Rebbi Ze`ira said, Rav Ḥuna in the name of Rav: Practice follows the House of Shammai<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">In the case of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sukkah 2:8:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sukkah.2.8.1">Mishnah 2:8</a>. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 3a" href="/Sukkah.3a">Babli 3a</a>.</i>. Rebbi Jeremiah, Rebbi Samuel bar Rav Isaac in the name of Rav: Since the House of Shammai dismissed the House of Hillel<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">No answer from the House of Hillel is noted.</i>, this implies that practice follows their words.

Halakhah 9

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Women, slaves, and minors are not obligated for the <i>sukkah</i>. Any minor who does not need his mother is obligated<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">As a minor he cannot be responsible for anything. His parents are obligated to bring him to the <i>sukkah</i> as part of his religious training.</i> for the <i>sukkah</i>. It happened that the Elder Shammai’s daughter-in-law gave birth and he removed the roof insulation and thatched the roof over the bed for the minor.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> What is a minor<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">Whose parents do not have to bring him to the <i>sukkah</i>.</i>? In the house of Rebbi Yannai they said, any who needs his mother to cleanse him. Rebbi Joḥanan said, any who awakes from his sleep and cries “Mamma”<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 28b" href="/Sukkah.28b">Babli 28b</a>.</i>. Rebbi Hoshaia stated: Any minor who does not need his mother is obligated for the <i>sukkah</i> but goes out with his mother’s <i>eruv</i><sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">If his mother made an <i>eruv</i> to move her Sabbath boundary on a Sabbath or holiday, it is not necessary to make a separate <i>eruv</i> for the child. Since the mother is not obligated in matters of <i>sukkah</i>, her status there cannot influence his status in matters of <i>eruv teḥumim</i>.</i>.

Halakhah 10

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> All seven days a person makes his <i>sukkah</i> permanent and his house casual. If it started to rain when is one permitted to leave? When the cereal becomes smelly. One has for this a parable, to what does this compare? To a slave who comes to mix a cup for his owner and the latter throws the pitcher in his face.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> It is written<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:42" href="/Leviticus.23.42"><i>Lev.</i> 23:42</a>.</i>, <i>in sukkot you shall sit</i>. But sitting means dwelling<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 28b" href="/Sukkah.28b">Babli 28b</a>.</i>, as you are saying, <i>you shall inherit it and sit in it</i><sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 11:31" href="/Deuteronomy.11.31"><i>Deut</i>. 11:31</a>.</i><i>;</i> that he eats in the <i>sukkah</i>, and rests in the <i>sukkah</i>, and brings his utensils to the <i>sukkah</i>.
[“If it started to rain when is one permitted to leave,: etc,] Not only if it gets smelly but even if it could get smelly. Not only a cereal of groats but any dish<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 29a" href="/Sukkah.29a">Babli 29a</a>.</i>. Just as one evacuates because of rain one evacuates because of a hot wind and because of mosquitoes. Rabban Gamliel entered and left the entire night; Rebbi Eliezer entered and left the entire night. One of Rebbi Mana’s students instructed a relative of the Patriarch: If he left, one does not importune him to return<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 29a" href="/Sukkah.29a">Babli 29a</a>.</i>. He had not heard that Rabban Gamliel entered and left the entire night; Rebbi Eliezer entered and left the entire night.

Chapter 3



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A robbed or dried up <i>lulav</i><sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:40" href="/Leviticus.23.40"><i>Lev.</i>23:40</a>: <i>You shall take for yourselves on the First Day a fruit of the splendor tree, date-tree palms, a branch of the rope tree, and brook-willows</i>. The <i>lulav</i> is the young palm branch. While in the verse it is called “palm”, because the leaves of the palm-tree spread out from the spine of the branch like fingers of a hand, it is traditional to take a young branch with the leaves still tightly clinging to its spine. From this the name <b>לולב</b> “tube” (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sukkah 3:1:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sukkah.3.1.6">Note 27</a>).</i> is disqualified. Of an <i>ashera</i><sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">A tree worshipped as a pagan deity, forbidden for all use.</i> or a seduced city<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">An apostate city practicing idolatry which has to be destroyed and all its property burned, forbidden for all usufruct, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 13:13-18" href="/Deuteronomy.13.13-18"><i>Deut</i>13:13–18</a>.</i>, it is disqualified. If it was truncated, its leaves broken out, it is disqualified. If its leaves were spread out, it is qualified; Rebbi Jehudah says, he shall tie them at the top<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">As long as the leaves are connected they can be tied together to produce the desired shape of a solid tube.</i>. The stone palms of Iron Mountain are qualified. Any <i>lulav</i> three hand-breadths long to shake it<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in Mishnaiot 8 ff., the main use of the “four kinds” mentioned in the verse is to shake them during the recitation of <i>Hallel</i>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 113-118" href="/Psalms.113-118"><i>Ps.</i> 113–118</a>.</i> is qualified.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “A robbed or dried up <i>lulav</i> is disqualified,” etc. Rebbi Ḥiyya stated: <i>You shall take for yourselves</i><sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:40" href="/Leviticus.23.40"><i>Lev.</i>23:40</a>: <i>You shall take for yourselves on the First Day a fruit of the splendor tree, date-tree palms, a branch of the rope tree, and brook-willows</i>. The <i>lulav</i> is the young palm branch. While in the verse it is called “palm”, because the leaves of the palm-tree spread out from the spine of the branch like fingers of a hand, it is traditional to take a young branch with the leaves still tightly clinging to its spine. From this the name <b>לולב</b> “tube” (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sukkah 3:1:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sukkah.3.1.6">Note 27</a>).</i>, from what is your own<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 29b" href="/Sukkah.29b">Babli 29b</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Emor, Chapter 17 2" href="/Sifra,_Emor,_Chapter_17.2"><i>Sifra Emor Pereq</i>17(2</a>). The paragraph is a much shortened version of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Vayikra Rabbah 30:6" href="/Vayikra_Rabbah.30.6"><i>Lev.</i><i>r</i>. 30(6</a>), starting with R. Ḥiyya’s statement and ending with the lesson “woe to this one …”</i>. Rebbi Levi said, to what is one compared who takes a robbed <i>lulav</i>? To one who honored the ruler with a basket and it turned out that it belonged to the latter. One says, woe to this one whose defender<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Greek συνήγορος.</i> became his prosecutor<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Greek κατήγορος.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is part of a text in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 12:2:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.12.2.2"><i>Yebamot</i>12:2 (Notes 50–59</a>,<b>ז</b>).</i> A ram’s horn of idolatry<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">The ram's horn used on New Year's Day.</i> or a seduced city<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">The usufruct of any implements of idolatrous worship is forbidden. The horn is only permitted since Divine Commandments are not for enjoyment or use. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Rosh Hashanah 28a" href="/Rosh_Hashanah.28a">Babli, <i>Roš Haššanah</i>28a</a>, agrees.</i>. Rebbi Eleazar said, it is qualified. Rebbi Ḥiyya stated, it is qualified. Rebbi Hoshaia stated, it is disqualified<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Rosh Hashanah 28a" href="/Rosh_Hashanah.28a">Babli, <i>Roš Haššanah</i>28a</a>, disagrees in this case since a ram's horn in a “seduced city” must be burned and therefore is considered to be ashes even if not burned. Arguments of this kind are absent in the Yerushalmi.</i>. Everybody agrees about a <i>lulav</i> that it is disqualified. What is the difference between a ram’s horn and a <i>lulav</i>? Rebbi Yose said, about a <i>lulav</i> it is written: <i>You shall take for yourselves</i><sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:40" href="/Leviticus.23.40"><i>Lev.</i>23:40</a>: <i>You shall take for yourselves on the First Day a fruit of the splendor tree, date-tree palms, a branch of the rope tree, and brook-willows</i>. The <i>lulav</i> is the young palm branch. While in the verse it is called “palm”, because the leaves of the palm-tree spread out from the spine of the branch like fingers of a hand, it is traditional to take a young branch with the leaves still tightly clinging to its spine. From this the name <b>לולב</b> “tube” (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sukkah 3:1:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sukkah.3.1.6">Note 27</a>).</i>, from what is your own<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 29b" href="/Sukkah.29b">Babli 29b</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Emor, Chapter 17 2" href="/Sifra,_Emor,_Chapter_17.2"><i>Sifra Emor Pereq</i>17(2</a>). The paragraph is a much shortened version of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Vayikra Rabbah 30:6" href="/Vayikra_Rabbah.30.6"><i>Lev.</i><i>r</i>. 30(6</a>), starting with R. Ḥiyya’s statement and ending with the lesson “woe to this one …”</i>. Not from what is forbidden for usufruct. But here, <i>a day of horn blowing it shall be for you</i><sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 29:1" href="/Numbers.29.1"><i>Num</i>. 29:1</a>.</i>. Rebbi Eleazar said, there he fulfills his obligation by the thing itself<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">It is a general rule that a stolen or robbed object has to be returned in kind to its owner only if it is unchanged. Once the thief or robber has changed it so its original shape cannot be restored, it becomes the property of its current holder and the original owner only has a claim for its money’s worth and eventual fines (<i>Or zarua</i> § 274). Therefore it is the robber’s property and even in the case of a <i>lulav</i> could be used on the holiday.</i>. But here he fulfills his obligation by the sound. Is there a sound which is forbidden for usufruct?
Where do they disagree<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 29:1" href="/Numbers.29.1"><i>Num</i>. 29:1</a>.</i>? If he robbed it smoothed. But if he robbed it and smoothed it, he owes him money<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">It is a general rule that a stolen or robbed object has to be returned in kind to its owner only if it is unchanged. Once the thief or robber has changed it so its original shape cannot be restored, it becomes the property of its current holder and the original owner only has a claim for its money’s worth and eventual fines (<i>Or zarua</i> § 274). Therefore it is the robber’s property and even in the case of a <i>lulav</i> could be used on the holiday.</i>. If he robbed a <i>lulav</i> from one, a myrtle branch from another, and a willow branch from a third and tied them together<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 30a" href="/Sukkah.30a">Babli 30a</a> holds that this case certainly is disqualified since the tie is reversible and a reversible change does not transfer ownership.</i>? Let us hear from the following: A robbed <i>sukkah</i>, there are Tannaim who stated, it is qualified; there are Tannaim who stated, it is disqualified. Rebbi Simon in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: He who said, it is qualified, if he robbed the real estate<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Since he changed the real estate by building a <i>sukkah</i> on it. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 31a" href="/Sukkah.31a">Babli 31a</a> disagrees since (1) real estate cannot be robbed since the owners eventually will be able to reclaim their property by proving their title in court (a principle accepted in the Yerushalmi, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 2:5:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.2.5.3"><i>Peah</i>2:7 Note 132</a>, <i>Kilaim</i>7:5 Note 72, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 10:6:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.10.6.2"><i>Bava qamma</i>10:6 Note 66</a>) and (2) the <i>sukkah</i> is only a temporary building.</i>. He who said, it is disqualified, if he robbed the agricultural waste. It is impossible that he not tie them together<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Therefore even if he robbed the material for the roof it should become the robber’s property.</i>. If he places it from the top. The rabbis of Caesarea in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: In every case it is disqualified<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">They hold that only permanent change can effect a transfer of ownership, the doctrine of the Babli.</i>. What is the robbed one which is disqualified<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">This question does not contradict the statement of the rabbis from Caesarea. Accepting their reasoning, what are the limits of “robbing a <i>sukkah</i>”?</i>? Any time a person enters another’s <i>sukkah</i> without his knowledge<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 31a" href="/Sukkah.31a">Babli 31a</a> characterized this as R. Eliezer’s minority opinion.</i>. As the following. Gamliel the twin made himself a <i>sukkah</i> on the market<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Using public property for a <i>sukkah</i> without proper authorization is robbing the public.</i>. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish passed by and told him, who gave you permission?
“Dried up is disqualified.” Rebbi Abbin in the name of Rebbi Jehudah bar Pazi: The dried-up is disqualified because <i>the dead ones cannot praise the Eternal</i><sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 115:17" href="/Psalms.115.17"><i>Ps.</i> 115:17</a>.</i>. It was stated in the name of Rebbi Jehudah, “the dried up one is qualified. Rebbi Jehudah told them, do they not have their sons inherit their <i>lulavim</i> in the port cities overseas<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Northern ports in countries without palm trees, where it may be impossible to get a new <i>lulav</i> every year. כרך is Greek χάραξ “palisade”. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sukkah (Lieberman) 2:9" href="/Tosefta_Sukkah_(Lieberman).2.9">Tosephta 2:9</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 31" href="/Sukkah.31">Babli 31a/b</a>.</i>?
