They told him, one does not make inferences from emergency situations.” They asked before Rebbi Avinna, if its fringe was dried up<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">If the top leaves were wilted.</i>, what? What is the difference to when its head was chopped off? He told them, the first is splendor<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">While “splendor” is mentioned only for the fruit of the splendor tree, if at all possible the entire bundle has to look its best.</i>, the second is not splendor.
Rebbi Maluk in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: If the spine is split it is as if the leaves were spread out<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">In this case, the Mishnah states that it is qualified. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 32a" href="/Sukkah.32a">Babli 32a</a> R. Matun in the name of R. Johua ben Levi is reported to state that it is disqualified.</i>. <i>Date-palm palms</i><sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:40" href="/Leviticus.23.40"><i>Lev.</i>23:40</a>: <i>You shall take for yourselves on the First Day a fruit of the splendor tree, date-tree palms, a branch of the rope tree, and brook-willows</i>. The <i>lulav</i> is the young palm branch. While in the verse it is called “palm”, because the leaves of the palm-tree spread out from the spine of the branch like fingers of a hand, it is traditional to take a young branch with the leaves still tightly clinging to its spine. From this the name <b>לולב</b> “tube” (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sukkah 3:1:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sukkah.3.1.6">Note 27</a>).</i>. Rebbi Tarphon says, tied date-palms<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">While in biblical Hebrew כַּפּוֹת is the plural of כַּף “palm, hand”, in rabbinic Hebrew (and Aramaic) there exists a verb כפת “to tie down”. R. Tarphon reads the verse in rabbinic Hebrew and disqualifies palm branches which are not tied tightly, a position attributed to R. Jehudah in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sukkah (Lieberman) 2:10" href="/Tosefta_Sukkah_(Lieberman).2.10">Tosephta 2:10</a>.</i>. Rebbi Aqiba says, <i>palms of date palms</i> as they are called<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">He reads the verse in biblical Hebrew and accepts the palm branch even if its leaves are spread out.</i>. Rebbi Jehudah says, if they are spread out he shall tie them together<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">In principle he agrees with R. Tarphon (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 31a" href="/Sukkah.31a">Babli 31a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 32a" href="/Sukkah.32a">32a</a>) but he requires actual tying only if otherwise the leaves would be spread out. Attributed to R. Tarphon in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Emor, Chapter 16 4" href="/Sifra,_Emor,_Chapter_16.4"><i>Sifra Emor Pereq</i>16(4</a>).</i>.
The following are the stone palms of Iron Mountain which are qualified: Any of which the top of one reaches the root of the next<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">There is no lower limit on the length of the leaves, but the spine of the branch has to be completely covered by leaves. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 32a" href="/Sukkah.32a">Babli 32a</a>.</i>. It was stated: Burned is disqualified, looking like burned is qualified<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">“Burned” means not really burned but stone dry and dark. As long as there is moisture in the spine, the color is irrelevant. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 32a" href="/Sukkah.32a">Babli 32a</a>.</i>.
Rebbi Simeon bar Abba in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan. So is the Mishnah: If one can shake it, it is qualified<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>lulav</i> must be large enough so that one may hold it in his hand, and it visibly bends somewhat if one shakes it.</i>. It was stated: Myrtle and willow twig three, and <i>lulav</i> four<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">Since the numerals are in the masculine they denote hand-breadths.</i>. It was stated, by the cubit of five {hand-breadths}, the words of Rebbi Tarphon, but the Sages are saying, by the cubit of six. For Rebbi Tarphon, large hand-breadths<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">The standard is the cubit, 1/8000 of an Egyptian parasang. The hand-breadth of the vessel standard, 5 to a cubit, is 6/5 of the hand-breadth of the distance standard, 6 to a cubit.</i>; in the opinion of the Sages, thin hand-breadths. Rebbi Jonah and Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish in the name of Rebbi Jehudah the Patriarch: The <i>lulav</i> a hand-breadth<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Exceeding the accompanying branches by a hand-breadth. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 32b" href="/Sukkah.32b">Babli 32b</a> identifies this with Rebbi Joḥanan’s criterion (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sukkah 3:1:8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sukkah.3.1.8">Note 32</a>).</i>. Rebbi Simon in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: the hyssop<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Needed to sprinkle, e. g., the water to purify a person healed from skin disease..</i> a hand-breadth. Rebbi Ze`ira asked, the <i>lulav</i> a hand-breadth, the hyssop a hand-breadth? Rebbi Yose said, Rebbi Simon confirmed it. Rebbi Ḥinena, Rebbi Simon in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: the <i>lulav</i> a hand-breadth, the hyssop a hand-breadth, the ram’s horn a hand-breadth<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">The ram’s horn for New Year’s Day must extend one hand-breadth outside the hand which holds it.</i>, the placenta a hand-breadth<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">As sign of a miscarriage.</i>, and some are saying, also the third wall a hand-breadth<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sukkah (Lieberman) 1:13" href="/Tosefta_Sukkah_(Lieberman).1.13">Tosephta 1:13</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sukkah 1:1:10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sukkah.1.1.10">Chapter 1 Note 10</a>.</i>. Rebbi Ze`ira asked, the <i>lulav</i> a hand-breadth except the spine<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">The excess length of the <i>lulav</i> has to be formed by its leaves, not the spine. Similarly, the top of the hyssop dipped in the purifying water is not counted for the minimum length.</i>? The hyssop a hand-breadth except for the stalk? Rebbi Yose, Rebbi Tabi in the name of Rav; Rebbi Ḥinena, Rebbi Parnakh, Rav Mattanah, Yose bar Manisha in the name of Rav: The <i>lulav</i> a hand-breadth except for the spine; the hyssop a hand-breadth except for its stalks.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A robbed or dried up myrtle is disqualified. Of an <i>ashera</i><sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">A tree worshipped as a pagan deity, forbidden for all use.</i> or a seduced city<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">An apostate city practicing idolatry which has to be destroyed and all its property burned, forbidden for all usufruct, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 13:13-18" href="/Deuteronomy.13.13-18"><i>Deut</i>13:13–18</a>.</i>, it is disqualified. If it was truncated, its leaves broken out, or its berries more than its leaves, it is disqualified; if one reduced their number it is qualified but one may not reduce on the holiday.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> It is written, <i>and a branch from the rope tree</i><sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:40" href="/Leviticus.23.40"><i>Lev.</i>23:40</a>: <i>You shall take for yourselves on the First Day a fruit of the splendor tree, date-tree palms, a branch of the rope tree, and brook-willows</i>. The <i>lulav</i> is the young palm branch. While in the verse it is called “palm”, because the leaves of the palm-tree spread out from the spine of the branch like fingers of a hand, it is traditional to take a young branch with the leaves still tightly clinging to its spine. From this the name <b>לולב</b> “tube” (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sukkah 3:1:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sukkah.3.1.6">Note 27</a>).</i>, a tree whose branches cover it and it rises like plaiting<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">The tops of the leaves look like forming a spiral.</i>. Which one is this? The myrtle. If you would say the olive tree, it rises like plaiting but its branches do not cover it. If you would say the vine<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">Arabic (from the Persian) זרגון “gold colored”, used for branches of the vine (French <i>sarment</i>).</i>, its branches cover it but it does not rise like plaiting. Therefore, which one is this? The myrtle.
Ḥiyya bar Ada in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish. They taught<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">That the berries of the myrtle twig have to be removed.</i> about the blackish ones. Why? Because they do not resemble its wood or because its fruit is ripened? What is the difference between these? If it is used to grow green ones. If you are saying, because they do not<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Clearly this has to be deleted.</i> resemble its wood, they do not resemble its wood. Therefore the reason must be because its fruit is ripened.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A robbed or dried up willow twig is disqualified. Of an <i>ashera</i><sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">A tree worshipped as a pagan deity, forbidden for all use.</i> or a seduced city<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">An apostate city practicing idolatry which has to be destroyed and all its property burned, forbidden for all usufruct, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 13:13-18" href="/Deuteronomy.13.13-18"><i>Deut</i>13:13–18</a>.</i>, it is disqualified. If it was truncated, its leaves broken out, or the osier<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">Arabic צפצפ “osier, willow from whose twigs one makes baskets.”</i> are disqualified. Wilted, and of which part of the leaves have fallen off, and from an unirrigated place<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">A field which gets its water exclusively from the rain, called <i>ba`al</i>.</i>, are qualified.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Emor, Chapter 16 6" href="/Sifra,_Emor,_Chapter_16.6"><i>Sifra Emor Pereq</i>16(6</a>), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 34a" href="/Sukkah.34a">Babli 34a</a>.</i>“It is written, <i>and brook-willows</i><sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:40" href="/Leviticus.23.40"><i>Lev.</i>23:40</a>: <i>You shall take for yourselves on the First Day a fruit of the splendor tree, date-tree palms, a branch of the rope tree, and brook-willows</i>. The <i>lulav</i> is the young palm branch. While in the verse it is called “palm”, because the leaves of the palm-tree spread out from the spine of the branch like fingers of a hand, it is traditional to take a young branch with the leaves still tightly clinging to its spine. From this the name <b>לולב</b> “tube” (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sukkah 3:1:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sukkah.3.1.6">Note 27</a>).</i>. I admit not only from a brook, from where also from an unirrigated place or from mountains? The verse says, <i>and willows</i><sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">The plural and the prefixed “and” indicate the inclusion of two additional kinds of willow twig.</i>. Abba Shaul says, <i>brook-willows</i>, two. A willow twig for the <i>lulav</i> and a willow twig for the Temple<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">He disputes the admission of willow twigs from trees growing far from water by pointing out that willow twigs were needed not only for the “4 kinds” tied to the <i>lulav</i> but also to surround the altar (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sukkah 4:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sukkah.4.3.1">Mishnah 4:4</a>). This explains both the plural and the prefixed “and”.</i>.” Then why was it written <i>and brook-willows</i>? To exclude the osier. <sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 34a" href="/Sukkah.34a">Babli 34a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sukkah (Lieberman) 2:7" href="/Tosefta_Sukkah_(Lieberman).2.7">Tosephta 2:7</a>.</i>“What is an osier? If its is like a sickle. It was stated: Like a sickle it is disqualified, like a plane it is (qualified) [disqualified]<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">The correct text is the (scribe’s); the [corrector’s] clearly is erroneous.</i>. What is a disqualified willow twig? The leaf is round and the stem white. What is a qualified willow twig? The leaf is long and the stem red.”

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Rebbi Ismael says, three myrtle branches and two willow twigs, one <i>lulav</i> and one <i>etrog</i>; even two truncated and one not chopped off. Rebbi Tarphon says, even all three truncated. Rebbi Aqiba says, just as there is one <i>lulav</i> and one <i>etrog</i> so there is one myrtle branch and one willow twig.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Emor, Chapter 15 7" href="/Sifra,_Emor,_Chapter_15.7"><i>Sifra Emor Pereq</i>15(7</a>), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 34b" href="/Sukkah.34b">Babli 34b</a>.</i>“Rebbi Ismael explained, <sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:40" href="/Leviticus.23.40"><i>Lev.</i>23:40</a>: <i>You shall take for yourselves on the First Day a fruit of the splendor tree, date-tree palms, a branch of the rope tree, and brook-willows</i>. The <i>lulav</i> is the young palm branch. While in the verse it is called “palm”, because the leaves of the palm-tree spread out from the spine of the branch like fingers of a hand, it is traditional to take a young branch with the leaves still tightly clinging to its spine. From this the name <b>לולב</b> “tube” (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sukkah 3:1:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sukkah.3.1.6">Note 27</a>).</i><i>a fruit of the splendor tree</i>, one. <i>Date-palm roped</i>, one<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">He reads the verse in accordance with R. Tarphon, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sukkah 3:1:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sukkah.3.1.6">Note 27</a>. The clause is in the singular.</i>. <i>A branch of the rope tree,</i> three<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">While “branch” is in the singular, there are three words to describe the myrtle branch.</i>. <i>And brook-willows</i>, two<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">As always, an unspecified plural means “2”, the smallest number &gt; 1.</i>. And two lifted ones; one which is not truncated. Rebbi Tarphon says, even if all three are truncated.” Rebbi Abba bar Mamal asked before Rebbi Immi: Since Rebbi Ismael adds for the myrtle<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">While R. Ismael seems to accept that only one myrtle branch is really needed since עֲנַף is a singular, the additional two being inferred from the additional words used in his characterization requiring two additional branches, it is difficult to understand why he requires only one <i>lulav</i> and only one <i>etrog</i> instead of two each.</i>, should he not add for the other kinds? He answered, you are thinking that Rebbi Ismael thinks that truncated is splendor, and we have stated, “Rebbi Tarphon says, even if all three are truncates.” Nobody says “even” if he does not accept the preceding statement<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">Since R. Tarphon and R. Ismael agree in the interpretation of the verse, the requirement that one branch be not truncated for R. Ismael is a rabbinic requirement, not derived from the verse.</i>. Rebbi Ḥaggai asked before Rebbi Jose: What does Rebbi Tarphon add to the words of Rebbi Ismael? He answered him, Rebbi Ismael does not think that truncated is splendor, but Rebbi Tarphon thinks that truncated is splendor. When Rebbi Yasa came up here he saw them being selective about myrtle branches<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">That they should not be truncated and tightly cover the stem of the branch.</i>. He said, why are the Westerners selective about myrtle branches? He had not heard thar Rebbi Simon said in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: <i>And they made known and proclaimed in all cities</i><sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Nehemiah 8:15" href="/Nehemiah.8.15"><i>Neh</i>. 8:15</a>. In the verse, myrtle and branches of the <i>`avot</i> tree are mentioned separately. How can they be identified?</i> etc. <i>Myrtle</i> is not the same as <i>rope tree</i>, but myrtle is for the <i>sukkah</i>, and <i>rope tree</i> for the <i>lulav</i><sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">Since the rope tree was mentioned in the Torah as part of the “four kinds” to be taken on the first day, “myrtle” must refer to a kind not satisfying the criteria specified in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sukkah 3:2:2-3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sukkah.3.2.2-3">Halakhah 2</a> which only may be used for roofing the <i>sukkah</i>. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 12a" href="/Sukkah.12a">Babli 12a</a>.</i>. Rebbi Ze`ira blew it<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">At the time of <i>shofar</i> blowing on New Year’s Day he instructed on the requirement for a qualified myrtle.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">The first sentences of this paragraph are in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Berakhot (Lieberman) 6:10" href="/Tosefta_Berakhot_(Lieberman).6.10">Tosephta <i>Berakhot</i>6:10</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 9:3:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.9.3.3"><i>Yerushalmi Berakhot</i> 9:3, Note 185</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 46a" href="/Sukkah.46a">Babli <i>Sukkah</i> 46a</a>.</i> He who makes a <i>lulav</i> for himself says, praise to Him who sanctified us by His commandments and commanded us to make a <i>lulav</i>. For another, to make a <i>lulav</i> in his name<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sukkah 1:2:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sukkah.1.2.3">Chapter 1, Note 124</a>.</i>. When he takes it he says, praise to Him who sanctified us by His commandments and commanded us about taking the <i>lulav</i>. If he prayed with it he says, praise to Him Who let us live, and kept us, and let us reach this time. He says the benediction every time when he takes it. How does one recite the benediction for the <i>Ḥanukkah</i> light? Rav said, praised to Him who sanctified us by His commandments and commanded us the commandment [to kindle]<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">Corrector’s addition, to be deleted as shown by the quote later in the paragraph. The mention of “kindling” is Babylonian, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 23a" href="/Shabbat.23a">Babli <i>Šabbat</i>23a</a>.</i> the light of <i>Ḥanukkah</i>. Everybody agrees that on the first day he says, about taking the <i>lulav</i>. What do they disagree about? The remaining days. Rebbi Joḥanan said, about taking the <i>lulav</i>. Rebbi Joshua ben Levi said, about commandments of the Elders<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">Since, as explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sukkah 3:11:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sukkah.3.11.1">Mishnah 3:12</a>, the biblical commandment to take the “four kinds” for seven days is restricted to “before the Eternal”, i. e., at the Temple. Without a Temple the obligation at other places is purely rabbinical except for the first day mentioned in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:40" href="/Leviticus.23.40"><i>Lev.</i> 23:40</a>.</i>. What did Rav say for the <i>lulav</i>? Since for <i>Ḥanukkah</i> which is from their words he is saying, about the commandment of the light of <i>Ḥanukkah</i>, for <i>lulav</i> which is from the Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">Everywhere, at least for the first day of the festival.</i> not so much more? What did Rebbi Joshua ben Levi say on <i>Ḥanukkah</i>? Since for <i>lulav</i> which is from the Torah he is saying, about commandments of the Elders, for <i>Ḥanukkah</i> which is from their words not so much more? The only problem is, what did Rebbi Joḥanan say on <i>Ḥanukkah</i>?
Ḥiyya the son of Rav was reciting the benediction every single time<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">Accepted as general practice <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 3:2:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.3.2.5"><i>Berakhot</i> 3:3, Note 131</a>.</i>. Rav Ḥuna recited it only once. Rebbi Ḥuna in the name of Rav Joseph: The reason of Rav Ḥuna. <i>Demay</i><sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">Produce of the Land of which one may be sure that heave was taken but probably not tithes (nor heave of the tithe). Cf Introduction to Tractate <i>Demay</i>.</i> is from their words and the remaining days are from their words. Since for <i>demay</i> one does not recite a benediction<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">When separating heave of the tithe. Rejected by the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 23a" href="/Shabbat.23a">Babli, <i>Šabbat</i>23a</a>.</i>, also for the remaining days one does not recite a benediction.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A robbed or dried up <i>etrog</i><sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">The Persian name of cedrat, <i>citrus medica</i>, identified as “fruit of the Hadar tree”.</i> is disqualified. Of an <i>ashera</i><sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">A tree worshipped as a pagan deity, forbidden for all use.</i> or a seduced city<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">An apostate city practicing idolatry which has to be destroyed and all its property burned, forbidden for all usufruct, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 13:13-18" href="/Deuteronomy.13.13-18"><i>Deut</i>13:13–18</a>.</i>, it is disqualified. Of `<i>orlah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">An edible fruit produced during the first three years of the tree’s planting, forbidden for all usufruct.</i>, it is disqualified. Of impure heave<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">Forbidden as fruit, must be burned.</i>, it is disqualified; of pure one should not take<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">Since by biblical commandment heave and Second Tithe in Jerusalem have to be consumed.</i> but if he took it is qualified. Of <i>demay</i><sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">Produce of the Land of which one may be sure that heave was taken but probably not tithes (nor heave of the tithe). Cf Introduction to Tractate <i>Demay</i>.</i>, the House of Shammai disqualify<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">Since they do not permit <i>demay</i> to be given even to the poor without separating the heave of the tithe, it is not edible at this moment.</i> but the House of Hillel qualify; of Second Tithe in Jerusalem<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">Since by biblical commandment heave and Second Tithe in Jerusalem have to be consumed.</i> one should not take but if he took it is qualified.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> It is written, <i>a fruit of the splendor tree</i>, a tree whose fruit is splendor and its wood is splendor. Which one is that? This is the <i>etrog</i>. If you would say, the pomegranate, its (wood) [fruit] is splendor but its (fruit) [wood] is not splendor<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">It is difficult to decide whether the text of the (scribe) or that of the [corrector] is the correct one. The corrector’s is preferable since (1) it is not parallel to the next sentence and (2) the pomegranate, while not easy to eat, is on the list of preferred fruits of the Holy Land (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 8:8" href="/Deuteronomy.8.8"><i>Deut</i>. 8:8</a>) and certainly tastes much better than carob pods.</i>. If you would say, the carob, its wood is splendor but its fruit is not splendor. Which one is it? The <i>etrog</i>. <i>Hadar</i>; Rebbi Levi said, for it stays on its tree from one year to the next<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">His Hebrew must pronounce ־ָ as /<i>a</i>/ and make no difference between ד nd דּ since he reads הָדָר “splendor” as הַדָּר “who dwells”.</i>. Rebbi Tanḥuma said, Aquila translated הדָר by ‘ύδωρ <sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">“Water.” This again makes no difference between ד and דּ but reads ־ָ either as /<i>o</i>/ or as Polish /<i>ó</i>/.</i>; a tree which grows on the water. “Rebbi Simeon ben Ioḥai stated: <i>You shall take for yourselves . . a fruit of the splendor tree</i>, a tree whose fruit is splendor and its wood is splendor; the smell of its fruit is the smell of its wood, the smell of its wood is the smell of its fruit; its fruit looks like its wood, its wood looks like its fruit. Which one is it? The <i>etrog</i>.”<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 35a" href="/Sukkah.35a">Babli 35a</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Emor, Chapter 16 4" href="/Sifra,_Emor,_Chapter_16.4"><i>Sifra Emor Pereq</i>16(4</a>), with different name traditions.</i>
<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph does not belong here; it is copied from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 3:2:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.3.2.2"><i>Eruvin</i> Chapter 3, Notes 76–78</a>. If the text is read as relevant to the discussion here, the question must be why the use of Second Tithe in Jerusalem, while disapproved of, is accepted by everybody when it should be disqualified by the House of Shammai.</i> Rebbi Jacob the Southerner asked, is our Mishnah not following the House of Shammai? As we have stated, “of <i>demay</i>, the House of Shammai disqualify but the House of Hillel qualify. Of Second Tithe in Jerusalem one should not take but if he took it is valid.”

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If scabs covered most of it<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>etrog</i>.</i>, or its protuberance was removed, or it was perforated, or peeled, or cracked, if anything was missing it is disqualified. If scabs covered a smaller part, or the stem was removed, or it was perforated but nothing was missing, it is qualified. A black <i>etrog</i> is disqualified, and the green like leeks Rebbi Meïr qualifies but Rebbi Jehudah disqualifies.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Isaac bar Naḥman in the name of Samuel: All disqualifications only disqualify on the first day of the holiday<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">When the standards have to be biblical. Quoted by <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosafot on Sukkah 35b:12:1" href="/Tosafot_on_Sukkah.35b.12.1">Tosaphot 35b s. v. עלתה</a>.</i>. There, they are saying, most of it on one side; its protuberance is disqualifying like most of it<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>etrog</i> is disqualified if either on one side most of it is covered by scab, as noted here, or if all around it has infected spots, as noted in the next Halakhah. Therefore if the top was covered by scab it is covered on all sides and therefore disqualified.</i>.
“If its protuberance was removed.” There, they say: its lily. Rebbi Isaac bar Ḥaqula said, the peak<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">Arabic פוק “acme”. The <i>etrog</i> has to beheld so that the protuberance is the top.</i>. If it was perforated but not pierced inside it is qualified, as we have stated there, “if the stem was removed, or it was perforated but nothing was missing, it is qualified.”
“A black <i>etrog</i> is disqualified.” What comes from the black one is qualified<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">The black <i>etrog</i> is infected and probably inedible; its seeds may produce normal trees and normal fruit.</i>.
“The green like leeks.” Rebbi Ze`ira asked before Rebbi Immi: Leek-colored or like leek-colored<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">Somewhat less dark.</i>? He told him, leek-colored. What is the deepest ירק<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 13:49" href="/Leviticus.13.49"><i>Lev.</i> 13:49</a>, cf. <i>Sifra Tazria Pereq</i> 14(2).</i>? Rebbi Eleazar said, like wax<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">Yellow colored. In biblical Hebrew both green and yellow are subsumed under ירק. The word used in modern Hebrew for yellow, צָהוֹב, biblically means “shiny, shiny red.”</i> and like the flower of the <i>qarmal</i> tree; Symmachos says, like a peacock’s wings. What is the deepest red? That is deep crimson. And here, he says so<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">Why is it green for the <i>etrog</i> but yellow for skin disease?</i>? Rebbi Phineas said, there is a difference there because it says, <i>deepest green</i>.

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The measure of a small <i>etrog</i>, Rebbi Meïr says, like a walnut, Rebbi Jehudah says, like an egg<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">A chicken egg.</i>. And a large one, so that he is able to hold the two of them<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>etrog</i> and the <i>lulav</i> together.</i> in one hand, the words of Rebbi Jehudah; Rebbi Yose says, even one in both of his hands.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">The origin of the paragraph is in <i>Ma`serot</i> 1:4, Notes 99–106, <b>ע</b>. A parallel but different discussion is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 36" href="/Sukkah.36">Babli 36a/b</a>.</i> Rebbi Aqiba says an unripe cedrat is not a fruit, but the Sages say it is a fruit. Rebbi Hila, Rebbi Yasa, in the name of Rebbi Eleazar: It follows that Rebbi Simeon<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">In Mishnah <i>Ma`serot</i>1:4 he frees unripe <i>etrog</i> from tithes.</i> follows the argument of his teacher, Rebbi Aqiba. Just as Rebbi Aqiba said it is not a fruit, so Rebbi Simeon said it is not a fruit. Rebbi Yose said, is everything qualified for <i>lulav</i> subject to tithes and everything disqualified for <i>lulav</i> not subject to tithes? Did they not object, there is the spotted one<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>etrog</i> is disqualified if either on one side most of it is covered by scab, as noted here, or if all around it has infected spots, as noted in the next Halakhah. Therefore if the top was covered by scab it is covered on all sides and therefore disqualified.</i>, one that grew in a form<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">Greek τύπος.</i>, or one shaped like a ball, which are disqualified for <i>lulav</i> but subject to tithes! It is reasonable {to hold that} Rebbi Simeon agrees with Rebbi Aqiba but Rebbi Aqiba does not agree with Rebbi Simeon. Rebbi Simeon agrees with Rebbi Aqiba since it is written <i>fruit</i><sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:40" href="/Leviticus.23.40"><i>Lev.</i>23:40</a>.</i> and it is not a fruit. Rebbi Aqiba does not agree with Rebbi Simeon, there is the spotted one, one that grew in a form, or one shaped like a ball, which are disqualified for <i>lulav</i> but subject to tithes.
We have stated, “like a walnut.” There are Tannaim who state, “up to a walnut.” He who says “like a walnut”, as a walnut itself is qualified. He who says “up to a walnut”, as a walnut itself is disqualified.
Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">This possibly might refer to R. Yose the Tanna. The argument that the omission of the connective “and” in the list indicates that the <i>etrog</i> must be kept separate from the other items is ascribed to R. Eleazar (the Tanna) in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Emor, Chapter 15 7" href="/Sifra,_Emor,_Chapter_15.7"><i>Sifra Emor Pereq</i>15(7</a>).</i> said, if there were written <i>and date-palm palms</i>, it would be correct. Only <i>date-palm palms</i> is written, even one in one hand and the other in the other hand. They said about Rebbi Aqiba that he entered the synagogue with an <i>etrog</i> on his shoulders<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 36b" href="/Sukkah.36b">Babli 36b</a>.</i>.

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One ties the <i>lulav</i> only with its own kind<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">One ties myrtle and willow branches to the palm branch only with palm leaves, myrtle or willow branches, in order not to add an unauthorized kind to the 4 authorized ones.</i>, the words of Rebbi Jehudah. Rebbi Meïr says, even with a string<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">In his opinion the tying of myrtle and willow branches to the palm branch is common usage, not biblical commandment, and the string never is counted as another kind.</i>. Rebbi Meïr said, it happened that the people of Jerusalem did tie their <i>lulavim</i> with golden jewels<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">Arabic גִמָאנ “jewels”.</i>. They told him, under these they tied it with its own kind. Where did they move<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">In the synagogue the main service involving the <i>lulav</i> is the recitation of <i>Hallel</i>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 113-118" href="/Psalms.113-118"><i>Pss</i>. 113–118</a>, where the <i>lulav</i> is moved at the recitation or repetition of designated verses.</i>? At <i>thank the Eternal</i><sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 118:1" href="/Psalms.118.1"><i>Ps.</i> 118:1</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 118:29" href="/Psalms.118.29">29</a>.</i> beginning and end, and also at <i>please, Eternal, please save</i><sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 118:25" href="/Psalms.118.25"><i>Ps.</i> 118:25</a>.</i>, following the words of the House of Hillel. The House of Shammai say, also at <i>please, Eternal, please give success</i><sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 118:25" href="/Psalms.118.25"><i>Ps.</i> 118:25</a>.</i>. Rebbi Aqiba said, I was watching Rabban Gamliel and Rebbi Joshua, when all the people were shaking their <i>lulavim</i> but they moved them only at <i>please, Eternal, please save</i>.<br>If somebody returns from a trip where he had no <i>lulav</i>, when he enters his house he can take it at his table<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">And make the benediction (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sukkah 3:4:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sukkah.3.4.4">Note 67</a>).</i>. If he did not take it in the morning he may take it in the afternoon since the entire day is qualified for the <i>lulav</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Therefore not at <i>thank</i><sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">Does R. Aqiba imply that Rabban Gamliel and R. Joshua did not move their <i>lulav</i> at the beginning and the end of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 118" href="/Psalms.118"><i>Ps.</i> 118</a>, which is accepted by all traditions and was never in doubt? The answer is that he only excludes action following the House of Shammai.</i>? To exclude also at <i>please, Eternal, please give success</i>.
Rav Ḥiyya bar Ashi in the name of Rav: If one rises early to go on a trip, he takes a <i>lulav</i> and moves it, or the <i>shofar</i> and blows it<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Berakhot 30a" href="/Berakhot.30a">Babli <i>Berakhot</i>30a</a>.</i>. When the time arrives to read the <i>shema`</i><sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">At dawn.</i>, he reads and prays. It was stated, he was to move three times. Rebbi Ze`ira asked, so one and so one, or so and so one one<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">The movement of the <i>lulav</i> is to move it away and return it near one’s body. The question is whether this counts as one or as two motions.</i>? There, we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Niddah</i>9:7. In a test of a stain whether it is from blood or not one has to move each chemical three times over the stain. The same question arises, are forth and back counted as two or one motions? <a class="refLink" data-ref="Niddah 63a" href="/Niddah.63a">Babli <i>Niddah</i>63a</a>; also <a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 76a" href="/Menachot.76a"><i>Menaḥot</i>76a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 119b" href="/Bava_Kamma.119b"><i>Bava qamma</i>119b</a>, in the name of R. Ze`ira’s student R. Jeremiah.</i>: “He has to rub each kind three times.” Rebbi Ze`ira asked, so one and so one, or so and so one?

Halakhah 9

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A person to whom a slave, or a woman, or a minor, read for him, repeats after them what they say, and it should be a curse for him. If a male adult reads for him, he answers <i>halleluja</i> after him<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">An illiterate who does not know the <i>Hallel</i> by heart, must have the psalms recited to him to make the movements prescribed in Mishnah 8. If a person who is not obligated to recite the Hallel and to move the <i>lulav</i>(a slave, woman, or minor) recites for him, he does not fulfill his duty by listening to them; he must repeat word for word. He is cursed for being an adult illiterate. If an obligated person reads for him, he is not different from people listening to the reader in the synagogue and answers <i>halleluja</i>123 times (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shabbat 16:1:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shabbat.16.1.7"><i>Šabbat</i> Chapter 16, Note 59</a>).</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">This Halakhah is copied from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 3:3:11" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.3.3.11"><i>Berakhot</i>3:3, Notes 153–157</a>, <b>ב</b>. It also is copied in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Rosh Hashanah 3:10:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Rosh_Hashanah.3.10.2"><i>Roš Haššanah</i>3:10</a>, <b>ר</b>.</i> It is stated: <sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 38a" href="/Sukkah.38a">Babli 38a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Berakhot 20b" href="/Berakhot.20b"><i>Berakhot</i>20b</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Berakhot (Lieberman) 5:11" href="/Tosefta_Berakhot_(Lieberman).5.11">Tosephta <i>Berakhot</i>5:11</a>.</i>“In truth, they said a woman may say Grace for her husband, a slave for his master, and a minor for his father. One understands a woman for her husband, a slave for his master<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">Since they are obligated to say Grace after a meal. The Yerushalmi has no problem with women or slaves but for the Babli the question remains whether women may in fact recite Grace for males who have an obligation from the Torah, i. e., who actually ate their fill.</i>. A minor for his father? Did not Rebbi Aḥa say in the name of Rebbi Yose bar Nahora: All they said about a minor is for the latter's education<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">The minor must be educated; there is an obligation on the father to teach him all religious obligations but the minor himself is not obligated and therefore cannot perform any religious duty for others.</i>? Explain it that he recites after him, as we stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">Verses <i>Ps.</i> 118:21–29 are customarily recited twice; either everybody recites them twice or the reader sings them first and the congregation repeats after him; cf. the author’s <i>The Scholar’s Haggadah</i>, p.368. In the Yemenite rite, also the starting clauses of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 114" href="/Psalms.114"><i>Pss</i>. 114</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 116" href="/Psalms.116">116</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 117" href="/Psalms.117">117</a>, 118 are repeated by the congregation.</i>: “A person to whom a slave, or a woman, or a minor, read for him, repeats after them what they say, and it should be a curse for him.” [Also they said,] there should be a curse on the man of twenty years who needs the child of ten.

Halakhah 10

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> At a place where there is the custom to repeat, one repeats<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">Verses <i>Ps.</i> 118:21–29 are customarily recited twice; either everybody recites them twice or the reader sings them first and the congregation repeats after him; cf. the author’s <i>The Scholar’s Haggadah</i>, p.368. In the Yemenite rite, also the starting clauses of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 114" href="/Psalms.114"><i>Pss</i>. 114</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 116" href="/Psalms.116">116</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 117" href="/Psalms.117">117</a>, 118 are repeated by the congregation.</i>, to recite a blessing afterwards, one recites a blessing afterwards<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">At the conclusion of the recitation of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 113-118" href="/Psalms.113-118"><i>Pss.</i>113–118</a>.</i>; everything follows local custom. If one buys a <i>lulav</i> from another in the Sabbatical year he has to give him the <i>etrog</i> as a gift since one is not permitted to buy it in the Sabbatical year<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">Edible fruits of the Land in the Sabbatical year are not the private property of the owner of the land; they cannot be objects of trade. But the branches of palm, myrtle, and willow are not edible and therefore can be objects of trade. In the Sabbatical year the <i>etrog</i> may not be sold separately; one sells the 4 kinds together and declares that payment is taken only for the three inedible kinds.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">The source of the text for this and the next paragraphs is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Megillah 1:9:2-20" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Megillah.1.9.2-20"><i>Megillah</i>1:11</a> (fol. 72a, מ). Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 117a" href="/Pesachim.117a">Babli <i>Pesaḥim</i>117a</a>.</i> Rav and Samuel. One said <i>hallelu yah</i>, and the other one said <i>hallēluja</i>. For him who said <i>hallelu yah</i> it is split but cannot be erased<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">Since the name of God may not be erased (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 12:4" href="/Deuteronomy.12.4"><i>Deut</i>. 12:4</a>, referring to vv. 2,3.)</i>. For him who said <i>hallelujah</i> it may be erased but cannot be split<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">Biblical spelling does not admit splitting of words.</i>. We do not know who said what. Since Rav said, I heard from my uncle<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">R. Ḥiyya the elder.</i>, if somebody would give me a book of Psalms written by Rebbi Meïr I could erase all <i>hallelujah</i> in it since he did not intend to sanctify them. This implies that he said <i>hallelujah</i>. The words of the rabbis disagree, as Rebbi Simon said in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi, the book of Psalms was said with ten expressions of praise, with “hail”<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">Psalms starting with אֲשְׁרֵי.</i>, with “excellence”<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">Psalms starting with לַמְנַצֵּחַ.</i>, with melody, with song, with chant, with instruction,<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">Psalms starting with מַשְׂכִּיל.</i> with shout, with thanksgiving, with prayer, with blessing. The most beatific of all of them is <i>hallelujah</i> since both the Name and praise are contained in it<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">Therefore <i>hallelujah</i> cannot be erased since it contains the Name.</i>.
Rebbi Ze`ira asked before Rebbi Abbahu: He told him, (as it comes in the mouth before you) [Rebbi Abba is on top before you.]<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">The scribe’s text, supported by the text in <i>Megillah</i>, is the only one which makes sense. One may answer either <i>hallelu yah</i> or <i>hallelujah</i>.</i> Rebbi Jonah answered both ways. Rebbi Eliezer<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">With the text in <i>Megillah</i> read: Eleazar.</i> did not answer any way<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">Here is a sentence missing, which is found in the origin in <i>Megillah:</i>“As it was stated: if he heard but did not answer he fulfilled his obligation, if he answered but did not hear he did not fulfill his obligation.”</i>. Rav in the name of Rav Abba bar Ḥana <sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">Again a clause missing from the parallel sources: {Some say, Rav Abba bar Ḥama} in the name of Rav. Both statements are possible; the authors were cousins. Here starts a parallel in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 8:8:4-7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.8.8.4-7"><i>Berakhot</i>8:9, Notes 177–182</a>,<b>ב</b>. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 38b" href="/Sukkah.38b">Babli 38b</a>.</i> in the name of Rav: Only if he answered at the start of the Chapters. Rebbi Ze`ira asked, what are the starts of Chapters? <i>Hallelujah, praise, servants of the Eternal, praise the Name of the Eternal</i><sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 113:1" href="/Psalms.113.1"><i>Ps.</i> 113:1</a>. In Yemenite prayer books the instructions for the recitation are, with the responses added in brackets: <i>Hallelujah [hallelujah], praise, servants of the Eternal, [hallelujah], praise the Name of the Eternal [hallelujah]</i>. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 38b" href="/Sukkah.38b">Babli 38b</a>.</i>. They asked before Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba: From where that he who heard but did not answer has fulfilled his obligation? He told them, since we are seeing great rabbis standing among the public when these say <i>praised be he who</i> comes and the others say <i>in the name of the Eternal</i><sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 118:26" href="/Psalms.118.26"><i>Ps.</i> 118:26</a>. Approved in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 38b" href="/Sukkah.38b">Babli 38b</a>. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 8:8:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.8.8.4">Yerushalmi <i>Berakhot</i>8:9 Note 178</a>.</i>, and both have fulfilled their obligation.
<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that the original of this paragraph is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 8:8:2-7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.8.8.2-7"><i>Berakhot</i>8:9</a>.</i> Rebbi Hoshaya stated: One answers Amen even if he did not eat<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">A person hearing other people reciting Grace has to say Amen; obviously he cannot participate in reciting part of the text; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 7:2:2-7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.7.2.2-7"><i>Berakhot</i>7:2</a>.</i>, but he may not say: “Let us praise Him, of Whose bounty we ate,” except if he did eat. It is stated<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Megillah (Lieberman) 3:27" href="/Tosefta_Megillah_(Lieberman).3.27">Tosephta <i>Megillah</i>3:27</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Berakhot 47a" href="/Berakhot.47a">Babli <i>Berakhot</i> 47a</a>.</i>: “One answers neither an orphan Amen, nor a plucked<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">Amen not completely pronounced.<br>The other sources have here an addition: “Ben Azai said, if one answered an orphan Amen, may his children be orphans; hurried, may the end of his days be hurried; plucked, may his soul be plucked off. For a long Amen, they lengthen his days and years in a good way.”</i> Amen. What is an orphan Amen? Rebbi Huna said, that is one who is obligated to recite a blessing, he answers Amen, and is not conscious for what he answered Amen. It is stated: One answers Amen after a Gentile who recited a benediction for the Eternal, but if he recited a benediction with the Name one does not answer<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Gentile is not subject to our rules, if he praises the Eternal, one answers after him Amen. But if he uses a Jewish formula one may not answer after him Amen unless one has ascertained that he conformed with our rules (S. Liebermann.)</i>. Rebbi Tanḥuma said, if a Gentile blesses you, answer after him Amen since it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 7:14" href="/Deuteronomy.7.14"><i>Deut</i>. 7:14</a>.</i> <i>You shall be blessed by all peoples</i>. A Gentile met Rebbi Ismael and blessed him. He answered: The word about you has already been said. He met a second one who cursed him; he answered: The word about you has already been said. His students said to him: Rebbi, did you say to the one what you said to the other? He said to them, so it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 27:29" href="/Genesis.27.29"><i>Gen</i>. 27:29</a>.</i> <i>Those who curse you are cursed, but those who bless you are blessed</i>.
Rebbi used to repeat things in it; Rebbi Eleazar ben Proteus recited simply in it<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">In Mishnaic times the order of recital of Hallel was not uniform; uniformity was not intended.</i>.
Rebbi Eleazar said, this follows the Elders of Galilea, for the Elders of Galilea say, it is forbidden to hand over food for two meals to a person suspect in matters of Sabbatical<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the last part of the Mishnah, where one has to be careful in a Sabbatical year not to buy the edible <i>etrog</i> from a person trading in Sabbatical produce. The Elders of Galilee do not permit to deal with such a person except for the absolute necessities of sustenance.</i>. Rebbi Mattaniah said, explain it as opinion of everybody, in case that <i>etrogim</i> are sold at an elevated price<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">In this case there are great incentives to trade in <i>etrogim</i> in the Sabbatical year. The only way not to “put a stone before a blind man”, in this case to prevent the non-observant from trading in <i>etrogim</i>, is to eliminate all considerations of money from the transfer of possession of the <i>etrog</i>.</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 2:3:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.2.3.4"><i>Gittin</i>2:3, Note 68</a>.</i> As the following: The cedrats<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">Persian <i>turung</i>, Farsi תרנגִ <i>toranj</i>, used for all citrus fruits.</i> were scarce there when Rav Naḥman bar Jacob gave an <i>etrog</i> as gift to his son and told him, if you have acquired it and fulfilled its obligation, return it to me<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote">This proves that the requirement that the <i>etrog</i> must be the personal property of the user may be satisfied even by a gift given on condition that it be returned; therefore certainly a permanent gift as described in the Mishnah.</i>.

Halakhah 11

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Originally the <i>lulav</i> was taken in the Temple for seven {days} and in the country<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote">Anywhere in the world outside of the Temple Mount.</i> one day. After the destruction of the Temple, Rabban Joḥanan ben Zakkai instituted that the <i>lulav</i> be taken in the country for seven {days} as remembrance of the Temple, and that the day of waving be entirely forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote">New flour is permitted for consumption by the `<i>omer</i> sacrifice of newly cut barley, by pharisaic tradition on the 16<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> of Nisan; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:14" href="/Leviticus.23.14"><i>Lev.</i> 23:14</a>. In the Temple the sacrifice was brought early in the morning; therefore new grain was permitted on that day. Rabban Joḥanan ben Zakkai instituted that new grain should not be used until it automatically became permitted on the 17<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup>.</i>.<br>If the first day of Tabernacles is a Sabbath, everybody brings their <i>lulav</i> to the synagogue<sup class="footnote-marker">151</sup><i class="footnote">On Friday afternoon since the <i>lulav</i> may not be carried in the public domain on the Sabbath. As <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sukkah 4:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sukkah.4.2.1">Mishnah 4:3</a> shows, this refers to any place outside the Temple Mount, where the obligation to take the <i>lulav</i> is biblical only on the first day.</i>. The next day they come in the morning and everybody recognizes his own and takes it, since they said that nobody can fulfill his obligation with another person’s <i>lulav</i> on the first day of Tabernacles; but on the other days of the holiday a person can fulfill his obligation with another person’s <i>lulav</i><sup class="footnote-marker">152</sup><i class="footnote">Since the verse says: <i>You shall take for yourselves on the First Day</i>, implying it need not be yours on any other day.</i>. Rebbi Yose says, if somebody forgot and took the <i>lulav</i> into the public domain on the first day of Tabernacles which is a Sabbath he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">160</sup><i class="footnote">He is not liable for a purification sacrifice even though bringing an object from the private into the public domain is a Sabbath violation (Mishnah Šabbat 1:1).</i> since he took it out with permission<sup class="footnote-marker">161</sup><i class="footnote">Not that he has permission to violate the Sabbath, but to take up the <i>lulav</i> which would be <i>muqṣeh</i> if the Sabbath were on another day of the holiday week.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> It is written<sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:40" href="/Leviticus.23.40"><i>Lev.</i> 23:40</a>, the verse about the <i>lulav</i> and the 4 kinds.</i>: <i>and you shall enjoy before the Eternal, your God, for seven days</i>. There are Tannaim who state, the verse speaks of the enjoyment of well-being sacrifices<sup class="footnote-marker">147</sup><i class="footnote">The general exhortation to enjoy the holidays (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 16:11" href="/Deuteronomy.16.11"><i>Deut</i>. 16:11</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 16:15" href="/Deuteronomy.16.15">15</a>) is read as referring to the joyous family meals connected with the holiday pilgrimage to the Temple. This interpretation would read the verse as duplicate of the same exhortation in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 16:15" href="/Deuteronomy.16.15"><i>Deut</i>. 16:15</a>.</i>. There are Tannaim who state, the verse speaks of the enjoyment of the <i>lulav</i><sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote">This interpretation reads <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:40" href="/Leviticus.23.40"><i>Lev.</i> 23:40</a> separate from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 16:15" href="/Deuteronomy.16.15"><i>Deut</i>. 16:15</a>, commanding a <i>lulav</i> festivity in the Temple, “<i>before the Eternal</i>”, in addition to the requirement of taking the <i>lulav</i> on the first day, independent of location. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 43a" href="/Sukkah.43a">Babli 43a</a>.</i>. For him who is saying, the verse speaks of the enjoyment of well-being sacrifices, the first day is from a word of the Torah and the other days are from a word of the Torah, and Rabban Joḥanan ben Zakkai instituted on basis of a word from the Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">149</sup><i class="footnote">It is obvious that instead of “well-being sacrifices” one has to read “<i>lulav</i>”. Since in this case there is a biblical requirement to take the <i>lulav</i> for seven days in the Temple, taking the <i>lulav</i> for seven days has biblical roots, and the statement about Rabban Joḥanan ben Zakkai makes sense.</i>. For him who is saying, the verse speaks of the enjoyment of <i>lulav</i>, the first day is from a word of the Torah and the other days are from their words, and Rabban Joḥanan ben Zakkai instituted on basis of their words. But is there institution after institution<sup class="footnote-marker">150</sup><i class="footnote">Here one has to read “well-being sacrifices”. If the verse does not force a 7-day observation of <i>lulav</i> anywhere, Rabban Joḥanan ben Zakkai would have extended a purely customary observation from the Temple to the outside world. Since rabbinic extensions of Biblical prescriptions are legitimate only as “fences around the law”, extending any of these is unnecessary. Therefore Rabban Joḥanan ben Zakkai must have held that <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:40" href="/Leviticus.23.40"><i>Lev.</i> 23:40</a> refers to <i>lulav</i>(<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sukkah 3:11:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sukkah.3.11.2">Note 149</a>). <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 11a" href="/Shabbat.11a">Babli <i>Šabbat</i>11a</a>.</i>?
The colleagues asked before Rebbi Jonah: Since you are saying there, <i>and you shall offer a gift to the Eternal seven days</i><sup class="footnote-marker">153</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:8" href="/Leviticus.23.8"><i>Lev.</i> 23:8</a>.</i>, there are no seven without a Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">154</sup><i class="footnote">In the detailed list of the holiday sacrifices, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 28-29" href="/Numbers.28-29"><i>Num</i>. 28–29</a>, it is written in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 28:24" href="/Numbers.28.24">v. 28:24</a> that on the Holiday of unleavened bread there have to be sacrifices for seven days. Therefore the mention of the seven days of sacrifices in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:8" href="/Leviticus.23.8"><i>Lev.</i> 23:8</a>, in the holiday list whose emphasis is not on the sacrifices, can be read as emphasizing that these sacrifices have to be offered also on the Sabbath, the Sabbath prohibition being not applicable in this regard.</i>, why not similarly <i>and you shall enjoy before the Eternal, your God, for seven days</i>, there are no seven without a Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">155</sup><i class="footnote">By the argument of the preceding note, it should follow that the Sabbath prohibitions are nonexistent for taking the <i>lulav</i> on a Sabbath which is the first day of the holiday, since it was established in the preceding paragraph that the second part of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:40" href="/Leviticus.23.40"><i>Lev.</i> 23:40</a> also refers to <i>lulav</i>.</i>? He answered them, there is a difference, for it is written <i>You shall take for yourselves on the First Day</i>, He separated the first from them<sup class="footnote-marker">156</sup><i class="footnote">Since there is no direct evidence that the second part of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:40" href="/Leviticus.23.40"><i>Lev.</i> 23:40</a> must refer to <i>lulav</i>.</i>. Then it should push aside in the Temple, it should not push aside in the country<sup class="footnote-marker">157</sup><i class="footnote">Since both <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:8" href="/Leviticus.23.8"><i>Lev.</i>23:8</a> and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 28:24" href="/Numbers.28.24"><i>Num</i>. 28:24</a> refer to the Temple service, the argument should be transferable to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:40" href="/Leviticus.23.40"><i>Lev.</i> 23:40</a>.</i>. Rebbi Jonah said, if it had said, “you shall take before the Eternal, your God,” I would have said, here he excluded and at another place included<sup class="footnote-marker">158</sup><i class="footnote">It would be reasonable to restrict the rules of <i>lulav</i> to the Temple.</i>. But <i>you shall take for yourselves</i> everywhere, <i>and you shall enjoy before the Eternal, your God, for seven days</i>, in Jerusalem<sup class="footnote-marker">159</sup><i class="footnote">Since the first part of 23:40 is addressed to everybody everywhere, it excludes differentiating between Temple and outside for the rules of the first day. Since the rules “before the Eternal” refer either to the Temple, as in matters of sacrifices, or to the place of the Temple, as in the rules of Second Tithe (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 14:26" href="/Deuteronomy.14.26"><i>Deut</i>. 14:26</a>); by the previous argument this cannot apply to lifting the Sabbath rules on the first day, and, therefore, not to any other day.</i>.
The colleagues say, the words of Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">166</sup><i class="footnote">The Tanna.</i> are that a positive commandment pushes aside a prohibition<sup class="footnote-marker">167</sup><i class="footnote">The obligation to take the <i>lulav</i> has precedence over the prohibition of work on the Sabbath.</i>. Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">168</sup><i class="footnote">The Amora.</i> told them, it is not from this<sup class="footnote-marker">169</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Sabbath involves both a prohibition of work and an obligation of rest, the argument of the colleagues is not applicable.</i> but as Rebbi Ila said and as is stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">170</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 41b" href="/Sukkah.41b">Babli 41b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sukkah (Lieberman) 2:10" href="/Tosefta_Sukkah_(Lieberman).2.10">Tosephta 2:10</a>.</i>: “This was the usage in Jerusalem, a person goes to the synagogue with his <i>lulav</i> in his hand, goes to visit the sick with his <i>lulav</i> in his hand. he recites the <i>shema`</i> and prays with his <i>lulav</i> in his hand. To lift his hands<sup class="footnote-marker">171</sup><i class="footnote">A Cohen to pronounce the Priestly Blessing.</i> or to read in the Torah, he hands it to another person<sup class="footnote-marker">172</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babylonian sources (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sukkah 3:11:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sukkah.3.11.4">Note 170</a>): “Puts it down on the floor.”</i>. If he put it down on the floor it is forbidden to move it<sup class="footnote-marker">173</sup><i class="footnote">In the Tosephta: Once he has completed his obligation he is forbidden to move it. In both versions of the <i>baraita</i>, if a person takes up the <i>lulav</i> and takes it outside when he does not need it any more on that day, he is liable for a Sabbath violation also for R. Yose.</i>.” Rebbi Abun said, this implies that it is forbidden for usufruct. It was stated: One moves fragrant wood<sup class="footnote-marker">174</sup><i class="footnote">Which is neither food nor implement and therefore not anything for which a general permission is given to be moved on the Sabbath.</i> to let a sick person smell it on the Sabbath. The rabbis of Caesarea are saying, one may wave the myrtle branch for a sick person on the Sabbath. <sup class="footnote-marker">175</sup><i class="footnote">The text from here to the end of the paragraph is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shabbat 19:4:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shabbat.19.4.3"><i>Šabbat</i>19, Notes 140–141</a>; also <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 6:5:3-6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.6.5.3-6"><i>Pesaḥim</i>6:7</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 8:1:18" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.8.1.18"><i>Yebamot</i> 8:1 Notes 106–107</a>.</i> Does the same hold for a knife for circumcision, the same for unleavened bread? Since Rebbi Joḥanan said, the words of Rebbi Yose, even if he finished he may return for fibers which do not invalidate the circumcision; this implies the same even for a knife for circumcision, the same for unleavened bread.

Halakhah 12

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A woman may receive<sup class="footnote-marker">162</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>lulav</i> when the men have performed the prescribed motions, since if it is not <i>muqṣeh</i> for one person it cannot be <i>muqṣeh</i> for another.</i> from her son’s hand or her husband’s hand and return it to the water on the Sabbath. Rebbi Jehudah says, on the Sabbath one returns, on the holiday one adds, on the intermediate days one exchanges<sup class="footnote-marker">163</sup><i class="footnote">The water which keeps <i>lulav</i> and twigs fresh.</i>. A minor who knows how to move is obligated<sup class="footnote-marker">164</sup><i class="footnote">The obligation is not the minor’s but his father’s; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sukkah 3:9:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sukkah.3.9.2">Note 113</a>.</i> for the <i>lulav</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> If he knows how to move<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">In the synagogue the main service involving the <i>lulav</i> is the recitation of <i>Hallel</i>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 113-118" href="/Psalms.113-118"><i>Pss</i>. 113–118</a>, where the <i>lulav</i> is moved at the recitation or repetition of designated verses.</i> he is obligated for <i>lulav</i>. If he knows how to dress, he is obligated for <i>ṣiṣit</i><sup class="footnote-marker">176</sup><i class="footnote">To have the tassels at the four corners of his toga. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 42a" href="/Sukkah.42a">Babli 42a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Arakhin 2b" href="/Arakhin.2b"><i>Arakhin</i>2b</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Chagigah (Lieberman) 1:2" href="/Tosefta_Chagigah_(Lieberman).1.2">Tosephta <i>Ḥagigah</i>1:2</a>.</i>. If he knows how to talk, his father teaches him the language of the Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">177</sup><i class="footnote">Biblical Hebrew.</i>. If he knows how to keep his hands clean, one eats heave on his hands<sup class="footnote-marker">178</sup><i class="footnote">If he washes his hands and then touched heave, the latter is not impaired by his touch and may be eaten by pure Cohanim.</i>. If he knows how to keep his body clean, one eats food in purity on his body<sup class="footnote-marker">179</sup><i class="footnote">If he knows what brings impurity and states that he is pure, the touch of his body does not contaminate food prepared in purity.</i>. But he does not go before the Ark<sup class="footnote-marker">180</sup><i class="footnote">As an appointed reader to lead the congregation in prayer.</i>, nor lift his hands<sup class="footnote-marker">171</sup><i class="footnote">A Cohen to pronounce the Priestly Blessing.</i>, nor stands on the podium<sup class="footnote-marker">181</sup><i class="footnote">A Levite singing in the Temple.</i>, before he grows a beard. Rebbi says, all of them at the age of twenty years<sup class="footnote-marker">182</sup><i class="footnote">Irrespective of whether he grew a beard or not.</i>, as it is said<sup class="footnote-marker">183</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Ezra 3:8" href="/Ezra.3.8"><i>Ezra</i>3:8</a>. The age limit was established for the second Temple, therefore it is valid for the future. The pentateuchal age limit for Levites was 25 years; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 8:24" href="/Numbers.8.24"><i>Num</i>. 8:24</a>.</i>: <i>They appointed the Levites, twenty years or older, to direct the work of the Eternal’s House</i>.

Chapter 4



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> <i>Lulav</i> and willow six and seven<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The entire Chapter only refers to the service in the Temple. The willow twigs referred to here are not those tied to the <i>lulav</i>; these are subsumed under the name of <i>lulav</i> and are not mentioned further, just as the myrtle branches are not mentioned. The willow twigs are large ones used around the altar, as described in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sukkah 4:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sukkah.4.3.1">Mishnah 4</a>. The numbers mentioned refer to the number of days the corresponding action is required. If the Sabbath of the holiday week was not the first day, the willow twigs to surround the altar were not brought on the Sabbath.</i>; <i>Hallel</i> and joy eight<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">These apply both on the seven days of Tabernacles and on the additional final holiday (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 29:35" href="/Numbers.29.35"><i>Num</i>. 29:35</a>) which is separate from Tabernacles.</i>. <i>Sukkah</i> and water libation seven<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Since no Sabbath violation is possibly connected with these, they are obligatory all seven days of Tabernacles.</i>, and the fife five and six<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The fife accompanying the nightly festivities of drawing the water for next day’s libation, impossible both on the full holiday and on the Sabbath.</i>. When is the <i>lulav</i> seven? If the first day of Tabernacles falls on a Sabbath, the <i>lulav is</i> taken seven times; on all other days six<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">If the first day of the holiday is a Sabbath, the use of the <i>lulav</i> in the Temple is prescribed by <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:40" href="/Leviticus.23.40"><i>Lev.</i> 23:40</a> and therefore supersedes any possible Sabbath violation. But the Sabbath on any other day of the holiday week has precedence and prevents the taking of the <i>lulav</i>. Similarly, the willows to surround the altar were carried on the seventh day even if it was a Sabbath since one holds that on that day it is a biblical command, as explained in the Halakhah.</i>.<br>How the willow seven? If the seventh day of willow falls on a Sabbath, the willow is seven {days}, on all other days six<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">If the first day of the holiday is a Sabbath, the use of the <i>lulav</i> in the Temple is prescribed by <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:40" href="/Leviticus.23.40"><i>Lev.</i> 23:40</a> and therefore supersedes any possible Sabbath violation. But the Sabbath on any other day of the holiday week has precedence and prevents the taking of the <i>lulav</i>. Similarly, the willows to surround the altar were carried on the seventh day even if it was a Sabbath since one holds that on that day it is a biblical command, as explained in the Halakhah.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “<i>Lulav</i> and willow six and seven,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is copied in <i>Ševi`it</i>1:7 (Notes 48–58, <b>שׁ</b>).</i> Rebbi Ze`ira, Rebbi Ila, Rebbi Yasa in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: The “willow”<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The entire Chapter only refers to the service in the Temple. The willow twigs referred to here are not those tied to the <i>lulav</i>; these are subsumed under the name of <i>lulav</i> and are not mentioned further, just as the myrtle branches are not mentioned. The willow twigs are large ones used around the altar, as described in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sukkah 4:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sukkah.4.3.1">Mishnah 4</a>. The numbers mentioned refer to the number of days the corresponding action is required. If the Sabbath of the holiday week was not the first day, the willow twigs to surround the altar were not brought on the Sabbath.</i> is practice going back to Moses on Mount Sinai. This is against Abba Shaul<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sukkah 3:3:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sukkah.3.3.2">Halakhah 3:3</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 34a" href="/Sukkah.34a">Babli 34a</a>.</i>, since Abba Shaul said, the willow is a word of the Torah: <i>and brook willows</i>, two. One willow for the <i>lulav</i>, the other willow for the Temple. Rebbi Abba, Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: willow and pouring water are practice going back to Moses on Mount Sinai<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 34a" href="/Sukkah.34a">Babli 34a</a>.</i>. This is against Rebbi Aqiba<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Sifry Num</i>. 250 the argument is given in the name of R. Jehudah ben Bathyra, with different arguments ascribed to R. Aqiba and R. Nathan. The argument notes that in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 29" href="/Numbers.29"><i>Num</i>. 29</a> the sacrifices for the different days of Tabernacles are given in stereotyped language; the words noted are deviations from the usual ונסכה, כמשפט. R. Aqiba’s argument is presented differently in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Zevachim 110b" href="/Zevachim.110b">Babli, <i>Zevaḥim</i> 110b</a>.</i>, since Rebbi Aqiba said the pouring of water is a word of the Torah: On the second day <i>and their libations</i><sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 29:19" href="/Numbers.29.19"><i>Num</i>. 29:19</a>.</i>. On the sixth say, <i>and its libations</i><sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 29:31" href="/Numbers.29.31"><i>Num</i>. 29:31</a>.</i>. On the seventh day, <i>and its rules</i><sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 29:33" href="/Numbers.29.33"><i>Num</i>. 29:33</a>.</i>. מ י מ spells “water”. Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba asked before Rebbi Joḥanan, why does one now plough because of old trees13? He said to him, when the practice was established it was given so that when they desired to plough they might plough. Rebbi Abba bar Zavda in the name of Rebbi Onias from Hauran: Willow, water libation, and ten saplings<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">The rule (Mishnah <i>Ševi`it</i>1:7) that ten tree saplings planted over a surface area of a <i>bet seah</i>(5’000 square cubits) may be tended up to New Year’s Day.</i> are institution of the prophets<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 44a" href="/Sukkah.44a">Babli 44a</a>; <i>Moed qaṭan</i>3b.</i>. Do they disagree? Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun in the name of Levi: That was the current practice; they forgot it, but the later ones got up and agreed to the opinion of the earlier ones to teach you that everything the court insists on will come to be in the end just as Moses was told on Sinai, as Rebbi Mana said, <i>for it is not an empty word</i><sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 32:47" href="/Deuteronomy.32.47"><i>Deut</i>. 32:47</a>.</i>, if it is empty it is <i>from you</i>. Why? Because you do not exert yourselves. <i>Because it is your life;</i> when is it your life? At the time that you exert yourselves. Rebbi Joḥanan said to Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba: Babylonian, two things came from you<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">They are Babylonian customs introduced into Palestine.</i>, prostrating oneself on the fast day<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Prostrating oneself in the re-enactment of the High Priest’s service on the Day of Atonement (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Avodah Zarah 4:1:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Avodah_Zarah.4.1.5"><i>Avodah zarah</i>4:1, Note 18</a>).</i>, and the willow of the seventh day<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Using separate willow twigs on the seventh day of Tabernacles in the prayers for winter rains. Confirmed in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 44a" href="/Sukkah.44a">Babli, 44a</a>.</i>. The rabbis of Caesarea say, also the moving<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">According to J. Levy, the root is קזז “to push aside, to move”. It refers to the calendar rule given by R. Simon in the following. It is established in Mishnah <i>Roš Haššanah</i> 2:11–12 that rabbinic Judaism permits a computed calendar which may deviate from the exact astronomical data. This is used to make sure that the Day of Atonement be neither Friday nor Sunday, since in those cases it would be difficult either to prepare a meal for breaking the fast or one to prepare for the fast. The calendar rules allow for one more condition. One prefers that New Year’s day not be on a Sunday, to make sure that the seventh day of Tabernacles not be on the Sabbath, rather than New Year’s Day (and the first day of Tabernacles) not be on the Sabbath, since outside the Temple the <i>shofar</i> may still be blown on the Second Day of the New Year.</i>.
Rebbi Simon ordered those who computed<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">The calendar.</i>, be careful not to put the <i>shofar</i> blowing on the Sabbath nor the willow on the Sabbath. And if you are in trouble, put the <i>shofar</i> blowing on the Sabbath but do not put the willow on the Sabbath.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> How is the order of the <i>lulav</i><sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">The way the <i>lulav</i> was taken in the Temple if the first day of Tabernacles was a Sabbath. Then the <i>lulav</i> could be moved anywhere on the Temple Mount, but in the city of Jerusalem it could not be brought from a private into the public domain. Therefore it was necessary to bring the <i>lulavim</i> to the Temple Mount on Friday.</i>? Everybody brings their <i>lulav</i> to the Temple Mount; the beadles accept from them and arrange them on the roof of the stoa<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">The double stoa enclosing the Temple Mount.</i>; but the Elders deposit theirs in the lodge<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">People who could not be expected to hold their own in the throng of the next morning.</i>.
