One teaches them<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Since on the first day nobody can fulfill his obligation with a <i>lulav</i> which is not his own property, and nobody could expect to receive his own <i>lulav</i> on the next day, it was necessary that each single worshipper donate his own <i>lulav</i> to whomever it would reach.</i> to say, it should be a gift to anybody into whose hands my <i>lulav</i> will come. They come early and the beadles throw them before them<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>lulavim</i> before the multitude.</i>, and they grab and hit one another. When the Court saw that it involves danger they instituted that each person should take it in his house.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Jacob the Southerner asked, does the Mishnah contradict Rebbi Dosa? For “Rebbi Dosa said, in the morning a person has to say, everything which the poor will collect today between the sheaves is ownerless property. Rebbi Jehudah says, in the evening. But the Sages say, a declaration of abandonment under duress is not an abandonment, because we are not responsible for cheaters.<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Peah (Lieberman) 2:5" href="/Tosefta_Peah_(Lieberman).2.5">Tosephta <i>Peah</i> 2:5</a>, Yerushalmi <i>Ma`aser Šeni</i> 5:1 (Notes 31–37), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 69" href="/Bava_Kamma.69">Babli <i>Bava qamma</i> 69a/b</a>. R. Dosa and R. Jehudah want to prevent the poor from sinning if they are collecting on the farmer’s field more than they are entitled to; therefore they recommend that the farmer declare anything the poor take against the biblical rules as abandoned. The Mishnah here does not contradict R. Dosa, but the Sages who disagree with R. Dosa. Therefore, “contradict R. Dosa” must be read as “contradict the <i>baraita</i> known as R. Dosa’s.”</i>” There you are saying, a declaration of abandonment under duress is not an abandonment, but here you are saying, a declaration of abandonment under duress is abandonment. Rebbi Eleazar said, there he declares ownerless by his own will, here he declares ownerless against his own will. Rebbi Ḥananiah the son of Rebbi Hillel said, this is correct. You have to know that this is so since already his replacement is in his hand<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">It is less of an abandonment than an involuntary exchange.</i>.
Rav commanded to the House of Rav; there came Rav Hamnuna commanding the colleagues, if you are giving a gift on the holiday<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>lulav</i> which must be the personal property of the worshipper on the first day of the holiday.</i>, only give it fully explicit. As the following: Rav Huna gave an <i>etrog</i> to his son. He told him, if today is holiday<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Because in Babylonia before the publication of the rules of calendar computation one had to observe all holidays as two days since knowledge of the declaration of the first of Tishre and first of Nisan may not have arrived there before the 15th, he had to make it clear that the transfer of property was intended to be valid in any case.</i> it is a gift to you, and if it is tomorrow it is a gift to you.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> How is the order of the willow? There was a place below Jerusalem called Moṣa. They descended there to collect willow branches, and come and erect them on the sides of the altar, their heads bent on the altar. They trumpeted straight, treble, and straight. Every day they surround the altar once and say, <i>please, Eternal, please save; please, Eternal, please let succed</i><sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 118:25" href="/Psalms.118.25"><i>Ps.</i> 118:25</a>.</i>. Rebbi Jehudah says, <i>Ani wahu please save, ani wahu please save</i><sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">The spelling וָהוּא is confirmed by Maimonides’s autograph Mishnah. In his interpretation the reference is to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 32:39" href="/Deuteronomy.32.39"><i>Deut</i> 32:39</a>: <i>I, I am He, and no power competes with me</i>.</i>. On that day<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">The seventh day of Tabernacles.</i> they surround the altar seven times. <sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">This part of the Mishnah is not discussed in the Halakhah; it is not in Maimonides’s autograph of the Mishnah nor in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 45b" href="/Sukkah.45b">Babli (45b</a>) it seems to be a <i>baraita</i>.</i> What are they saying when taking leave? Beauty for you, altar, beauty for you, altar. Rebbi Eliezer says, to the Eternal and you, altar, to the Eternal and you, altar.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> What is Moṣa<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">In the bottom of the valley North of Jerusalem, near the water.</i>? Memsia<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">This name is unexplained. It cannot be identified with a place מציא (in Galilee) mentioned in versions of the <i>baraita</i> about the borders of the Land (<i>Ševi`it</i> 6:1, Tosephta <i>Ševi`it</i> 4:11, <i>Sifry Deut</i>. 51), but missing in the mosaic from the synagogue of Reḥov.</i>. Rebbi Tanḥuma said, its name was Colonia<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">A colony of Roman veterans.</i>.
Bar Qappara said, they<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">The willow branches taken for the altar, in contrast to those taken with the <i>lulav</i>, which are short.</i> are eleven cubits high. Rebbi Yose said, the Mishnah says so, “their heads bent on the altar.<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">Since the altar was 10 cubits high, the willows had to be appreciably larger in order to bend over. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 45a" href="/Sukkah.45a">Babli 45a</a>.</i>” Rebbi Ze`ira sent asking Rebbi Daniel ben Rav Qatina, did you hear from your father that it needs a benediction<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">A special benediction, “praise to You, Eternal, King of the Universe, Who sanctified us with His commandments and commanded us to take willow branches.”</i>, is taken alone<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">No <i>lulav</i> in the hands of the Cohanim circling the altar (as well as in the hands of the congregants during the prayers for rain, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sukkah 4:1:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sukkah.4.1.2">Note 19</a>).</i>, and has a minimum? Rebbi Ayvi bar Nagari came in the name of Rav Huna and said with reason that it needs a benediction and is taken alone. Whether it has a minimum I did not hear. There, they are saying Rav Sheshet and Rav Naḥman bar Jacob. One says, three leafed branches. But the other said, even one branch<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">It is clear that the Galilean authors Bar Qappara and R. Yose speak about the ceremony in the Temple. From the parallel in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 44b-45a" href="/Sukkah.44b-45a">Babli, 44b - 45a</a>, it seems that the Babylonian authors, from R. Ze`ira to Rav Naḥman, speak about the ceremony in the synagogue, where on the seventh day a separate bunch of willow twigs is taken</i>.
It was stated: Bodily defective ones<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">Since nobody but priests are permitted to enter the inner courtyard with the altar, by necessity only priests may circle the altar, and bodily defective priests are excluded from Temple service (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 21:16-24" href="/Leviticus.21.16-24"><i>Lev.</i> 21:16–24</a>).</i>. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish asked before Rebbi Joḥanan, may bodily defective ones enter between the altar and the Temple entrance<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">It is stated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Kelim 1:9" href="/Mishnah_Kelim.1.9">Mishnah <i>Kelim</i> 1:9</a> that bodily defective priests may not enter the space between altar and Temple entrance.</i>? He told him, they were qualified<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">The rule of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Kelim 1:9" href="/Mishnah_Kelim.1.9">Mishnah <i>Kelim</i> 1:9</a> is rabbinic but the rite of the willows is a tradition ascribed to Moses; in this case the defective priests are qualified since the rite is not a sacrificial service. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 44a" href="/Sukkah.44a">Babli 44a</a>.</i>.
Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: So is the Mishnah, אֲנִי וָהוּ please save. [Ani wahu please save.]<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">The corrector’s addition in brackets has to be deleted. R. Abbahu notes that the text of the Mishnah, reading וָהוּא, has to be corrected to וָהוֹ. Both אני and והו are of the 72 secret names of God which can be derived fron <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 14:19-21" href="/Exodus.14.19-21"><i>Ex.</i> 14:19–21</a>, 3 verses of 72 letters each, which have to be written boustrophedon, the first and last from left to right and the middle one from right to left, and then read from top to bottom. (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Rashi on Sukkah 45a" href="/Rashi_on_Sukkah.45a">Rashi 45a</a> on the Mishnah; cf. the author’s <i>The Scholar’s Haggadah</i>, p. 300, Note 244.)</i>
Rebbi Abbahu said, <i>for you as help for us</i><sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 80:3" href="/Psalms.80.3"><i>Ps.</i> 80:3</a>.</i>. For you is recognized<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">The reading of the text is unsafe and its meaning unclear. In line with the following homilies, the presumed meaning is that God liberated Himself by liberating Israel from Egypt.</i>. Rebbi Abba from Saronga, <i>and the Eternal will first save the tents of Jehudah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sach</i>. 12:17.</i>. It is written “he saved.<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">Meaning: He saved Himself.</i>” Rebbi Zibai explained, <i>for now leave</i><i>the city and dwell in the field</i><sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mica</i> 4:10.</i>, my <i>Shekhina</i> is in the field<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">A homily on the word ושכנת which is revocalized וּשְּכִנָתִ “My Presence”, the field being a simile for the desert.</i>. Ḥananiah the son of Rebbi Joshua’s brother says, <i>I am the Eternal, your God, Who led you out of the land of Egypt</i><sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 20:2" href="/Exodus.20.2"><i>Ex.</i> 20:2</a>.</i>, it is written “who was led out with you.<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">There is no textual evidence for a missing letter in this word.</i>”
<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">Most of this paragraph also is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Vayikra Rabbah 23:8" href="/Vayikra_Rabbah.23.8"><i>Lev. rabba</i> 23(8</a>), <i>Cant. rabba</i> 4(17).</i> Rebbi Berekhia, Rebbi Jeremiah in the name of Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba: Levi bar Sisi explained in Nahardea: <i>They saw the God of Israel and below His feet like the work of a brick of sapphire, and like the true sky in purity</i><sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 24:10" href="/Exodus.24.10"><i>Ex.</i> 24:10</a>.</i>. That is, before they were freed. But after they were freed, (it was not its way to be put) [where usually the brick is put]<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">The sapphire brick was at God’s feet as symbol of the enslavement of Israel; after their freeing it was no longer necessary but took its place in the Heavenly Temple. The corrector’s text in brackets is that of the Midrashim.</i> there it is put. Rebbi Berekhia said, it is not written here <i>the work</i>, but <i>like the work</i>, it and all its tools<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ʼεργαλεῑα “tools of trade”.</i>, (it and the basket and the rake.)<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">The scribe’s text in parentheses is confirmed by the Midrashim. The remainder of the text up to the statement of Bar Qappara is not in the parallel sources.</i> [it and all its merchandise. Rebbi Miasha said, in Babylonia it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Ezekiel 1:26" href="/Ezekiel.1.26"><i>Ez</i>. 1:26</a>.</i>, <i>like the looks of sapphire stone</i>, but in Egypt it is written, <i>like the work of</i>] <i>a brick of sapphire</i>. To teach you that just as the stone is harder than the brick, so the servitude of Babylonia was harder than the servitude of Egypt. Bar Qappara stated, before Israel was rescued from Egypt it<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">The brick of sapphire.</i> was traced in the sky; after they were rescued it was no longer seen in the sky. What is the reason? <i>and like the true sky in purity</i>, the sky if it is empty of clouds.
<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael Ba</i> 14, <i>Beshallaḥ Wayissa</i> 1; <i>Sifry Num.</i> 84, partially <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 30:11-34:35" href="/Exodus.30.11-34.35"><i>Tanḥuma Ki Tissa</i> 14</a>.</i> It was stated in the name of Rebbi Eliezer, an idol crossed with Israel through the Sea. What is the reason? <i>Before Your people which You redeemed for Yourself, peoples and his god</i><sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="II Samuel 7:24" href="/II_Samuel.7.24"><i>2S.</i> 7:24</a>.</i>. Rebbi Aqiba said to him, Heaven beware! If you are saying so it turns out that you profane the holy. Why does the verse say, <i>which You redeemed for Yourself</i>? If it were possible to say, as if You redeemed Yourself.
“On that day<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">The seventh day of Tabernacles.</i> they surrounded the altar seven times.” Rebbi Aḥa said, a remembrance of Jericho.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> As one does it on a weekday one does it on the Sabbath, only that one collected them<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">The willow branches.</i> on Sabbath Eve and keeps them in golden vats so they should not wilt. Rebbi Joḥanan ben Beroqa says, they brought palm branches<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">Fully open palm branches, in contrast to the tube-like <i>lulav</i>.</i> and struck them on top of the altar<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">This is the reading of most Mishnah mss. (including Maimonides’s autograph) and the quote from the Babli by R. Isaiah from Trani. But in the Babli (Munich ms. and <i>editio princeps</i>) the reading is: On the floor near the altar.</i>; the day was called “day of striking palm branches.” Immediately<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">After the end of services on the morning of the seventh day of Tabernacles.</i> the children untie their <i>lulavim</i> and eat their <i>etrogim</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> He said only minors, therefore not adults<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">Since only children may eat their <i>erg</i> after services on the seventh day.</i>. Did not Rebbi Avinna say in the name of Rav, if an <i>etrog</i> became disqualified on the first day of the holiday one is permitted to eat it? Rebbi Yose said, there<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">The seventh day is the last day <i>lulav</i> and <i>etrog</i> are taken. Since the <i>lulav</i> may be taken all day long, the same <i>lulav</i> could be used by others; it is not food and remains reserved for its religious use. But a disqualified <i>etrog</i> cannot be used for any religious action, it is not reserved for any use other than food.</i> while he cannot use it to fulfill his obligation, others might use it to fulfill their obligation. But here neither he nor others can use it to fulfill their obligation.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> How <i>Hallel</i> and joy eight? This shows that a person is obligated to honor the last day of the festival like all other days of the festival<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in the Halakhah, <i>Hallel</i> is obligatory on all holidays except the last day of Passover, and an obligatory sign of holiday enjoyment is the consumption of meat; in Jerusalem of the holiday sacrifice.</i>. How the <i>sukkah</i> seven? If he finished eating one may not undo his <i>sukkah</i> but one removes his vessels after afternoon prayers in honor of the last day of the festival<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">On which there is no obligation of <i>sukkah</i>.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sukkah (Lieberman) 3:2" href="/Tosefta_Sukkah_(Lieberman).3.2">Tosephta 3:2</a>. In the Babli this is an Amoraic tradition by R. Joḥanan in the name of R. Simeon ben Joṣadaq [<a class="refLink" data-ref="Arakhin 10a" href="/Arakhin.10a"><i>Arakhin</i> 10a</a>, <i>Ta`anit</i> 28b (<i>Soferim</i> 20:9)].</i>: On eighteen days and one night one recites the <i>Hallel</i> every year. The eight days of Tabernacles, the eight days of Ḥanukkah, the holiday of Pentecost, and the first day of the holiday of <i>Pesaḥ</i> and its night.
<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph also is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Chagigah 1:4:2-3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Chagigah.1.4.2-3"><i>Ḥagigah</i> 1:4</a> (ח).</i> Rebbi Ze`ira, Ulla bar Ismael in the name of Rebbi Eleazar: With pilgrimage well-being offerings which were slaughtered on the eve of a festival one does not satisfy his obligation for the pilgrimage<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">A pilgrimage to the Temple on the occasion of one of the three festivals of pilgrimage brings with it the obligation for two kinds of sacrifices. The first is the “elevation offering of appearance”, in response to the commandment (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 34:20" href="/Exodus.34.20"><i>Ex.</i> 34:20</a>): <i>They shall not appear empty-handed before My presence</i>. The second is a family sacrifice with the full name שַׁלְמֵי חֲגִיגָה “pilgrimage well-being offering”, usually shortened to חֲגִיגָה “pilgrimage offering”, in response to the commandment to be joyous on the festival of pilgrimage, since when there is a possibility of sacrificing the eating of well-being sacrifices is an act of joy (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 27:7" href="/Deuteronomy.27.7"><i>Deut</i>. 27:7</a>). But this must be a sacrifice on the holiday, not in preparation for the holiday. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 70b" href="/Pesachim.70b">Babli <i>Pesaḥim</i> 70b</a>, bottom.</i>. Rebbi Abba objected, was it not stated: With the pilgrimage offering of the Fourteenth one fulfills the obligation of joy but one does not fulfill the one of well-being offerings<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">The pilgrimage offering of the Fourteenth is an additional sacrifice on the 14<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> of Nisan, together with the <i>Pesaḥ</i> sacrifice. Since the <i>Pesaḥ</i> is to be eaten after the main meal, the meat for the main course is provided by the additional well-being offering. Since the meal is taken in the night of the holiday, it contributes to the enjoyment of the holiday, but it is not a sacrifice on the holiday. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 70a" href="/Pesachim.70a">Babli <i>Pesaḥim</i> 70a</a>. (Cf. the author’s <i>The Scholar’s Haggadah</i>, pp. 248–249.)</i>? Rebbi Ze`ira said, explain it that he slaughtered it on the holiday. Rebbi Abba said, if he slaughtered it on the holiday it is not a festival offering of the Fourteenth. What about it? Rebbi Ze`ira said, when we still were there, we heard stated Ulla bar Ismael in the name of Rebbi Eleazar. When we came up to here we heard stated, Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Eleazar: <i>only  be joyful</i><sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 16:15" href="/Deuteronomy.16.15"><i>Deut</i>. 16:15</a>.</i>, to add the night of the (last) [first]<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">The (scribe’s text) is confirmed in <i>Ḥagigah</i>; the [corrector’s] is from <i>Sifry Deut.</i> 142. The clause <i>only be joyful</i> seems unnecessary since the preceding verse already commands to enjoy the festival of Tabernacles. It is read to mean that the final day, which no longer belongs to Tabernacles, only has the obligation of joy, but none of the two sacrifices which already were obligatory on Tabernacles. Therefore by the argument presented for the offering of the Fourteenth, the last day may be celebrated with meat from a sacrifice brought on the preceding day. The corrector’s text is irrelevant for the discussion here, it refers the sacrifice of the Fourteenth to the 14<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> of Tishre, to provide food for the initial meal of the holiday.</i> festival day for joy. Or maybe also the nights of the (first) [last] day of the festival? <i>Only</i> split<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Ḥagigah</i>: “or inverse.” The text here is the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 48a" href="/Sukkah.48a">Babli’s, 48a</a>.</i>. Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Eleazar: <i>and you shall be jouyous</i><i>on your festival of pilgrimage</i><sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 16:14" href="/Deuteronomy.16.14"><i>Deut</i>. 16:14</a>.</i>. When you are obligated for a sacrifice of pilgrimage you are obligated for joy<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">Therefore no sacrifice on the eve of a holiday can be obligatory.</i>. They objected, did we not state, <i>hallel</i> and joy eight? Think of it, if the first day of the holiday falls on a Sabbath! He cannot slaughter on the eve of the holiday since Rebbi Ze`ira, Ulla bar Ismael in the name of Rebbi Eleazar said, with pilgrimage well-being offerings which were slaughtered on the eve of a festival one does not satisfy his obligation for the pilgrimage. He cannot slaughter on the holiday since we already have learned that the pilgrimage offering does not push aside the Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">Since no fixed date is given in the verse for these sacrifices, they cannot be brought on a Sabbath.</i>. When did they say, <i>Hallel</i> and joy eight? Rebbi Yose said, Rav Eudaimon the emigrant explained it for Cohanim and the goat<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">If the first day is a Sabbath, all required holiday sacrifices are brought on the altar. They all are holocausts except for the goat as purification offering whose meat is eaten by the Cohanim. But since it is a Sabbath, it may not be cooked or roasted for private use and the meat must be eaten raw. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 71a" href="/Pesachim.71a">Babli <i>Pesaḥim</i> 71a</a> holds that eating raw meat is no joy.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">A copy of this paragraph is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 6:6:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.6.6.4"><i>Berakhot</i> 6:6 (Notes 185–196</a>,ב).</i>“How the <i>sukkah</i> seven? If he finished eating one may not undo his <i>sukkah</i> but one removes his vessels after afternoon prayers in honor of the last day of the festival.” Rebbi Abba bar Cahana, Rav Ḥiyya bar Ashi in the name of Rav: A person must make his <i>sukkah</i> invalid as long as it is still day<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">In Israel, where holidays are kept only one day, if a person wants to use his <i>sukkah</i> on the last day of the festival he has to make sure not to violate the biblical injunction not to add to the biblical commandments (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 4:2" href="/Deuteronomy.4.2"><i>Deut</i>. 4:2</a>). If the <i>sukkah</i> becomes disqualified, it may be used later. It may not be made disqualified until after the afternoon snack eaten there on the seventh day.</i>. Rebbi Joshua ben Levi said, a person must make <i>Qiddush</i><sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">The sanctification of Sabbath and holidays (cf. <i>Berakhot</i> Chapter 8; also the author’s <i>The Scholar’s Haggadah</i>, pp. 209–227.)</i> inside his house<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">On the last day of the festival, irrespective of the place of the meal.</i>. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa in the name of Samuel: If one made <i>Qiddush</i> in one house and changed his mind to eat in another house he must make <i>Qiddush</i><sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">Samuel’s formulation in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 101a" href="/Pesachim.101a">Babli <i>Pesaḥim</i> 101a</a> is: there cannot be <i>Qiddush</i> except at the place of the holiday meal.</i>. Rebbi Aḥa, Rebbi Ḥinena in the name of Rav (Hoshaiah)<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">Scribe’s text, wrongly deleted by the corrector and therefore missing in the printed edition. The author’s note in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 6:6:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.6.6.4"><i>Berakhot</i> 6:6, Note 191</a>, has to be corrected accordingly.</i>: He who loves his <i>sukkah</i> makes <i>Qiddush</i> of the last holiday in his house and eats in his <i>sukkah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">In direct contradiction to Samuel’s statement.</i>. Rebbi Abun said, they do not disagree. What Rav said was, if he did not have the intention to eat in another house. But what Samuel said was if he had the intention to eat in another house. Rebbi Mana said, Samuel comes like Rebbi<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">Read: Rav.</i> Ḥiyya and Rebbi Hoshaiah like Rebbi Joshua ben Levi. Rebbi Immi, this implies that they disagree explicitly<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">Since Rav Ḥiyya bar Ashi requires that one disqualify the <i>sukkah</i> for the last day, he must hold that one makes <i>Qiddush</i> for the last day in the <i>sukkah</i> if one desires to use the <i>sukkah</i>, and this follows Samuel.</i>.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> How was the water libation seven days? He<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">The priest selected to pour the water on the altar.</i> was filling a golden flask holding three <i>log</i><sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">About 1.6 1.</i> from the Siloam. When they<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">The procession accompanying the priest. The trumpets were blown by priests in the Temple domain. The Water gate was the South-Eastern entrance to the Temple Mount.</i> reached the Water Gate they blew the trumpet straight, treble, and straight. He ascended the ramp and turned to his left. There were two silver bowls there; Rebbi Jehudah said that they were of lime but were darkened because of the wine. They had holes like two nostrils, one wide and one narrow so that both should finish at the same time<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">The holes for the water libation were smaller than those for the wine libation since the viscosity of the wine is larger than that of the water. It therefore is presumed that the viscosity of the wine was approximately constant through the years.</i>.<br>The Western one<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">The bowl for the libations.</i> for the water, and the Eastern one for the wine. If he poured the water into that for the wine or wine into that for the water, he accomplished his duty. Rebbi Jehudah says, one <i>log</i> he was pouring all eight days<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">One <i>log</i>, not three; for eight days, not seven.</i>. One says to the one pouring, lift your hand, since once he<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">A Sadducee objecting to the pouring of water not justified in the Pentateuch.</i> poured on his feet and all the people stoned him with their <i>etrogim</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina stated, to make a procession<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>pompa</i>, Greek μομπή “solemn procession”.</i> on the occasion. Yose ben Ḥanina in the name of Menaḥem from Yodfat: <i>It is Rebbi Aqiba’s</i> Yose ben Ḥanina<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah, which on a holiday permits trumpet blowing for something occurring outside the Temple precinct which otherwise would be rabbinically forbidden. Also the amount of 3 <i>log</i> is derived from biblical libations; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Zevachim 110b" href="/Zevachim.110b"><i>Zevaḥim</i> 110b</a>.</i>, since Rebbi Aqiba said, the water libation is a word from the Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Sifry Num</i>. 250 the argument is given in the name of R. Jehudah ben Bathyra, with different arguments ascribed to R. Aqiba and R. Nathan. The argument notes that in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 29" href="/Numbers.29"><i>Num</i>. 29</a> the sacrifices for the different days of Tabernacles are given in stereotyped language; the words noted are deviations from the usual ונסכה, כמשפט. R. Aqiba’s argument is presented differently in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Zevachim 110b" href="/Zevachim.110b">Babli, <i>Zevaḥim</i> 110b</a>.</i>.
There, we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Zevachim 13:6" href="/Mishnah_Zevachim.13.6">Mishnah <i>Zevaḥim</i> 13:6</a>.</i>: “Rebbi Eleazar<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">The Tanna, ben Shamua.</i> says, also one who pours a libation of water on Tabernacles outside of the Temple is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">As a matter of principle, any sacrifice outside the Temple conducted in the way it is biblically prescribed for the Temple is a deadly sin and if performed inadvertently makes the person liable for a purification sacrifice. To state that making a water libation outside the Temple creates such a liability for the performer is stating that the libation in the Temple is biblically prescribed.</i>.” Rebbi Joḥanan said<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Zevachim 110b" href="/Zevachim.110b">Babli <i>Zevaḥim</i> 110b</a>.</i>, the entire system of Rebbi Eleazar is the system of his teacher Rebbi Aqiba. Just as Rebbi Aqiba said, the water libation is a word from the Torah, so Rebbi Eleazar is saying, the water libation is a word from the Torah. There are Tannaim who state in the name of Rebbi Eleazar, it is necessary that the filling be for the holiday; there are Tannaim who state in the name of Rebbi Eleazar, it is not necessary that the filling be for the holiday<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">Since anything sacrificed on the altar needs a dedication, and using Temple vessels for not dedicated contents is sinful, if the water libation is a biblical requirement it needs a Temple vessel and dedication of the water at the moment of filling for the holiday. If the water libation is tradition, it does not have to be drawn for its purpose from the start.</i>. He who said, it is necessary that the filling be for the holiday, Rebbi Eleazar following Rebbi Aqiba; he who said, it is not necessary that the filling be for the holiday, Rebbi Eleazar following the rabbis. This you cannot say since Rebbi Joḥanan said, the entire system of Rebbi Eleazar is the system of his teacher Rebbi Aqiba. Just as Rebbi Aqiba said, the water libation is a word from the Torah, so Rebbi Eleazar is saying, the water libation is a word from the Torah. What is the difference between them? Rebbi Ze`ira said, only if he poured three <i>log</i> inside and three <i>log</i> outside<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">This statement is elliptic. It can be read parallel to a statement of R. Simeon ben Laqish in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Zevachim 110b" href="/Zevachim.110b">Babli <i>Zevaḥim</i> 110b</a> as stating that since in the Temple the libation is only valid if it is of exactly three <i>log</i> (a quarter of a <i>hin</i>, the smallest admissible libation of wine, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 15:4" href="/Numbers.15.4"><i>Num</i>. 15:4</a>), also outside it creates liability only if it is exactly 3 <i>log</i>.</i>. There are Tannaim who state, there is prescribed measure; there are Tannaim who state, there is no prescribed measure. He who said that there is prescribed measure, not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">If the measure is not exactly 3 <i>log</i>, since inside the Temple this is a biblical obligation.</i>; he who said there is no prescribed measure, liable<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">In any amount, since inside the ceremony is tradition, not Scripture.</i>.
Yose Bar Ashian in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: The bowls have to be plugged at the moment of pouring. What is the reason? <i>In the sanctuary pouring a libation of shekhar to the Eternal</i><sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 49b" href="/Sukkah.49b">Babli 49b</a>. For the cryptic quote of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 28:7" href="/Numbers.28.7"><i>Num</i>. 28:7</a> it is explained first in <i>Sifry Num.</i> 143 that the repetition הסך נסך includes not only libations of wine but also those of water; it is added in the next paragraph that שכר therefore cannot be interpreted only by the Hebrew connotation of “intoxicating drink” but also (in parallel with the Arabic שנר) “to be abundantly full (of milk), thanks, gratitude”. [<a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 49b" href="/Sotah.49b">Babli <i>Sotah</i> 49b</a>, <i>Tanḥuma Pinḥas</i> 12, <i>Pesiqta Rabbati</i> 17 (ed. Ish-Shalom 80 a/b), <i>Pesiqta dRav Cahana et qorbani laḥmi</i> (ed. Buber Note 20).]</i>.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sukkah (Lieberman) 3:15" href="/Tosefta_Sukkah_(Lieberman).3.15">Tosephta 3:15</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 49a" href="/Sukkah.49a">Babli 49a</a>.</i>: “Rebbi Yose says, the basis was perforated down to the abyss. What is the reason? <sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Isaiah 5:2" href="/Isaiah.5.2"><i>Is.</i> 5:2</a>.</i><i>He turned it up, removed its stones, planted precious vine, and built a tower in its midst</i>, this is the Temple, <i>and a wine-press he excavated in it</i>, this is the altar. <i>And also an altar</i>, that is the basis.” Rebbi Simeon says, it was work of Heaven. Since it was work of Heaven, maybe it was not beautiful like artist’s work? The verse says, <i>the roundnesses of your hips are like hoops, formed by artist’s hands</i><sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Song of Songs 7:2" href="/Song_of_Songs.7.2"><i>Cant</i>. 7:2</a>.</i>; they were more beautiful than those formed by artist’s hands. It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sukkah (Lieberman) 3:15" href="/Tosefta_Sukkah_(Lieberman).3.15">Tosephta 3:15</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 49a" href="/Sukkah.49a">Babli 49a</a>.</i>: “Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Ṣadoq said, a small opening was between the Temple Hall and the altar. Once in seventy years young priests climb down and bring it<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">The congealed remainders of the wine poured on the altar as libations.</i> up in sanctity since it was congealed like fig cakes; they come and burn it in sanctity. Just as its pouring was in sanctity, so its burning was in sanctity.” Rebbi said, the Torah let you grasp an expression of love, an expression of fullness, and an expression of inebriation<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sukkah 4:6:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sukkah.4.6.4">Note 107</a>, explanation of שכר.</i>. Rebbi Judah bar Laqqara in the name of Rebbi Samuel bar Naḥman: Since the Temple was destroyed, congealing wine disappeared, and white glass disappeared. What is white glass? It was flexible.
They wanted to say that the large {hole}<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">In the bowl mentioned in Mishnah 7.</i> was for water, the small one for wine, for Rebbi Jonah said in the name of Rebbi Immi: A hole which does not let pass water passes wine, what does not let pass wine passes oil, does not let pass oil passes honey. And even if you say it is the other way<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">If one confirms by experiment that the more viscous fluid requires a larger hole, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 48b" href="/Sukkah.48b">Babli 48b</a>.</i> around, is that not Rebbi Jehudah’s? And Rebbi Jehudah said, a <i>log</i> he was pouring all eight days<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">The entire part of Mishnah after R. Jehudah’s statement also is R. Jehudah’s. Since he states that the volume of water is one third of the volume of wine, it is obvious that the hole of the water bowl must be much smaller than that of the wine bowl if both bowls should simultaneously empty. For the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 48b" href="/Sukkah.48b">Babli 48b</a> his bowl must be smaller.</i>. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish asked before Rebbi Joḥanan: If he preceded them before the sacrifice, what<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">If pouring the water is an independent sacral act it cannot be performed before the morning daily sacrifice nor after the evening daily sacrifice. If it is an appendix to the sacrifices like the libations of wine it can be performed any time, including the night following the day. Babli <i>Ta`anit</i> 2b.</i>? If he poured in the night, what? If he did not pour today may he pour the next day? He said to him, let us hear from the following which Rebbi Ila said in the name of Rebbi Yasa: <i>And its libations</i><sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 29:31" href="/Numbers.29.31"><i>Num</i>. 29:31</a>, the text for the sixth day of Tabernacles.</i>, the same for libations of water as for libations of wine<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yoma 1:5:5-6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yoma.1.5.5-6"><i>Yoma</i>, Halakhah 1:5, Notes 213–222</a>.</i>. This implies that if he preceded them before the sacrifice, it is qualified. If he poured in the night, it is qualified. If he did not pour today he may not pour the next day, because if the day passed, its sacrifice passed.
The rabbis increase the water and decrease the days; Rebbi Jehudah decreases the water and increases the days<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">One <i>log</i>, not three; for eight days, not seven.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yoma 1:5:5-6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yoma.1.5.5-6"><i>Yoma</i>, Halakhah 1:5, Notes 213–222</a>.</i> Some want to say, he was the same for the Cow<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">Where the Sadducees insisted that the Cohen who burns the Red Cow must be pure from the preceding sundown. However, it is difficult to square what is reported in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Parah 3:5" href="/Tosefta_Parah.3.5">Tosephta <i>Parah</i> 3:8</a> with what is presumed here: “It happened that a Sadducee was pure from the preceding sundown when he came to burn the Cow, but Rabban Joḥanan ben Zakkai realized it; he came and leaned both of his hands on him and told him, Sir High Priest, how wonderful you are as High Priest, go and immerse yourself. He went down and immersed himself. After he came back he grated his earlobe and said to him, Ben Zakkai, when I shall be free to deal with you; he answered him, if you will be free. It was not three days before they put him in the grave.”<br>Biblical impurity is only original impurity and derivative impurity in the first degree. Rabbinic impurity has derivative impurity in the second degree for unwashed hands, third degree impurity for heave, and fourth degree for sacrifices. Therefore, the intrinsically pure Ben Zakkai (who before the destruction of the Temple was not Rabban), by putting his second degree impure hands on the priest defiled the latter for <i>sacra</i> and forced him to immerse himself in a <i>miqweh</i>. Our sources seem to describe the Sadducee position correctly; cf. Dead Sea Scrolls fragment 4Q394, lines 16–18.</i>, on Tabernacles, and on the Day of Atonement<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">Reported in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yoma 1:5:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yoma.1.5.4">Yoma 1:5, Note 211</a>. He followed Sadducee doctrine in bringing the incense into the Holiest of Holies, and died soon after.</i>. Rebbi Simon does not say so, but either the Cow and Tabernacles were one and Atonement one, or Cow and Atonement were one and Tabernacles one. He who said, after a short time he died, he did all three. He who said, his nose gushed out worms and something like a calf’s hoof grew in his brain, follows him who said, either Cow and Tabernacles were one and Atonement one, or Cow and Atonement were one and Tabernacles one. The Courtyard cried about them, get out of here, sons of Eli, you defiled our God’s Temple. “On that day, the corner of the altar was damaged and they put a block of salt there lest it should look deficient since any altar without corner, or walkway, or base is damaged.<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sukkah (Lieberman) 3:16" href="/Tosefta_Sukkah_(Lieberman).3.16">Tosephta <i>Sukkah</i> 3:16</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 48b" href="/Sukkah.48b">Babli 48b</a>.</i>”
They asked before Rebbi Abbahu: Is it not written<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 16:17" href="/Leviticus.16.17"><i>Lev.</i> 16:17</a>.</i>, <i>nobody shall be in the Tent of Meeting during his coming to atone in the sanctuary until he leaves</i>, not even those about whom it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Ezekiel 1:10" href="/Ezekiel.1.10"><i>Ez</i>. 1:10</a>. If the offending priest was hit on his head by a calf’s foot it must have been by one of the four-headed angels seen by Ezechiel whose feet are calves’ feet (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Ezekiel 1:7" href="/Ezekiel.1.7"><i>Ez</i>. 1:7</a>).</i>, <i>the shapes of their faces are human shapes</i> shall not be in the Tent of Meeting? He answered them, if he enters following normal rules.

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> As it was done on a weekday it was done on the Sabbath, except that on Friday he filled a non-sanctified barrel<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">The sacrificial day starts in the morning, and all remainders from the previous day become disqualified at that time. The Sabbath starts on the preceding evening; the water must be drawn on Friday. If pouring the water libation were an independent sacrificial action, the water in a Temple vessel would become disqualified at the end of the night. Since it was established (Note 121) that the water libation is an accessory action, the water would not become disqualified even in a Temple vessel; the profane vessel is used to give the water ceremony a boost in status.</i> from the Siloam and deposited it in the lodge. If it was spilled or became uncovered<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">Wine or water left uncovered and unsupervised becomes forbidden for health reasons lest a snake living in the house drink from it and on this occasion leave some poison in the fluid. The rules about this are given in <i>Terumot</i> 8:5–7. If it becomes unusable for humans it is unbecoming to use it for Heaven.</i> he filled from the basin since water or wine which remained uncovered are disqualified from being put on the altar.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Why non-sanctified? Even if it is sanctified! Did not Rebbi Aḥa, Rebbi Ḥinena say in the name of Rebbi Yasa, <i>you shall sanctify the altar; then the altar will be most holy</i><sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 40:10" href="/Exodus.40.10"><i>Ex.</i> 40:10</a>.</i>. Since the altar only sanctifies with intention, so vessels should only sanctify with intention<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">The verse starts: <i>anoint the altar and all its vessels</i>, which implies that the power of sanctification is the same for the altar and its vessels. Since it is forbidden to bring profane (not dedicated) matter to the altar, profane matter remains profane also on the altar. This proves that the altar and therefore the sanctified vessels only sanctify with dedication.</i>. Ḥizqiah said, that they should not say, we saw that water filled for the sanctification of hands and feet become disqualified by staying overnight<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">Since sanctification of hands and feet must be with water from a Temple vessel, the water becomes disqualified at daybreak. The problem is that people will say that they saw the water becoming disqualified and nevertheless used for libation, they will wrongly conclude that disqualified water may be used on the altar.</i>. In the House of Rebbi Yannai they said, that they should not say, we saw that water filled for Tabernacles become disqualified by staying overnight<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">Again, and be used nevertheless.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, because of the evil impression; but we do not know whether following Ḥizqiah or according to everybody’s opinion following Rebbi Yannai. Rebbi Pedat in the name of Rebbi Hoshaia: The water for a deviant wife becomes disqualified by staying overnight<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 2:2:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.2.2.9"><i>Soṭah</i> 2:2, Note 114</a>. This is a matter of dispute. There is an opinion that the water has to be taken from the water basin in the Temple. Any water taken from there and sanctified in a Temple vessel belongs to the service of that day and becomes disqualified by the next dawn. But according to the opinion that the water may come from outside sources, this need not be the case.</i>. Rebbi Abuna: anything of whose kind nothing possibly is for the altar cannot become disqualified by staying overnight<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote">Disputing R. Pedat in principle.</i>.
They wanted to say, if he transgressed<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">And brought uncovered wine or water to the altar.</i> and brought it is qualified. Rebbi Joshua the Southerner stated before Rebbi Jonah that uncovered water and wine are disqualified<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">In any case.</i> from the altar. What is the reason? <i>And one sheep from 200, from the drink of Israel</i><sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Ezekiel 45:15" href="/Ezekiel.45.15"><i>Ez</i>. 45:15</a>.</i>, from something permitted to Israel<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">While a prophet cannot prescribe, he can present authoritative practice. Since uncovered water is forbidden to Israel, it is absolutely disqualified for the altar.</i>. So far water, wine? Rebbi Sabbatai said, <i>which gladdens God and men</i><sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Judges 9:13" href="/Judges.9.13"><i>Jud</i>. 9:13</a>, referring to wine. Since drinking uncovered wine, even if filtered to remove the poison, leaves people with fear and does not gladden; it is disqualified for all religious observances.</i>.

Chapter 5



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The fife five and six; this is the fife of the water-drawing festivity which supersedes neither the Sabbath nor the holiday. There is a saying that one who did not see the joy of the water-drawing festivity did never see real joy.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “The fife five and six,” etc. Therefore the one of sacrifices supersedes<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Since it is emphasized that at the water-drawing festivity fifes may not be used, it is implied that on other occasions fifes may be played in the Temple on the Sabbath. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 51a" href="/Sukkah.51a">Babli 51a</a>.</i>. The Mishnah is Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Jehudah’s, as it was stated: The fife accompanying the sacrifices supersedes the Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Playing a musical instrument on the Sabbath and holidays in general is rabbinically forbidden, so one should not be tempted to tune or repair the instrument, which would be a biblical infraction. Since it is stated in <i>Eruvin</i> Chapter 10 that in general rabbinic Sabbath prohibitions are not to be observed in the Temple, the burden of proof is on those authorities who deny the use of fifes in the Temple on a Sabbath.</i>, the words of Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Jehudah, but the Sages say, it supersedes neither the Sabbath nor the holiday. There we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Arakhin 2:3" href="/Mishnah_Arakhin.2.3">Mishnah <i>Arakhin</i> 2:3</a>.</i>: “On twelve days in the year the fife beats the time before the altar, at the sacrifice of the First <i>Pesaḥ</i>, the sacrifice of the Second <i>Pesaḥ</i>, on the first day of Passover, on the day of Pentecost, and on the eight days of Tabernacles.” Are there eight days without a Sabbath? Rebbi Yose said it anonymously, Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: It is Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Jehudah’s<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Mishnah implicitly states that fifes were used on the Sabbath in the week of Tabernacles.</i>. In the opinion of those rabbis why does it not supersede? Because it is not clear<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">There is no biblical indication that fifes be used in Temple worship. A popular usage is no reason to disregard rabbinic prohibitions (i. e., popular restrictions) in the Temple.</i>. But is it not written<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Kings 1:40" href="/I_Kings.1.40"><i>1K</i>. 1:40</a>, a description of Solomon’s coronation.</i>, <i>and the people blew fifes</i>? That is written for Solomon’s festivity. Rebbi Jonah in the name of Rebbi Abba bar Mamal: <i>And heartfelt joy like one marching with a fife</i><sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Isaiah 30:29" href="/Isaiah.30.29"><i>Is.</i> 30:29</a>. The verse starts with a reference to <i>song of the night starting the holiday</i>, referring to the recital of <i>Hallel</i> in the Passover night, as explained in Jonathan’s paraphrase of the verse. Therefore the verse shows that the combination of playing the fife and reciting the <i>Hallel</i> is traditional.</i>. Any time one uses a fife one recites the <i>Hallel</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun in the name of Rebbi Abba bar Mamal: Why does one recite <i>Hallel</i> all seven days of Tabernacles<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">In contrast to Passover, where <i>Hallel</i> is recited (and therefore the fife played) only on the first day. (Later practice to recite a truncated form of <i>Hallel</i> on these days is a Babylonian peculiarity unknown to and irrelevant for Galilean sources.)</i>? Corresponding to the <i>lulav</i> which is renewed all seven days<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Because it frequently needs new willow branches and occasionally also new myrtle branches.</i>. And why a fife for all these things? We have stated, for one is at stated times and the other is at stated times; this is beloved and that is beloved. They attached the beloved to the beloved. “On Pentecost one says to him, here you have <i>maṣsah</i>, here you have leavened.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sukkah 1:10:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sukkah.1.10.1">Mishnah 1:10</a>.</i>” There are Tannaim who state, here you have leavened, [here you have <i>maṣsah</i>.]<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Corrector’s addition, unnecessary since the question is which of the breads, the azyme shew-bread or the leavened two extra loaves of Pentecost (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:17" href="/Leviticus.23.17"><i>Lev.</i> 23:17</a>) are distributed first.</i> He who said, here you have <i>maṣsah</i>, because it is beloved; he who said, here you have leavened, because it is frequent<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Even though this text is repeated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sukkah 5:8:2-8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sukkah.5.8.2-8">Halakhah 5:8</a>, it seems that text should read that <i>maṣsah</i> is frequent, and therefore leavened bread better liked. In fact the two breads of Pentecost are the only leavened breads permitted in the Temple area during the entire year and it is a general principle that in the Temple service the more frequent must have precedence over the less frequent.</i>.
Rebbi Joshua ben Levi said, why is it called drawing festivity? For from there one was drawing the holy spirit, following <i>you shall draw water in rejoycing from the fountains of salvation</i><sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Isaiah 12:3" href="/Isaiah.12.3"><i>Is.</i> 12:3</a>. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 50b" href="/Sukkah.50b">Babli 50b</a>.</i>. An illustration<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">The following (up to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sukkah 5:1:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sukkah.5.1.3">Note 21</a>) is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bereishit Rabbah 98:16" href="/Bereishit_Rabbah.98.16"><i>Gen. rabba</i> 98(16</a>) (M. Sokoloff, <i>The Genizah fragments of Bereshit Rabba</i>, Jerusalem 1982, p. 187 l. 21–30). There the statements of RR. Levi and Joḥanan are interchangend.</i>. Rebbi Levi and Jehudah bar Naḥman were taking two tetradrachmas to assemble the congregation before Rebbi Joḥanan. Rebbi Levi went and preached, Jonah ben Amittai was from the tribe of Asher, for it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Judges 1:31" href="/Judges.1.31"><i>Jud</i>. 1:31</a>.</i>, <i>Asher did not disinherit the inhabitants of Acco and the inhabitants of Sidon</i>. And it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Kings 17:9" href="/I_Kings.17.9"><i>1K</i>. 17:9</a>. This quote presupposes that Jonah ben Amittai was the son of the widow from Sarepta.</i>, <i>arise and go to Sarepta which belongs to Sidon</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan came and preached, Jonah ben Amittai was from the tribe of Zevulun, as it is witten<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Joshua 19:10" href="/Joshua.19.10"><i>Jos</i>. 19:10</a>.</i>, <i>the third lot fell to the sons of Sevulun according to their families</i>, and it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Joshua 19:13" href="/Joshua.19.13"><i>Jos</i>. 19:13</a>. The verse shows that Gat-Ḥepher belonged to Zevulun.</i>, <i>from there it turned eastward to Gat Ḥepher and Et Qaṣin</i>. And it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="II Kings 14:25" href="/II_Kings.14.25"><i>2K.</i> 14:25</a>.</i>, <i>following the word of the Eternal, the God of Israel, which He spoke through His servant, Jonah ben Amittai the prophet from Gat Ḥepher</i>. On the next Sabbath Rebbi Levi said to Jehudah bar Naḥman: you take the two tetradrachmas and go<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Gen. rabba</i> R. Levi gives the money to Jehudah bar Naḥman so that the latter lets him go and give the sermon, to harmonize his statement with R. Joḥanan’s.</i> assemble the congregation before Rebbi Joḥanan. He went and said before them, Rebbi Joḥanan did teach us well, his mother from Asher and his father from Zevulun. <i>And his hip from Sidon</i><sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 49:13" href="/Genesis.49.13"><i>Gen</i>. 49:13</a>, from Jabob’s blessing of Zevulun. The one prophet from the tribe of Zevulun came from a mother from Sidonian territory, which was Asher territory.</i>, the hip from which he came was from Sidon. And it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jonah 1:3" href="/Jonah.1.3"><i>Jonah</i> 1:3</a>. The following argument is the reason the preceding Midrash was quoted here, in connection with the water-drawing festivities.</i>, <i>he descended to Yafo</i>, when it should have been “he descended to Acco<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">As a Galilean he could have used the nearest port.</i>”! Rebbi Jonah said, Jonah ben Amittai belonged to the pilgrims<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Who came to Jerusalem in Jehudah, avoiding the Northern sanctuaries in Bethel and Dan.</i>, came to the water-drawing festivity and the holy spirit rested on him, to teach you that the holy spirit only rests on a happy heart<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 30b" href="/Shabbat.30b">Babli <i>Šabbat</i> 30b</a>.</i>. What is the reason? <i>It was when the musical instrument played, God’s spirit was on him</i><sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="II Kings 3:15" href="/II_Kings.3.15"><i>2K.</i> 3:15</a>.</i>. Rebbi Benjamin bar Levi said, it is not written here “it was when he played on the musical instrument”, but <i>it was when the musical instrument played, God’s spirit was on him</i><sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to a statement of R. Levi in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 1:1:27" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.1.1.27"><i>Berakhot</i> 1:1 (Note 97</a>), that Elisha used David’s Aeolian lyre which played by itself in the wind.</i>.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 51b" href="/Sukkah.51b">Babli 51b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sukkah (Lieberman) 4:6" href="/Tosefta_Sukkah_(Lieberman).4.6">Tosephta 4:6</a>.</i>: “Rebbi Jehudah said, anybody who did not see the double stoa<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Greek διπλη̅-στοά.</i> of Alexandria did not ever see the glory of Israel. It was like a large basilica<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Greek βασιλική, ‘η; Latin <i>basilica</i>.</i> with a stoa inside a stoa. Sometime there were there twice as many as left Egypt. Seventy golden chairs were there, inlaid with precious stones and pearls, corresponding to the Seventy Elders; each of them costing 250’000 gold denars. A wooden platform was at the center and the congregation’s beadle stood on it. If one of the congregation came to read in the Torah the official waved cloths and they answered after him “amen”. For every benediction which he said, the official waved cloths and they answered after him “amen”. Nevertheless they were not sitting mixed but those of each single profession were sitting separately, so that a stranger could associate with those of his profession, and from there he could find support.” And who destroyed it? Trajanus the evil one.
Rebbi Simeon ben Yoḥai said, at three places were Israel warned not to return to the land of Egypt, [as it is written:] <i>for as you saw Egypt today you shall nevermore see them for ever</i><sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 14:13" href="/Exodus.14.13"><i>Ex.</i> 14:13</a>.</i>. <i>And the Eternal said to you, you shall never again return on this way</i><sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 17:16" href="/Deuteronomy.17.16"><i>Deut</i>. 17:16</a>.</i>. <i>And the Eternal will bring you back to Egypt on ships</i><sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 28:68" href="/Deuteronomy.28.68"><i>Deut</i>. 28:68</a>.</i>. In all three cases they returned and in all three cases they fell. One in the days of Sanherib the king of Assyria, [as it is written]: <i>woe on those who descend to Egypt for help</i><sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Isaiah 31:1" href="/Isaiah.31.1"><i>Is.</i> 31:1</a>.</i>. What is written after this? <i>But Egypt is human and not god, and their horses are flesh</i>, etc<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Isaiah 31:3" href="/Isaiah.31.3"><i>Is.</i> 31:3</a>.</i>. And one in the days of Joḥanan the son of the bald; <i>it shall be that the sword which you are fearing, there will reach you</i><sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jeremiah 42:16" href="/Jeremiah.42.16"><i>Jer.</i> 42:16</a>.</i>. And once in the days of the evil <i>Trajanus</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">In slightly different versions this is in <i>Thr. rabba</i> 1(48), 4(23). This is the only Talmudic reference to the great Jewish revolt in Egypt, Cyrenaica, and Cyprus which erupted when Trajanus attacked and temporarily occupied Mesopotamia, the home of the main body of Jews outside the Roman empire.</i> A son was born to him on the Ninth of Ave and they did fast. His daughter died on Ḥanukkah and they lit lights. His wife sent and told him, instead that you conquer the barbarians come and conquer the Jews who revolted against you. He intended to come in ten days and came and found them occupied [in the Torah with the verse:] <i>He will carry against you a people from far away, from the ends of the earth</i><sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 28:49" href="/Deuteronomy.28.49"><i>Deut</i>. 28:49</a>.</i>, etc. He asked them, with what were you occupied. They answered him, with such-and-such. He said to them, this man is he since he intended to come in ten days but came in five. He surrounded them by legions and killed them. He said to their wives, if you listen to my legions I shall not kill you. They told him, what you did to those on the ground floor do to those on the gallery<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">Since the action of Trajanus earlier was characterized as directed against the great synagogue in Alexandria, “those on the ground floor” are the men sitting in the synagogue, whereas the women are “those on the gallery”, probably the roof of the double stoa.</i>. He mixed their blood with their blood and the blood flowed in the sea up to Cyprus. At that moment the horn of Israel was trimmed and will not be restituted until the Son of David will come.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> After the end of the first day of Tabernacles they descended to the women’s courtyard<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">The one nearest to the entrance to the Temple compound and the largest one.</i> and instituted there a great alteration<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">The alteration, as described in the Halakhah, was that the courtyard was reserved for men but the women were relegated to a balcony erected for the occasion.</i>. Golden candelabra were there with golden bowls on top<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli version, each candelabrum carried four separate bowls which therefore required four separate ladders to be filled.</i> and four ladders for each candelabrum. Four children<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">These cannot have been minors but must have been older teenagers since the weight of the oil, not counting the vessels, either was about 12 kg or 48 kg per person.</i> of the young Cohanim, in their hands oil carafes of 120 <i>log</i>, filled each bowl.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 53a" href="/Sukkah.53a">Babli 53a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sukkah (Lieberman) 4:5" href="/Tosefta_Sukkah_(Lieberman).4.5">Tosephta 4:5</a>. In Babli and Tosephta the statement is formulated in the first person, “we did not taste.” This is appropriate since he was a Levite involved in the Temple service.</i>“Rebbi Joshua ben Ḥanania said, all during the water-drawing festivity they did not at all taste the taste of sleep. At the start they went to sacrifice the daily morning sacrifice; from there they went to sacrifice the <i>musaf</i> sacrifices; from there they went [to sacrifice vows and voluntary offerings<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">A vow expresses an obligation to bring a certain kind of sacrifice, whereas a voluntary offering is a dedication of a certain animal as sacrifice. If the animal becomes disqualified after dedication, in the case of a vow the owner has to procure a replacement, but not in the case of the voluntary offering.</i>; from there they went]<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">Corrector’s addition; unknown source.</i> to eat and drink. From there they went to study Torah; and from there they went to sacrifice the daily evening sacrifice; and from there they went to the water-drawing festivity.” But was it not stated<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 53a" href="/Sukkah.53a">Babli 53a</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 2:1:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.2.1.4"><i>Nedarim</i> 2:1 Note 20</a> (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 15a" href="/Nedarim.15a">Babli <i>Nedarim</i> 15a</a>). Both Babli sources are Amoraic in the name of R. Joḥanan.</i>, “an oath that I shall not sleep for three days,” one whips him<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">For a vain oath since nobody can go without sleep for three days.</i> and he sleeps immediately? They were dozing<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">As the Babli explains, R. Joshua did not say that they did not sleep but that they did not taste the taste of sleep.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 51b-52a" href="/Sukkah.51b-52a">Babli 51b/52a</a>.</i>“And instituted there a great alteration.” What alteration did they make there? They caused the men to be for themselves and the women to be for themselves, following what we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Middot 2:5" href="/Mishnah_Middot.2.5">Mishnah <i>Middot</i> 2:5</a>.</i>: “Originally it<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">The wall of the women’s courtyard.</i> was smooth but they surrounded it with ledges standing out<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ʼεξωστήρ “what is standing out”, beams standing out from the wall which for the water-drawing festivities could be covered with planks to create a balcony for the women. This dates the (pharisaic) festivities clearly at a date later than the return from Babylon.</i> that the women could see from above and the men below, so they should not be mixed. From where did they learn? From the words of the Torah: <i>The Land will be lamenting, all families separately</i><sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Zechariah 12:12" href="/Zechariah.12.12"><i>Sach</i>. 12:12</a>(-14). The essence of the proof is from the part of the verses not quoted in the text, ending with <a class="refLink" data-ref="Zechariah 12:14" href="/Zechariah.12.14">v. 14</a>: <i>each family separately and their women separately</i>.</i>. Two Amoraim, one said, this is the eulogy of the Messiah, but the other one said, this is the eulogy of evil inclinations<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">According to the Babli, the Messiah referred to here is the Messiah from the tribe of Joseph who will be killed in the war of Gog (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Ezekiel 39" href="/Ezekiel.39"><i>Ez</i>. 39</a>). When evil inclinations will be abolished, it will appear to the just ones like a big mountain and they will cry because they could not believe they overcame such a big obstacle, but to the sinners it will appear as a small lump and they will cry for they could not overcome such a little obstacle.</i>. He who said, this is the eulogy of the Messiah, if at the moment they are mourning you are saying the men to be for themselves and the women to be for themselves, when they are merry not so much more? He who says, this is the eulogy of evil inclinations, if there are no evil inclinations you are saying the men to be for themselves and the women to be for themselves, when evil inclinations exist not so much more!
“Golden candelabra were there.” Bar Qappara said, their height was 100 cubits<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 52b" href="/Sukkah.52b">Babli 52b</a>, “50 cubits”.</i>. But did we not state, anything reaching 100 cubits needs a basis of 33? A ladder on one side and 33, and a ladder on the other side and 33; but was it not stated, the entire courtyard was only 187 long by 135 wide<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Middot 5:1" href="/Mishnah_Middot.5.1">Mishnah <i>Middot</i> 5:1</a>. If good architectural practice requires that a brick wall 100 high not be steeper than about 72°, then two brick candelabra side by side will fill 132=4×33 cubits; with two ladders it more than fills the entire width of the courtyard, crowding out the people.</i>? It was found stated: Their place was by a wonder<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">Or the candelabra were mounted on metal poles.</i>.
How<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the amount of oil used for the illumination; see <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sukkah 5:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sukkah.5.2.1">Note 42</a>. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 52b" href="/Sukkah.52b">Babli 52b</a>.</i>? In the hand of all of them 120 <i>log</i> or in the hand of each of them 120 <i>log</i>?

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> They tore apart the worn-out trousers of the Cohanim and their belts<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">Which are biblically prescribed and therefore Temple property, not personal property of the priests.</i>, and from these were lighting<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">Making the wicks to light the candelabra.</i>. No courtyard was in Jerusalem which was not illuminated by the light of the water-drawing festivity.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> It was stated: From the worn-out trousers of the High Priest they lit the inner lights, and from the worn-out trousers of common priests they lit the outer lights<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">The inner lights are those on the candelabrum in the sanctuary; the outer lights are those used in the Temple courtyard.</i>. Rebbi Samuel bar Rav Isaac said, it is written, <i>to elevate the permanent light</i><sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 27:20" href="/Exodus.27.20"><i>Ex.</i> 27:20</a>.</i>. They estimated to say that nothing produces a flare but flax<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">Less restrictive in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 21a" href="/Shabbat.21a">Babli, <i>Šabbat</i> 21a</a>.</i>.
What means “tore apart”? Rebbi Ḥaggai explained before Rebbi Yose, “made wicks”.
It was stated: “No courtyard was in Jerusalem which was not illuminated by the light of the water-drawing festivity.” It was stated: A woman could check her wheat by the light of the fire on the altar. Would they not commit larceny<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">Since using Temple property for private affairs is the crime of <i>me`ilah</i> which requires restitution, payment of a fine, and a reparation offering. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 52b-53a" href="/Sukkah.52b-53a">Babli 52b/53a</a>.</i>? No, as Rebbi Joshua ben Levi said, taste, looks, and sound are not subject to larceny<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">Since they are not money’s worth.</i>. Six sounds were heard in Jericho. <sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Tamid 3:8" href="/Mishnah_Tamid.3.8">Mishnah <i>Tamid</i>3:8</a>.</i>“In Jericho one heard the sound of the opening of the main gate<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">Of the Temple courtyards.</i>. In Jericho one heard the sound of the organ<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">Described in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sukkah 5:6:2-7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sukkah.5.6.2-7">Halakhah 6</a>.</i>. In Jericho one heard the sound of the wooden mechanical device for the basin which Ben Qaṭin made<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">When at dawn the basin was lifted from the cistern; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yoma 3:8:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yoma.3.8.1"><i>Yoma</i>3:10, Note 192</a>.</i>. In Jericho one heard the voice of Gabinius the herald. In Jericho one heard the sound of the fife<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">Not the ones of the water-drawing festivities but the ones played before the altar, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sukkah 5:1:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sukkah.5.1.2">Note 3</a>.</i>. In Jericho one heard the sound of the cymbal<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">Which signals the start of the Levites’ song for the daily morning sacrifice.</i>, and some are saying, also the voice of the High Priest when he mentioned the Name on the day of Atonement. In Jericho one smelled the compounding of the incense. Rebbi Eliezer ben Dalgai said, my family had goats in the Akhwar Mountains<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">An unidentified locality; in Babli sources מכוור.</i> which were sneezing from the smell of compounding the incense.”

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The pious ones and people of good deeds<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">As defined in the Halakhah.</i> were dancing in public with torches and reciting words of praise. And the Levites with lutes, with harps, and with cymbals and all kinds of innumerable musical instruments on the fifteen steps which descend from the courtyard of Israel to the women’s courtyard corresponding to the fifteen songs of ascent in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 74" href="/Psalms.74">Psalms<sup class="footnote-marker">74</a></sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 120-134" href="/Psalms.120-134"><i>Pss</i>. 120–134</a>.</i> on which the Levites were standing for song.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “The pious ones and people of good deeds.” Some of them were saying, blessed be my youth which did not shame my old age; these are the people of good deeds. But others were saying, blessed be my old age which atoned for my youth. Both of these were saying, blessed be who never sinned; and he who sinned may be forgiven. Hillel the Elder, if he saw them wantonly transgressing said to them, even though I am here, who is here? Does He need acclamations? Is it not written<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Daniel 7:10" href="/Daniel.7.10"><i>Dan</i>. 7:10</a>.</i>, <i>thousands upon thousands serve Him, and myriads of myriads stand before Him</i>? If he saw them acting correctly he was saying, if I am not here who is here? Even though there are many acclaims before Him, the acclaim of Israel is beloved more than anything else. What is the reason? <i>And the pleasantness of the songs of Israel</i><sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="II Samuel 23:1" href="/II_Samuel.23.1"><i>2S.</i> 23:1</a>.</i>; <i>enthroned on the praises of Israel</i><sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 22:4" href="/Psalms.22.4"><i>Ps.</i> 22:4</a>.</i>.
Ben Yehoṣadaq was priding himself for his vaulting. <sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 53a" href="/Sukkah.53a">Babli 53a</a>. The corrector’s change [in brackets] follows the Babli and should be deleted. In the Babli the torches are lit.</i> They said about Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel that he was dancing with eight golden torches and neither of them touched (the ground) [any other.] When he was bowing down to his shinbone he thrust his thumb into the ground, bowed down to his shinbone, and immediately stood up. <sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">Parallels are in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 1:5:12" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.1.5.12"><i>Berakhot</i>1:8 (Note 249</a>), <i>Gen</i>. <i>rabba</i>39(18). The text in <i>Berakhot editio princeps</i> is the corrector’s, quite different from the scribe’s which is consistent with the text here.</i> What is bowing down to one’s shinbone and what is kneeling? The great Rebbi Ḥiyya showed bowing down to his shinbone before Rebbi, [became lame] and was healed. Levi ben Sisi showed kneeling before Rebbi, became lame and was not healed.
<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">A slightly shortened version is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 2:4:4-6:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.2.4.4-6.2"><i>Sanhedrin</i>2:4, Notes 129–139</a>.
